Shahadat — Testimony

Testimony of People of Innovation: A Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Dispute on acceptance of testimony from innovators

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on testimony of ahl al-ahwa', explain the distinction between fisq in belief and practice, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the qiyas from the Companions.

Core Idea
Fisq in belief does not create suspicion of lying; religious scrupulousness deters false testimony.

This passage addresses a core disagreement: should the testimony of someone who holds heretical beliefs (ahl al-ahwa') be accepted? The Shafi'i school rejects it outright due to fisq. The Hanafi school, however, distinguishes between fisq in belief and fisq in practice, arguing that innovators are actually more careful about truthfulness. The passage also provides a unique qiyas from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani based on the actions of the Companions during the fitna.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling from the Hanafi school: testimony of ahl al-ahwa' is accepted, except for the Khattabiyyah sect.
The exception shows that Hanafis do not accept all innovators blindly.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The opponent is Imam al-Shafi'i, who rejects testimony of ahl al-ahwa' because their fisq in belief disqualifies them as witnesses.
Shafi'i relies on the general rule that a fasiq's testimony is rejected.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i proof is that fisq in belief is worse than fisq in action, and the Quran says 'no testimony for a fasiq'.
The key term is 'fisq' – transgression.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafis respond that fisq in belief does not make one prone to lying; these people are deeply religious and fear lying. They cite the example of the Companions who opposed Ali based on legitimate interpretation (ta'wil) – their testimony would still be accepted.
Imam Muhammad uses qiyas: if the Companions' testimony is accepted despite their opposition to Ali (which is innovation), then ahl al-ahwa' should also be accepted.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: In Hanafi courts, testimony from a Kharijite or Shiite is generally accepted, while in Shafi'i courts it is rejected. Only extremists like the Khattabiyyah are excepted in the Hanafi school.
This difference affects many cases in regions with diverse sects.

Source Text

وعند الشافعي لا تقبل شهادتهم لفسقهم. قلنا : لا يقع في الاعتقاد الباطل إلا ديانة، والكذب عند الجميع حرام. وأما الخطابية : فهم من غلاة الروافض يعتقدون الشهادة لكل من حلف عندهم. وقيل : يرون الشهادة لشيعتهم واجبة. وأصحاب الحديث. قوله : لفسقهم؛ إذ الفسق من حيث الاعتقاد أغلظ من الفسق من حيث التعاطي ولا شهادة للفاسق. قوله : قلنا...الخ؛ تقريره؛ أن ما ذهب إليه أهل الأهواء فسق من حيث الاعتقاد والفاسق إنما لا تقبل شهادته لتهمة الكذب، والفسق من حيث الاعتقاد لا يدل على ذلك؛ لأنه ما أوقعه في الهوى إلا تعمقه في الدين. ألا ترى أن فيهم من يعظم الذنب حتى يجعله كفراً، ومنهم من يجعل منزلته به بين الإيمان والكفر، فيكون هو أقوى اجتناباً عن الكذب، حذراً عن الخروج من الدين، وصار كمن يشرب المثلث ويأكل متروك التسمية عامداً معتقداً إباحة ذلك، بخلاف الفسق من حيث التعاطي فإن فيه تهمة الكذب ظاهراً. واستدل محمد له على قبول شهادة أهل الأهواء، فقال: أرأيت أن أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ساعدوا معاوية رضي الله عنه على مخالفة علي رضي الله عنه، ولو شهدوا بين يدي علي رضي الله عنه أكان ترد شهادتهم؟ ومخالفة علي رضي الله عنه بعد عثمان رضي الله عنه بدعة وهوى، وكيف الخروج عليه بالسيف لما كان عنده تأويل وتدين لم يمنع قبول شهادته، ذكره الزَّيْلَعِيُّ في «شرح الكنز».
According to al-Shafi'i, their testimony is not accepted because of their fisq. We say: They fall into false belief only out of religious devotion, and lying is forbidden by all. As for the Khattabiyyah, they are from the extremists of the Rawafid who believe in testifying for everyone who swears in their presence. It is said they consider testimony for their sect obligatory. The people of hadith. He said: 'Because of their fisq' – since fisq in belief is more severe than fisq in practice, and there is no testimony for a fasiq. He said: 'We say...' – its explanation: what the people of desires have adopted is fisq in terms of belief, and the fasiq's testimony is only rejected due to suspicion of lying, but fisq in belief does not indicate that; because nothing led them to desire except their deep engagement in religion. Do you not see that among them are those who magnify sin until they make it kufr, and among them are those who make its status between faith and disbelief, so they are stronger in abstaining from lying, fearing exit from religion, becoming like one who drinks wine and eats food over which the name of Allah was intentionally omitted, believing it permissible. Unlike fisq in practice, for there is an apparent suspicion of lying. Muhammad argued for acceptance of testimony of ahl al-ahwa', saying: 'Do you not see that the Companions of the Messenger of Allah supported Mu'awiya against Ali, and if they testified before Ali, would their testimony be rejected? Opposition to Ali after 'Uthman is innovation and desire, and how is it that going out against him with the sword, since he had interpretation and religiosity, did not prevent acceptance of his testimony?' Al-Zayla'i mentioned it in Sharh al-Kanz.
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding the acceptance of testimony from people of innovation (ahl al-ahwa'). The Shafi'i position is that such testimony is not accepted because their fisq (transgression due to corrupt belief) disqualifies them. The Hanafi response, attributed to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, argues that fisq in belief does not necessarily lead to false testimony; in fact, innovators are more cautious about lying because of their religious scrupulousness. The commentary further distinguishes between fisq in belief (which arises from deep religious commitment) and fisq in practice (which creates suspicion of falsehood). A specific group, the Khattabiyyah, is mentioned as an exception even among Hanafis? Actually the text says they are from the extreme Rawafid who testify for anyone who swears in their presence, implying their testimony is not accepted due to clear partiality. Imam Muhammad uses an analogy (qiyas) with the Companions who supported Mu'awiya against Ali; despite their opposition to Ali (which was based on interpretation), their testimony would still have been accepted. Thus, by analogy, testimony of ahl al-ahwa' should be accepted as long as they are not clearly dishonest. The relied-upon Hanafi position is that testimony of ahl al-ahwa' is accepted except for the Khattabiyyah and similar extremists.

Key Terms
أهل الأهواء (ahl al-ahwa'): People of desires — those who follow heretical innovations in religious beliefs.
الفسق (al-fisq): Transgression — acting against religious commands, here divided into fisq in belief (i'tiqadi) and fisq in practice (amali).
الخطابية (al-Khattabiyyah): An extremist sect from the Rawafid who believe in testifying for anyone who swears before them.
التأويل (al-ta'wil): Interpretation — a legitimate basis for differing opinions, used here to justify the Companions' actions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school accept testimony from ahl al-ahwa' despite their heretical beliefs?
A) Because their beliefs are not considered sinful.
B) Because their religious scrupulousness makes them less likely to lie.
C) Because they are not considered fasiq in the Hanafi school.
D) Because the Shafi'i school also accepts it.

Key Takeaway
Fisq in belief does not automatically disqualify a witness; Hanafis accept testimony from innovators unless they belong to sects known for false testimony.

Shahadat — Testimony

Dhimmi Testimony: General Rule, Proof, and Five Exceptions
Ruling with proof and five exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on dhimmi testimony, identify the Quranic proof, and list the five exceptions where such testimony is rejected.

Core Idea
A dhimmi's testimony is valid on other dhimmis and musta'min, but never on a Muslim.

The Hanafi school holds that a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject) can testify against another dhimmi or a musta'min (temporary resident), even if they are from different religious communities. This ruling is based on Quran 4:141, which prohibits giving disbelievers authority over believers. However, there are five specific cases where the testimony is not accepted to protect Muslim interests or prevent injustice. Let us work through the general rule, its proof, and each exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
State the rule: a dhimmi's testimony is accepted against another dhimmi (even of a different religion) and against a musta'min. The musta'min's testimony is not accepted against a dhimmi.
The text explicitly says 'wa al-dhimmi 'ala mithlihi' and 'wa 'ala al-musta'min'.
2
Locate the proof
Find the verse cited: Quran 4:141. Understand its implication: disbelievers cannot have a 'way' (sabil) over believers, so their testimony cannot be used against a Muslim.
The verse is a strong (qat'i) proof in transmission and meaning.
3
Understand the one-millah principle
Note that 'kufr is one millah' – for legal equality, all dhimmis are considered one community, so a Jew can testify against a Christian.
This is why 'even if they differ in religion' is included.
4
Enumerate the five exceptions
List each exception from the text: (1) two Christians testify that a Christian converted to Islam but he denies; (2) testimony against a deceased Muslim debtor with insufficient estate; (3) testimony about an item bought from a Muslim who denies the sale; (4) four Christians testify that a Christian committed zina with a Muslim woman (unless he claims coercion); (5) two disbelievers testify that a slave belongs to a disbeliever, but a Muslim judge had already ruled otherwise.
These exceptions protect Muslim rights or prevent harm to the dhimmi's own interests.
5
Apply the rule to a novel scenario
Given a scenario, determine whether dhimmi testimony is accepted based on the rule and exceptions.
Example: can a dhimmi testify against a Muslim? No – the verse prohibits it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذمي على مثله وإن خالفا ملة، وعلى المستأمن، والمستأمن على مثله إن كانا من دار واحدة. شهادة الذمي تقبل عندنا. قوله : والذمي؛ أن يقبل شهادة الذمي على مثله؛ أي على ذمي آخر عدلاً في دينهم. كذا في «الجوهرة»، وقيد بقوله : على مثله؛ لأنها لا تقبل على مسلم؛ لقوله : وَلَن يَجْعَلَ اللَّهُ لِلْكَفِرِينَ عَلَى الْمُؤْمِنِينَ سَبِيلًا. وتقبل شهادة الذمي على مثله إلا في خمس مسائل: الأولى: ما إذا شهد نصرانيان على نصراني أنه قد أسلم وهو يجحد، فلم تجز شهادتهما، وكذا لو شهد عليه رجل وامرأتان من المسلمين وترك على دينه، ولو شهد نصرانيان على نصرانية أنها أسلمت جاز، وأجبرها على الإسلام، ولا تقتل. الثانية: ما إذا شهدا على ميت وهو مديون مسلم، والتركة لا تفي. الثالثة: ما إذا شهدا عليه بعين اشتراها من مسلم، والمسلم ينكر البيع. الرابعة: ما إذا شهد أربعة على نصراني أنه زنى بمسلمة إلا إذا قال: استكرهها، فإنه يحد الرجل وحده. الخامسة: ما إذا ادعى مسلم عبداً في يد كافر، فشهد كافران أنه عبده، وقضى به فلان القاضي المسلم. ذكره في «الأشباه والنظائر». وإن؛ وصلية. خالفا ملة؛ كاليهود والنصارى؛ لأن الكفر كله ملة واحدة، وتقبل شهادة الذمي على المستأمن؛ لأن الذمي أعلى حالاً منه؛ لكونه من أهل دارنا، ولهذا يقتل المسلم بالذمي لا بالمستأمن، ولا تقبل شهادة المستأمن على الذمي؛ لقصور ولايته عليه؛
And the dhimmi (testifies) upon his like even if they differ in religion, and upon the musta'min, and the musta'min upon his like if they are from one abode. The testimony of the dhimmi is accepted with us. He said: 'And the dhimmi' — that the testimony of a dhimmi upon his like is accepted, meaning upon another dhimmi who is righteous in their religion. Thus in al-Jawhara, and it is restricted by his saying: upon his like, because it is not accepted upon a Muslim, due to His saying: 'And never will Allah grant the disbelievers a way over the believers.' And the testimony of a dhimmi upon his like is accepted except in five cases: First: if two Christians testify upon a Christian that he has converted to Islam while he denies, then their testimony is not valid, and similarly if a man and two women from the Muslims testify upon him and he remains on his religion; and if two Christians testify upon a Christian woman that she converted to Islam, it is valid, and he forces her to Islam, and she is not killed. Second: if they testify upon a deceased who is in debt to a Muslim and the estate is insufficient. Third: if they testify upon him concerning an item he bought from a Muslim and the Muslim denies the sale. Fourth: if four testify upon a Christian that he committed zina with a Muslim woman, unless he says he coerced her, then only the man is flogged. Fifth: if a Muslim claims a slave in the hand of a disbeliever, and two disbelievers testify that it is his slave, and such-and-such Muslim judge ruled accordingly. Mentioned in al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir. And 'even if' is conjunctive. 'They differ in religion' like Jews and Christians, because disbelief is all one religion. And the testimony of a dhimmi upon a musta'min is accepted because the dhimmi is of higher status, being from our abode; hence a Muslim is executed for killing a dhimmi but not for killing a musta'min, and the testimony of a musta'min upon a dhimmi is not accepted due to the deficiency of his authority over him.
Source pages p. 552

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling on the testimony of dhimmis (non-Muslim subjects of the Islamic state) against other dhimmis and musta'min (non-Muslims granted safe passage). The core ruling is that a dhimmi's testimony is accepted against another dhimmi, even if they belong to different religions (e.g., a Jew testifying against a Christian). It is also accepted against a musta'min, but not vice versa. The proof is the Quranic verse 4:141, which establishes that disbelievers cannot have authority over believers; thus their testimony cannot be used against a Muslim. The commentary lists five exceptional cases where a dhimmi's testimony against another dhimmi is not accepted, each involving a conflict of interest or potential harm to a Muslim. For example, if two Christians testify that another Christian has converted to Islam but he denies it, their testimony is rejected to protect his freedom of conscience. Similarly, if they testify against a deceased Muslim debtor with insufficient estate, or in cases where the testimony would disadvantage a Muslim in a transaction or criminal case. The general principle is that kufr (disbelief) is considered one millah (religious community) for legal purposes, so dhimmis of different religions can testify against each other. The acceptance of dhimmi testimony against musta'min is based on the dhimmi's higher status as a permanent resident, while the musta'min's lower status means his testimony against a dhimmi is rejected.

Key Terms
الذمي (al-dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state who pays jizyah and receives protection.
المستأمن (al-musta'min): A non-Muslim who is granted safe passage (aman) into Muslim territory temporarily.
الملة (al-millah): Religious community or creed.
الشهادة (al-shahadah): Testimony given in court as evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi law, the testimony of a dhimmi is accepted in all of the following situations EXCEPT:
A) Upon another dhimmi of a different religion.
B) Upon a musta'min from the same dar.
C) Upon a Muslim in a property dispute.
D) Upon a dhimmi who has converted to Islam but denies it.

Key Takeaway
A dhimmi's testimony is valid against other dhimmis and musta'min, but never against a Muslim, based on Quran 4:141; five specific exceptions protect Muslim rights.

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate
8 min
Testimony of Dhimmis and Musta'mins in Hanafi Law
Explains Hanafi acceptance of non-Muslim witness testimony with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on dhimmi and musta'min testimony, explain the evidence from Quran 4:141, and evaluate the Shafi'i/Maliki objection based on Quran 3:82 and 2:282.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school accepts dhimmi testimony against non-Muslims but never against Muslims, based on Quran 4:141.

This passage from the Hanafi commentary discusses a crucial area of procedural law: when can a non-Muslim's testimony be accepted in an Islamic court? The Hanafi school takes a nuanced position, accepting testimony from dhimmis (permanent non-Muslim residents) against other non-Muslims, and from musta'mins (temporary residents) under certain conditions. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its evidence, and the strong opposition from Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school rules regarding the testimony of dhimmis and musta'mins: accepted against their like under specific conditions, but never against a Muslim.
The key distinction is between testimony against Muslims vs. non-Muslims.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i reject dhimmi testimony entirely. Understand their reasoning: disbelievers are fasiq and not approved witnesses.
The opposition is based on a different interpretation of Quranic verses.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Analyze the Shafi'i/Maliki evidence: Quran 3:82 ('the disbelievers are the wicked') and Quran 2:282 ('from among those whom you approve as witnesses'), and the analogy with slaves.
The opponent views disbelief as inherent untrustworthiness.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine how Hanafis counter: Quran 4:141 prevents giving disbelievers authority over believers, but this only applies when testimony would affect Muslims. Among non-Muslims, the requirement of 'approval' is satisfied within their own community, and kufr is one millah (religion) for legal capacity.
The Hanafi response narrows the application of the opposing verses.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: in Hanafi courts, a dhimmi can testify against another non-Muslim; in Shafi'i/Maliki courts, such testimony is inadmissible, potentially affecting justice in multi-faith societies.
The difference has real-world implications for inter-communal litigation.

(٢) ينظر: «الأم»(٧ : ١٣٤). (۳) آل عمران : ۸۲. (٤) البقرة : ٢٨٢. (٥) النمل : ١٤ .
And the dhimmi [testimony] is accepted against his like even if they differ in religion, and against the musta'min, and the musta'min against his like if they are from one territory. The testimony of dhimmis is accepted according to us. ... And according to Malik and al-Shafi'i, it is not accepted. Then according to us, it is accepted only against the dhimmi and musta'min, even if they differ in religion like Christians and Zoroastrians, for all disbelief is one religion. And it is not accepted against a Muslim. And the testimony of a musta'min is accepted against a musta'min if they are from one territory, but if from two territories like Turks and Romans, it is not accepted. And it is also not accepted against a Muslim, nor against a dhimmi, because he is of lower status. ...
Source pages p. 552

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the acceptance of testimony from non-Muslims. It opens with the Hanafi ruling: the testimony of a dhimmi (a non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state) is accepted against another dhimmi, even if they belong to different non-Muslim religions (e.g., a Jew testifying against a Christian), and also against a musta'min (a non-Muslim temporary resident). The musta'min's testimony is accepted against another musta'min only if they are from the same territory (dar). The commentary then explains the reasoning: for dhimmis, because kufr (disbelief) is considered one millah (religion) in terms of legal capacity, and the dhimmi has higher status than a musta'min as he is a permanent resident. The text then mentions that Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i reject the testimony of dhimmis altogether, citing Quranic verses that call disbelievers 'fasiqun' (wicked) and require witnesses to be 'those whom you approve'. The Hanafi response is that these verses refer to giving disbelievers authority over believers, not to testimony among themselves. The Hanafi position is that dhimmi testimony is acceptable within their own community and against those of lower status, but not against Muslims, as per the Quranic principle that Allah will not give disbelievers a way over believers. Five exceptional cases are listed where dhimmi testimony is not accepted even against another dhimmi, such as when they testify that a dhimmi accepted Islam but he denies it. This detailed ruling demonstrates the Hanafi balance between upholding the dignity of Muslims and facilitating justice among non-Muslim residents.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject living permanently under the protection (dhimma) of the Islamic state, paying jizya.
مستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim who enters the Islamic territory temporarily with a pledge of security (aman), such as a trader or envoy.
ملة (millah): Religious community or creed; used here to denote different non-Muslim religions (e.g., Christianity, Judaism, Zoroastrianism).
فاسق (fasiq): A wicked person who commits major sins; the opposing school characterizes all disbelievers as fasiq to reject their testimony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, in which of the following cases is the testimony of a dhimmi accepted?
A) Against a Muslim on a debt
B) Against another dhimmi who converted to Islam but denies it
C) Against a musta'min from a different territory
D) Against a dhimmi of a different non-Muslim religion

Key Takeaway
Dhimmi testimony is accepted against other non-Muslims (with five exceptions) but never against Muslims, based on Quran 4:141.

Shahadat — Testimony

Acceptance of Non-Muslim Testimony: Hanafi Position and Dispute
Dispute on accepting non-Muslim testimony against each other

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on accepting non-Muslim testimony and evaluate the opposing Shafi'i/Maliki argument.

Core Idea
A dhimmi's testimony is accepted against another dhimmi but not against a Muslim — authority (wilāyah) over one's own kind is the key.

Testimony (shahādah) is a foundational evidence in Islamic courts. A critical question is whether a non-Muslim can serve as a witness. In this passage, the Hanafi school lays out its distinctive position: the testimony of a dhimmī or musta’min is accepted, but only against those of their own status, not against Muslims. This ruling is defended against the objections of Mālik, al-Shāfi‘ī, and Ibn Abī Layla, using hadith and rational analogy. Let's unpack the reasoning and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify exactly when non-Muslim testimony is accepted and when it is rejected according to the Hanafi school.
The text says: 'إنما تُقبل على الذمي والمستأمن' — it is only accepted against the dhimmī and musta’min.
2
Identify the opposing schools and their views
Recognize that Mālik and al-Shāfi‘ī reject non-Muslim testimony entirely, while Ibn Abī Layla accepts only if the creeds match.
The opponents' arguments are listed in the passage.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the scriptural and logical basis for the opposing view: non-Muslims are sinners and liars, and their testimony would harm Muslims.
They cite Quran 2:282 and 27:14.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Evaluate how the Hanafi school rebuts each argument: distinction between belief sin and conduct, trustworthiness in transactions (Quran 3:75), and the hadith from Ibn Mājah.
The difference from slaves is that dhimmīs have wilāyah over their own kind.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference in rulings between the schools for real-life cases.
In Hanafi fiqh, a dhimmī’s testimony is accepted in cases involving only non-Muslims; in Shāfi‘ī/Mālikī, it is never accepted.

Book of Testimony and Retraction from it, Chapter of Acceptance and Non-acceptance. (1) (2) [1] Inn Allāh. 553 According to Mālik and al-Shāfi‘ī, it is not accepted. Then according to us, it is only accepted against the dhimmi and the musta’min, even if they differ in creed like Christians and Magians, for disbelief is all one creed. And it is not accepted against a Muslim. Nor is it accepted against a Muslim, nor against a dhimmi because his status is lower than his. The testimony of a musta’min is accepted against his like if from one territory, so much so that if they are from two territories like Romans and Turks, it is not accepted because authority between them is cut off by difference of territories; hence inheritance does not run between them. (4) [1] His statement: Not accepted; testimony of a dhimmi against his like and against a harbi because he is a sinner; Allah said: 'Indeed, the disbelievers are the wicked' (3:82). And it is more severe than sin in practice, so it is more entitled that his testimony be rejected. And because Allah said: 'Among those you approve as witnesses' (2:282), and the disbeliever is not approved. And because the testimony of a slave is rejected because slavery is an effect of disbelief. So how can the testimony of one who has the reality of disbelief be accepted? And because accepting his testimony would oblige the judge to judge by his testimony, and it is not permissible to bind a Muslim by the testimony of a disbeliever. Hence his testimony against a Muslim is not accepted by consensus, so that a Muslim is not harmed by the testimony of a disbeliever. And because they do not avoid lying; indeed Allah informed about them that they deny the verses out of obstinacy while knowing they are Allah's truth: 'And they rejected them, though their souls were convinced of them, out of injustice and haughtiness' (27:14). That was a lie from them, and the liar's testimony is not accepted. So they are not qualified for them, like an apostate. And because they are not qualified for testimony against a Muslim, so likewise against a disbeliever like a slave, because one who is qualified for it does not differ between person and person. Ibn Abi Layla said: If their creeds agree, their testimony against each other is accepted; if they differ, it is not accepted, due to Allah's statement: 'No testimony of one creed against another creed except Muslims, for their testimony is accepted against all creeds.' (1) Our proof: What Ibn Mājah recorded in his Sunan from Jābir ibn ‘Abdullāh: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) permitted the testimony of the people of dhimmah against each other.' And sin with respect to belief does not prevent acceptance because he refrains from the prohibitions of his religion most strongly, and lying is prohibited in all religions. Allah described them with trustworthiness in His statement: 'Among the People of the Book is he who, if you entrust him with a great amount, he will return it to you' (3:75). Trustworthiness is pleasing, even if the disbeliever is not pleasing due to his disbelief. Since he is trustworthy in transactions, he is trustworthy in testimony, because it is from the fulfillment of trust. (2) The difference between him and a slave is that a slave is not qualified for authority over anyone like a child, and testimony is from the category of authority. The disbeliever is qualified for authority over his own kind, so he is also qualified for testimony over his own kind. The judge is not obliged to judge by the statement of a disbeliever; rather he is obliged by taqlīd when proof is established, and judgment is a trust with him; he must fulfill it, just as he must consider the absent. The lowliness of them and of Muslims, without looking at the reason for which the right is established for them, and their avoidance of lying is a witness. The obstinacy and denial that Allah related about them regarding those of that time, with their knowledge, does not require that those of our time from them are knowers of the truth; rather the apparent is that he believes disbelief to be true due to ignorance of it. If he knew, he would have accepted Islam. And in that time too there were those who did not know. Do you not see His statement: 'And among them are unlettered ones who do not know the Book except fantasies' (2:78), and His statement:
Source pages p. 553

Commentary
This passage is a detailed juristic dispute on the acceptance of testimony from non-Muslims (dhimmī and musta’min). The author presents the Hanafi position explicitly: the testimony of a dhimmī is accepted only against another dhimmī or a musta’min, not against a Muslim. The same applies to a musta’min’s testimony against his like if from the same territory. The reason is that disbelief is considered one creed (millah wāḥidah) so they can testify against each other, but authority (wilāyah) ends with different territories. The Mālikī and Shāfi‘ī schools are reported as rejecting such testimony entirely. Then Ibn Abī Layla’s view is given: acceptance only if they share the same creed. The Hanafi proof includes a hadith from Ibn Mājah where the Prophet permitted dhimmī testimony against each other, and rational arguments: their religious sin (kufr) does not necessarily entail dishonesty because they still avoid forbidden acts in their religion (including lying). They are described as trustworthy in transactions (Qur’an 3:75). The objection from slavery (slave testimony rejected) is answered: a slave has no authority over anyone, while a non-Muslim has authority over his own community. The text also addresses the objection that non-Muslims are liars (citing Qur’an 27:14) by explaining that obstinate denial was specific to those who knew the truth; contemporary non-Muslims may be ignorant, and if they knew they would convert. The author concludes that the judge does not enforce the testimony of a non-Muslim absolutely; rather, he accepts it based on legal reasoning (taqlīd) and must fulfill the trust of judging correctly.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject living under Muslim rule with a contract of protection.
مستأمن (musta’min): A non-Muslim from enemy territory given safe conduct (amān) for a limited period.
ولاية (wilāyah): Authority or legal capacity to act on behalf of another; here it refers to standing as a witness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Always, regardless of territory.
B) Only if they are from the same territory (dār).
C) Only if they share the same religion.
D) Never.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, non-Muslim testimony is accepted against their own kind based on wilāyah and trustworthiness, but never against Muslims.

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate

Testimony of Dhimmis: Hanafi vs. Ibn Abi Layla
Resolving dispute on dhimmi testimony with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school accepts testimony of dhimmis on each other regardless of denomination and evaluate Ibn Abi Layla's counter-argument.

Core Idea
A dhimmi's testimony on another dhimmi is accepted due to trustworthiness in transactions and hadith permission.

This lesson examines a classical dispute on whether the testimony of non-Muslim subjects (dhimmis) is accepted against each other when they belong to different denominations (e.g., Christian testifies against Jew). The Hanafi school, relying on a hadith and Quranic trustworthiness, accepts such testimony broadly. Ibn Abi Layla restricts it to those of the same religion. Understanding the proofs and reasoning is crucial for applying shahada rules in diverse societies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and opposing positions
State the core question: Is the testimony of a dhimmi on another dhimmi accepted only within the same religious denomination or across denominations? Note that Ibn Abi Layla conditions acceptance on identical religions, while the Hanafi school accepts generally.
2
Analyze Ibn Abi Layla's proof
Examine his claim that only Muslims can testify across religions, attributed to a divine statement. Consider its basis and why it is not a Quranic verse.
The text quotes 'لا شهادة لأهل ملة على أهل ملة أخرى إلا المسلمون' but this is not a standard Quranic verse; it may be a misquoted tradition or interpretation.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof: hadith and Quran
Review the hadith from Ibn Majah that the Prophet explicitly permitted testimony of dhimmis on each other. Also consider Quran 3:75 describing some People of the Book as trustworthy in keeping deposits.
The Hanafi argument links trustworthiness in transactions to trustworthiness in testimony, as testimony is a form of fulfilling a trust.
4
Understand the Hanafi response to objections
Address the objections that dhimmis are obstinate and may lie. The Hanafi response argues that they avoid lying in their own religion, and contemporary dhimmis may be ignorant of the truth rather than deliberately obstinate.
The text distinguishes between the obdurate of past times and the ignorant of the present.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Hanafi law, a Jew can testify against a Christian dhimmi, etc. Under Ibn Abi Layla, such interfaith testimony would be rejected. The Hanafi position is mufta bihi.
The ruling also distinguishes dhimmis from slaves and apostates who lack wilayah (guardianship) over others.

وقال ابن أبي ليلى له : إن اتفقت مللهم تقبل شهادة بعضهم على بعض ، وإن اختلفت لا تقبل ؛ لقوله الله : «لا شهادة لأهل ملة على أهل ملة أخرى إلا المسلمون، فإن شهادتهم مقبولة على أهل الملل كلهم. ولنا : ما أخرجه ابن ماجة في «سننه» : عن جابر بن عبد الله : «إنّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أجاز شهادة أهل الذمة بعضُهم على بعض ) ، والفسق من حيث الاعتقاد لا يمنع القبول ؛ لأنه يمتنع عن محظور دينه أشدُّ الامتناع، والكذب محظور في الأديان كلّها، ووصفهم الله بالأمانة في قوله : وَمِنْ أَهْلِ الْكِتَابِ مَنْ إِن تَأْمَنْهُ بِقِنطَارٍ يُؤَدِهِ إِلَيْكَ والأمانة مرضيّة، وإن لم يكن الكافرُ مرضيّاً لكفره ، ولَمَّا كان مؤتمناً في المعاملات كان مؤتمناً في الشهادة ؛ لأنها من أداء الأمانة. والفرق بينه وبين العبد أنَّ العبد ليس من أهل الولاية على أحد كالصبي ، والشهادة من باب الولاية، والكافر أهل للولاية على جنسه ، فيكون أهلاً للشهادة أيضاً على جنسه ، والقاضي لم يلزمه القضاء بقول الكافر. وإنما لزمه بالتقليد عند قيام الحجة والقضاء أمانة عنده، فيجب عليه أداؤه ، كما يلزمه النظر للغيب، والصغار منهم ومن المسلمين من غير أن ينظر بأي سبب وجب لهم الحق، وامتناعهم عن الكذب شاهد. والعناد والجحود الذي حكى الله عنهم في حق من كان في ذلك الزمان ، مع علمهم لا يوجب من أن تكون من في عصرنا منهم أن يكون عالماً بالحق، بل الظاهر أنه يعتقد الكفر حقاً لجهله به ، ولو علم لأسلم وقد كان في ذلك الزمان أيضاً من لا يعلم. ألا ترى إلى قوله له : ( وَمِنْهُمْ أُمِنُونَ لَا يَعْلَمُونَ الْكِتَابَ إِلَّا أَمَانِي ) ، وقوله له : إِنَّ فَرِيقًا مِّنْهُمْ لَيَكْتُمُونَ الْحَقِّ وَهُمْ يَعْلَمُونَ ) وقولهم : من كان أهلاً للشهادة لا يختلف بين شخص وشخص، منقوض بأن شهادة المسلم على عدوه لا تقبل فلا يتعدّر ردّ الشهادة بالنسبة إلى شخص للتهمة، فكذا هذا. والمرتد لا ولاية له على أحدٍ فلا تقبل شهادته على أحد، كالعبد والصبي، وملة الكفر واحدة فتقبل شهادة بعضهم على بعض ، واختلفت مللهم ؛ لأن بعضهم ليس في قهر بعض، فلا يؤدي إلى النقول عليه.
Ibn Abi Layla said to him: If their religions agree, their testimony is accepted on each other, and if they differ, it is not accepted because of God's saying: 'There is no testimony for the people of one religion against the people of another religion except Muslims, for their testimony is accepted against all religious communities.' And we have: what Ibn Majah extracted in his Sunan from Jabir ibn Abdullah that the Prophet (peace be upon him) permitted the testimony of the people of the dhimma on each other. And fisq (transgression) in terms of belief does not prevent acceptance because he strenuously refrains from the prohibitions of his religion, and lying is prohibited in all religions. And God described them with trustworthiness in His saying: 'And among the People of the Scripture is he who, if you entrust him with a great burden, will return it to you.' And trustworthiness is pleasing, even if the disbeliever is not pleasing due to his disbelief. And since he is trusted in transactions, he is trusted in testimony because it is a form of fulfilling trust. And the difference between him and a slave is that the slave is not qualified for guardianship over anyone, like a child, and testimony is a matter of guardianship, while the disbeliever is qualified for guardianship over his own kind, so he is also qualified for testimony over his own kind. And the judge is not obligated to judge by the statement of the disbeliever; rather he is obligated by taqlid when the proof is established, and judgment is a trust with him, so he must fulfill it, as he is required to consider the absent. And their abstention from lying is a witness. And the obstinacy and denial that God narrated about them concerning those of that time, despite their knowledge, does not require that those of our time among them be knowledgeable of the truth; rather the apparent is that he believes disbelief to be true due to his ignorance of it, and if he knew he would have embraced Islam. And in that time also there were those who did not know. Do you not see His saying: 'And among them are unlettered who do not know the Book except wishful thinking,' and His saying: 'Indeed, a party among them conceal the truth while they know.' And their saying: 'Whoever is qualified for testimony does not differ between persons' is refuted by the fact that the testimony of a Muslim against his enemy is not accepted, so the rejection of testimony with respect to a person due to suspicion is not precluded, and likewise this. And the apostate has no guardianship over anyone, so his testimony is not accepted against anyone, like a slave and a child. And the religion of disbelief is one, so testimony of some of them on others is accepted, even if their specific denominations differ, because some are not under the dominance of others, so it does not lead to being swayed.
Source pages p. 554

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the acceptance of testimony given by dhimmis (non-Muslim subjects) against each other. The text presents the view of Ibn Abi Layla, who conditions acceptance on the unity of their religious affiliation (e.g., Christians against Christians, Jews against Jews). If their religions differ, he rejects it. He cites a statement attributed to God that only Muslims' testimony is accepted across all religions. The Hanafi school argues against this, presenting a hadith from Ibn Majah that the Prophet (peace be upon him) allowed the testimony of dhimmis on each other. They reason that their transgression (fisq) in belief does not prevent acceptance because they strictly avoid the prohibitions of their own religion, and lying is prohibited in all religions. Moreover, God describes them as trustworthy in commercial dealings (Quran 3:75), so they are trusted in testimony as well, since testimony is a form of fulfilling a trust. The text then distinguishes dhimmis from slaves: a slave has no guardianship (wilayah) over others, like a child, whereas a disbeliever has guardianship over his own kind, making him qualified to testify over them. The judge's obligation to accept such testimony arises from taqlid (following established rulings) and because judgment is a trust. The text also responds to the objection that some dhimmis in the past were obstinate despite knowing the truth by noting that contemporary dhimmis may be ignorant and would accept Islam if they knew. Finally, it deals with the apostate: an apostate has no guardianship, so his testimony is rejected, similar to a slave or child. The unity of disbelief as a single religion means testimony among different non-Muslim groups is accepted as long as none is under the dominance of another, preventing bias.

Key Terms
أهل الذمة (ahl al-dhimmah): Non-Muslim subjects living under Muslim rule with a pact of protection
الولاية (al-wilayah): Legal capacity to act on behalf of others; here, the capacity to testify due to having authority over one's own group

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in the text, what is the basis for accepting the testimony of dhimmis on each other?
A) The Quranic verse that commands accepting testimony from all people
B) A hadith from Ibn Majah that the Prophet permitted it, and their trustworthiness in transactions
C) Consensus of the Companions
D) Analogy with Muslim testimony

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts testimony of dhimmis on each other across denominations due to the Prophet's permission and their trustworthiness in their own religious practices.

Testimony of an Enemy Due to Religion: The Hanafi Acceptance of Religious Enmity
Explaining acceptance of religious enemy's testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting testimony from an enemy due to religion and explain the rational proof for its acceptance.

Core Idea
Religious enmity strengthens a witness's credibility and does not disqualify testimony.

In this lesson, we examine a nuanced Hanafi ruling: the testimony of a person who is your enemy due to religious reasons is accepted. This may seem counterintuitive, but the jurists explain that such enmity actually indicates strong faith and upright character. We will explore the definition of an enemy, the proof for acceptance, and the crucial distinction between religious and worldly enmity.

Guided Path
1
Define the enemy
The text defines an enemy (al-aduww) as one who rejoices at your sorrow and grieves at your joy, or is recognized by custom. Identify what kind of enmity is discussed here.
The definition helps separate religious from worldly enmity.
2
Identify the ruling
The testimony of an enemy due to religion is accepted (tuqbal). This is the central ruling. Note that the text explicitly says 'and an enemy... etc.' meaning acceptance.
3
Examine the proof
The text gives a rational proof: religious enmity indicates the witness's strength of religion and uprightness ('adalah). Additionally, it draws an analogy with the accepted testimony of a Muslim against a disbeliever despite religious enmity.
The proof is logical, not textual.
4
Distinguish from worldly enmity
Worldly enmity, such as that of a slandered person testifying against the slanderer, may disqualify the witness because it can lead to transgression. However, quoting al-Qunya, the text clarifies that worldly enmity alone does not prevent acceptance unless it causes fisq, benefit, or harm.
This distinction is crucial for application.
5
Apply the relied-upon view
The correct and relied-upon position is that worldly enmity does not automatically disqualify; it must lead to transgression. This is based on al-Qunya and is the mufta bihi view.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وعدو...الخ ; أي وتقبل شهادة عدوّ بسبب الدين، والعدو : من يفرح لحزنك ويحزن لفرحك ، وقيل : يعرف بالعرف ، ووجه قبول شهادته أنَّ العداوة بسبب الدين تدلُّ على قوَّة دينه وعدالته ؛ وهذا لأنَّ المعاداة قد تكون واجبة، بأن رأى فيه منكراً شرعياً ولم ينته بنهيه. وقد قبلوا شهادة المسلم على الكافر مع ما بينهما من العداوة الدينية بخلاف العداوة الدنيوية، كشهادة المقذوف على القاذف، والمجروح على الجارح وغيرهما، فإنّها حرام فمن ارتكبها لا يؤمن من النقول عليه. وقال في «القُنْية ) : العداوة بسبب الدنيا لا تمنع ما لم يفسق بسببها، أو يجلب منفعة، أو يدفع بها عن نفسه مضرّة، وهو الصحيح، وعليه الاعتماد. انتهى. وهاهنا مباحث نفيسة ذكرها الحموي وغيره من الأعلام تركناها خوفاً عن تطويل الكلام.
His saying: 'And an enemy... etc.' means: The testimony of an enemy due to religion is accepted. The enemy is one who rejoices at your sorrow and grieves at your joy, and it is said he is known by custom. The reason for accepting his testimony is that enmity due to religion indicates the strength of his religion and his uprightness. This is because enmity may be obligatory, such as when he sees a religious wrongdoing in someone and the person does not desist despite his prohibition. And they have accepted the testimony of a Muslim against a disbeliever despite the religious enmity between them, unlike worldly enmity, such as the testimony of a accused person (of slander) against the slanderer, or a wounded person against the assailant, etc., for that is prohibited, and whoever commits it is not safe from being swayed. And he said in al-Qunya: Worldly enmity does not prevent acceptance as long as one does not commit transgression due to it, or bring benefit, or repel harm from oneself, and that is the correct view, and it is relied upon. End. And there are valuable discussions here mentioned by al-Hamawi and other notable scholars, which we have omitted for fear of prolonging the discussion.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on accepting the testimony of an enemy, specifically enmity due to religious reasons. The commentator defines an enemy as someone who delights in your misfortune and is saddened by your joy, or is recognized by common custom. The acceptance of such testimony is justified because religious enmity actually demonstrates the witness's strong faith and upright character ('adalah). In fact, religious enmity can be obligatory if the witness observes a religious wrong and the wrongdoer persists. The text draws an analogy: Muslim testimony against a disbeliever is accepted despite religious enmity. However, worldly enmity (e.g., due to personal disputes) may disqualify a witness. Examples include the testimony of a person who was slandered against the slanderer, or a wounded person against his attacker — such testimony is prohibited because the witness is motivated by personal vendetta and may lie. The text then cites al-Qunya, which clarifies that worldly enmity per se does not prevent acceptance unless it leads the witness to commit transgression (fisq), seek personal benefit, or use it to repel harm from himself. This is the correct and relied-upon view. The commentator notes that more detailed discussions exist but are omitted for brevity.

Key Terms
العدو (al-aduww): An enemy; one who rejoices at your misfortune and grieves at your joy
العداوة بسبب الدين (al-`adaawah bi-sabab al-deen): Enmity arising from religious differences or due to religious reasons
القُنْية (al-Qunyah): A Hanafi legal work by al-Ansari (d. 1198 CE), often cited in later fatwa collections

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on accepting the testimony of a person who is an enemy due to religious reasons?
A) It is rejected in all cases
B) It is accepted because religious enmity indicates strong faith
C) It is accepted only if the enemy repents
D) It is rejected if the enmity is intense

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts the testimony of a religious enemy because such enmity reflects strong faith and upright character, while worldly enmity may disqualify only if it leads to transgression or personal gain.

Hanafi Definition of 'Adalah for Witnesses
Defining 'adalah as condition for testimony acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'adalah according to the Hanafi school and state its minimal requirements for accepting testimony.

Core Idea
‘Adalah is the predominance of religion and intellect over desire — the minimum suffices to avoid losing rights.

This passage from ‘Umdat al-Ri‘āyah unpacks a critical concept in Islamic testimony law: what makes a witness upright (ʻadl)? The author clarifies that ʻadālah is not an unattainable perfection but a practical threshold — Islam, sound reason, and a prevailing inclination towards good over base desires. Understanding this minimal standard is essential for applying the rules of testimony in Islamic courts without causing injustice through excessive stringency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the concept being defined
The passage defines ‘adālah (uprightness) as a condition for obligating acceptance of testimony. Note that it is a condition for wujūb (obligation), not for the act of testifying itself.
ʻAdālah is required for the testimony to be binding on the judge.
2
Break down the definition into components
Extract the three components mentioned: (1) Islam, (2) sound intellect, and (3) the ability to oppose one’s own desires. The latter is further specified as the predominance of religion and reason over passion.
These components are the minimum; any less would fail the condition.
3
Understand the rationale for the minimal standard
The text states there is no fixed limit for perfection; requiring full perfection would cause loss of rights. The minimum suffices to prevent injustice.
This is a practical concession to ensure the testimony system functions.
4
Apply the definition to a hypothetical scenario
Consider a witness who occasionally commits minor sins but is generally religious and rational. According to this definition, he may still be ‘adl because the religious and rational aspect dominates.
Minor sins do not automatically negate ʻadālah if they do not overpower one's faith.
5
Distinguish from stricter definitions
The Hanafi position contrasts with schools that require explicit avoidance of all major sins or a spotless record. Note that the text accepts the minimal predominance, not perfection.
This ikhtilāf is a practical mercy in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ومن اجتنب...الخ ، قد أشار هاهنا إلى العدالة، فإنّها شرط وجوب قبول الشهادة، وهي الاستقامة وهي بالإسلام واعتدال العقل، ومعارضة هـوى يصله ويصده، وليس لكمالها حد يدرك مداه، ويكتفي بقبولها أدناه كيلا تضيع الحقوق ، وهو رجحان جهة الدين والعقل على الهوى والشهوة.
His saying: 'And whoever avoids the major sins... etc.' has indicated here 'adalah (uprightness), for it is a condition for the obligation of accepting testimony. It is uprightness, and it consists of Islam, soundness of intellect, and opposing desires that could lead one astray. There is no limit for its perfection that can be reached; the minimum degree suffices to prevent the loss of rights. It is the predominance of the aspect of religion and intellect over desire and passion.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage defines 'adalah (uprightness) as a condition for the obligatory acceptance of testimony. The author indicates that 'adalah comprises three elements: Islam, sound intellect, and the ability to oppose one's own desires that may mislead. Importantly, 'adalah does not have a fixed measure of perfection; rather, the minimum acceptable level is sufficient to avoid the loss of people's rights. If we required absolute perfection, many rights would be lost due to lack of witnesses. Thus, 'adalah is defined as the predominance of one's religious and rational inclination over base desires and passions. This is a practical definition used by the Hanafi school to ensure the system of testimony remains functional.

Key Terms
العدالة (al-`adaalah): Uprightness consisting of Islam, sound intellect, and predominance of religion and reason over desire
الاستقامة (al-istiqamah): Uprightness; synonym for 'adalah in this context

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the minimal standard for 'adalah in a witness?
A) Absolute perfection in character
B) Islam, sound intellect, and predominance of religion over desire
C) Knowledge of all legal rulings
D) Being a Companion of the Prophet

Key Takeaway
Hanafi 'adalah is a practical threshold: predominance of faith and reason over desire, not sinlessness.

Testimony of a Religious Enemy: Hanafi Ruling
Explains the acceptance of testimony of an enemy due to religion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between religious enmity and worldly enmity and state the Hanafi ruling on accepting testimony from each category.

Core Idea
Religious enmity proves uprightness; worldly enmity raises suspicion.

This passage clarifies a common misunderstanding about testimony: enmity does not automatically disqualify a witness. The Hanafi school distinguishes between enmity arising from religious commitment, which actually indicates reliability, and worldly enmity, which may indicate bias. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating witnesses in Islamic courts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What act is being ruled upon? (testimony of a religious enemy). What is the ruling? (accepted).
The ruling is 'sahih' (valid).
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
What reasoning is given? (religious enmity indicates strength of religion and uprightness; enmity may be obligatory when seeing a religious wrong).
The proof is aqli (rational) based on the concept of 'adala.
3
Contrast with worldly enmity
How does the passage distinguish? (worldly enmity involves personal harm/benefit and is forbidden, so testimony may be rejected if it leads to fisq).
Examples: slandered person vs. slanderer, injured vs. injurer.
4
Examine the later authority (al-Qunyah)
What does al-Qunyah add? (worldly enmity does not prevent unless it leads to fisq or personal benefit; this is the relied-upon view).
Al-Qunyah's view is stated as 'al-sahih' and 'alayhi al-i'timad.
5
Apply the ruling
How would you evaluate a witness who has a worldly feud with the defendant? (Not automatically rejected; check if the enmity leads to sinful behavior or personal interest).
This shows the nuance in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَعَدُوٌّ بِسَبَبِ الدِّينِ. وَوَجْهُ قَبُولِ شَهَادَتِهِ أَنَّ الْعَدَاوَةَ بِسَبَبِ الدِّينِ تَدُلُّ عَلَى قُوَّةِ دِينِهِ وَعَدَالَتِهِ؛ وَذَلِكَ لِأَنَّ الْمُعَادَاةَ قَدْ تَكُونُ وَاجِبَةً، بِأَنْ رَأَى فِيهِ مُنْكَراً شَرْعِيًّا وَلَمْ يَنْتَهِ بِنَهْيِهِ. وَقَدْ قَبِلُوا شَهَادَةَ الْمُسْلِمِ عَلَى الْكَافِرِ مَعَ مَا بَيْنَهُمَا مِنَ الْعَدَاوَةِ الدِّينِيَّةِ، بِخِلَافِ الْعَدَاوَةِ الدُّنْيَوِيَّةِ، كَشَهَادَةِ الْمَقْذُوفِ عَلَى الْقَاذِفِ، وَالْمَجْرُوْحِ عَلَى الْجَارِحِ وَغَيْرِهِمَا، فَإِنَّهَا حَرَامٌ فَمَنِ ارْتَكَبَهَا لَا يُؤْمَنُ مِنَ النَّقُولِ عَلَيْهِ. وَقَالَ فِي «الْقُنْيَةِ» : الْعَدَاوَةُ بِسَبَبِ الدُّنْيَا لَا تَمْنَعُ مَا لَمْ يَفْسُقْ بِسَبَبِهَا، أَوْ يَجْلِبَ مَنْفَعَةً، أَوْ يَدْفَعَ بِهَا عَنْ نَفْسِهِ مَضَرَّةً، وَهُوَ الصَّحِيحُ، وَعَلَيْهِ الِاعْتِمَادُ. انْتَهَى.
And an enemy due to religion (his testimony is accepted). The reason for accepting his testimony is that enmity due to religion indicates the strength of his religion and his uprightness; that is because enmity may be obligatory, if he sees in him a religious wrong and he does not desist by his prohibition. And they have accepted the testimony of a Muslim against a disbeliever despite the religious enmity between them, contrary to worldly enmity, like the testimony of a slandered person against the slanderer, and the injured against the injurer and others, for it is forbidden; so whoever commits it is not safe from suspicion upon him. And it is said in al-Qunyah: 'Worldly enmity does not prevent [acceptance] as long as he does not become sinful because of it, or derive benefit, or ward off harm from himself by it; and this is the correct view, and it is relied upon.' End.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on accepting the testimony of a person who is an enemy due to religious reasons. The author explains that such enmity actually indicates the witness's strong faith and uprightness, since religious enmity may be obligatory when one witnesses a religious violation. This is supported by the established practice of accepting a Muslim's testimony against a disbeliever despite religious enmity. However, worldly enmity, such as that between a slandered person and slanderer, is different because it involves personal benefit or harm, and engaging in such enmity may lead to suspicion of partiality. The quote from al-Qunyah clarifies that worldly enmity does not automatically disqualify a witness unless it leads to sinful behavior or personal gain.

Key Terms
العدو (al-‘aduw): An enemy; someone who delights in your sorrow and grieves at your joy, or defined by custom.
العداوة (al-‘adāwah): Enmity; here divided into religious (due to faith) and worldly (due to personal matters).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on the testimony of a person who is an enemy due to religious reasons?
A) It is rejected because enmity raises suspicion.
B) It is accepted because religious enmity indicates strong faith and uprightness.
C) It is accepted only if the enemy is also a relative.
D) It is rejected because enmity nullifies ‘adala.

Key Takeaway
Religious enmity is a sign of uprightness and does not disqualify a witness; worldly enmity only disqualifies if it leads to sin or personal benefit.

Definition of 'Adala and Major Sins for Witnesses
Defining the condition of 'adala for witnesses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'adala for witnesses and identify the conditions that make a witness 'adl.

Core Idea
A witness must avoid major sins, not persist in minor sins, and manifest muru'ah—this is the Hanafi standard for 'adala.

In this passage, the commentator defines the key quality of a witness: 'adala (uprightness). The text presents the conditions laid out by Hanafi jurists, including the avoidance of major sins and the importance of honorable conduct. It then discusses the disagreement over the enumeration of major sins and gives the preferred view of Imam Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being defined? What is the practical consequence of a witness being 'adl? The act is testimony (shahada), and the ruling is that if the witness is 'adl, his testimony is accepted.
2
List the conditions of 'adala
Extract from the text the specific requirements for a witness to be considered 'adl: avoiding major sins, not persisting in minor sins, predominance of reason (ghalaba al-sawab), and manifest muru'ah (honorable conduct).
The first three are explicit; muru'ah is added by Abu Yusuf.
3
Understand the disagreement on major sins
The text notes that scholars differed on the definition of major sins. Some listed seven specific sins, but the list is incomplete here. Recognize that the Hanafi school does not limit major sins to a fixed list; rather, it is any sin for which the Lawgiver has prescribed a specific punishment or warned.
The preferred view is that of Abu Yusuf, who defines 'adala broadly.
4
Analyze Abu Yusuf's definition
Abu Yusuf adds the condition of manifest muru'ah and explicitly states that persistence on minor sins is not allowed. Note that lack of muru'ah vitiates 'adala.
This definition is relied upon in the Hanafi school.
5
Relate to practical witness qualification
Consider a real scenario: a witness avoids major sins but has a habit of lying about trivial matters. According to the definition, if lying is a major sin or he persists in it, his testimony is rejected. If it is a minor sin without persistence, and he has muru'ah, he may still be 'adl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنِ اجْتَنَبَ الْكَبَائِرَ وَلَمْ يُصِرَّ عَلَى الصَّغَائِرِ وَغَلَبَ صَوَابُهُ. اخْتَلَفُوا فِي تَفْسِيرِ الْكَبَائِرِ، قِيلَ: هِيَ سَبْعٌ: الْإِشْرَاكُ بِاللَّهِ تَعَالَى، وَالْفِرَارُ مِنَ الزَّحْفِ، وَعُقُوقُ الْوَالِدَيْنِ، وَقَتْلُ النَّفْسِ بِغَيْرِ حَقٍّ، [وَ... ؟] . وَأَحْسَنُ مَا قِيلَ فِيهِ مَا رُوِيَ عَنْ أَبِي يُوسُفَ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ: إِنَّ الْعَدْلَ أَنْ يَكُونَ مُجْتَنِباً عَنِ الْكَبَائِرِ غَيْرَ مُصِرٍّ عَلَى الصَّغَائِرِ، وَأَنْ تَكُونَ مُرُوءَتُهُ ظَاهِرَةً، فَعَدَمُهَا مُفَوِّتَةٌ لَهَا. وَفِي «الْخَانِيَّةِ»: الْفَاسِقُ إِذَا تَابَ لَا تُقْبَلُ شَهَادَتُهُ مَا لَمْ يَمْضِ عَلَيْهِ زَمَانٌ تَظْهَرُ فِيهِ التَّوْبَةُ، ثُمَّ بَعْضُهُمْ قَدَّرَهُ بِسِتَّةِ أَشْهُرٍ، وَبَعْضُهُمْ قَدَّرَهُ بِسَنَةٍ، وَالصَّحِيحُ أَنَّ ذَلِكَ مُفَوَّضٌ إِلَى رَأْيِ الْقَاضِي وَالْمُعَدِّلِ. انْتَهَى. وَفِي «الْخُلَاصَةِ»: وَلَوْ كَانَ عَدْلًا فَشَهِدَ بِزُورٍ ثُمَّ تَابَ فَشَهِدَ، تُقْبَلُ مِنْ غَيْرِ مُدَّةٍ.
And whoever avoids major sins and does not persist in minor sins, and his reason predominates. They differed concerning the interpretation of major sins. It was said: they are seven: associating partners with Allah, fleeing from battle, disobedience to parents, killing a soul without right, [and ?]. And the best that has been said about it is what is reported from Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him: 'The upright (adl) is one who avoids major sins, does not persist in minor sins, and whose honorable conduct (muru’ah) is manifest; so lack of it vitiates it.' And in al-Khaniya: 'The sinner, if he repents, his testimony is not accepted unless a period passes in which repentance appears; then some estimated it as six months, and some estimated it as a year, and the correct view is that this is left to the opinion of the judge and the one who assesses uprightness.' End. And in al-Khulasa: 'If one was upright and then gave false testimony, then repented and testified, it is accepted without any period.' End.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage defines the condition of ‘adala (uprightness/justice) required for a witness’s testimony to be accepted. It specifies three elements: avoidance of major sins (kabā’ir), not persisting in minor sins (ṣaghā’ir), and predominance of reason (ghalaba al-ṣawāb). The text then discusses disagreements regarding the definition of major sins. Some say they are seven specific sins: shirk, fleeing from battle, disobeying parents, and murder without right (the remaining three are not listed here). The soundest opinion is attributed to Imam Abu Yusuf, who says that ‘adala consists of avoiding major sins, not persisting in minor ones, and having manifest chivalry (murū’ah). The passage also addresses repentance of a sinner: according to al-Khaniya, a former sinner’s testimony is not accepted until a period of time passes to confirm the repentance, but the duration is left to the judge’s discretion. In contrast, al-Khulasa states that if an originally upright person committed false testimony and then repented, his testimony is accepted immediately without any waiting period.

Key Terms
العدالة (al-‘adālah): Uprightness; the quality of a witness that makes his testimony acceptable, defined as avoiding major sins, not persisting in minor sins, and manifest muru’ah.
الكبيرة (al-kabīrah): Major sin; here defined as the seven listed, and generally as any sin for which the Lawgiver has prescribed a specific punishment or warned specifically.
الصغيرة (al-ṣaghīrah): Minor sin; persistence on it makes it major.
المروءة (al-murū’ah): Honorable conduct; a quality of social respectability and decency that is manifest in a person's behavior.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did Abu Yusuf add to the definition of ‘adala?
A) The requirement to give charity
B) The requirement of manifest muru’ah
C) The requirement to pray five times
D) The requirement to be a scholar

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of 'adala for a witness centers on avoiding major sins, not persisting in minor ones, and having manifest muru'ah.

Repentance and Restoration of Testimony
Ruling on repentance and restoration of witness 'adala.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the acceptance of testimony after repentance, distinguishing between the case of a former sinner and a previously upright person who gave false testimony.

Core Idea
Repentance restores 'adala, but a former sinner must show a period of consistency, while an upright person's false testimony requires immediate re-acceptance after repentance.

This passage continues the discussion on witness qualifications by addressing repentance. It cites two different scenarios from Hanafi sources: a fasiq (sinner) who repents must wait for a period determined by the judge, while a righteous person who committed false testimony and then repents is accepted immediately.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two distinct scenarios: (1) a fasiq (sinner) who repents, and (2) a person who was 'adl but later gave false testimony and then repented. They are treated differently.
2
Extract the ruling for the first case
For a fasiq who repents, his testimony is not accepted until a period of time passes during which his repentance appears. Some scholars estimated six months, others a year, but the correct view is that the duration is left to the judge's discretion.
This is from al-Khaniya.
3
Extract the ruling for the second case
For a person who was originally 'adl, then gave false testimony, then repented, his testimony is accepted immediately without any waiting period.
This is from al-Khulasa.
4
Analyze the rationale
Why the difference? The fasiq may have a history of sinfulness, so his repentance needs to be verified over time. The 'adl person's character is presumed good; his false testimony was an aberration, and repentance can restore his previous status swiftly.
The judge has discretion to assess sincerity.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider a witness who was a known thief (major sin) but has repented and lived righteously for a year. According to this ruling, the judge may require a waiting period (perhaps one year) before accepting his testimony. In contrast, a respected person who once lied in court but repented can testify immediately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

التوبة (al-tawba): Repentance; return from sin to obedience, which can restore the 'adala of a witness.

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate

Conditions of Witness Uprightness (ʿAdālah)
Explaining the conditions for witness ʿadālah and responding to a scholarly critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for witness uprightness and explain the commentary's critique regarding redundancy of the two stated conditions.

Core Idea
A witness must avoid major sins and not persist in minor sins; persistence makes a minor sin major.

The Hanafi school requires that a witness be ʿadl (upright). The base text mentions two conditions: not persisting in minor sins and having good deeds prevail. However, the commentator al-Birjandī critically examines these, arguing that persistence turns a minor sin into a major one, making the first condition redundant, and that avoiding major sins naturally leads to prevailing goodness. This lesson unpacks the actual requirements: avoiding major sins, non-persistence in minor sins, and possessing apparent murūʾah (chivalry).

Guided Path
1
Identify the explicit conditions in the base text
The matn states: 'and he did not persist in minor sins, and his correctness prevailed.' These seem to be two separate conditions.
2
Analyze the redundancy critique
The commentary argues that persistence in a minor sin makes it a major sin, so the first condition is already covered by avoiding major sins. Also, if one avoids major sins, good deeds likely prevail, so the second condition is also redundant.
This critique is based on the Hanafi principle that al-iṣrār ʿalā al-ṣaghīrah yajʿaluhā kabīrah.
3
Recognize the actual condition from Abu Yusuf
The best definition of ʿadālah according to Abu Yusuf is: avoidance of major sins, non-persistence in minor sins, and having apparent murūʾah.
Murūʾah includes public decorum and avoiding disreputable behavior.
4
Examine the treatment of repentance
A fasiq who repents must demonstrate repentance over time (judge's discretion). However, an ʿadl person who commits perjury and then repents is accepted immediately.
The time period is not fixed; some said six months, some a year, but the correct view leaves it to the judge.
5
Conclude the operative ruling
The conditions of ʿadālah are not strict prerequisites but descriptions of acceptable witnesses. The operative position is that a witness must avoid major sins, not persist in minor sins, and have murūʾah.
This balances the need for credible testimony with practical reality.

And he did not persist in minor sins, and his correctness prevailed. They differed in interpreting the major sins: It is said they are seven: 1. Associating partners with Allah, 2. Fleeing from the battle, 3. Disobedience to parents, 4. Killing a soul without right. And the best of what is accepted is what was narrated from Abu Yusuf - may Allah have mercy on him - that uprightness is to avoid major sins, not persist in minor sins, and that his chivalry (murūʾah) be apparent; lacking it forfeits it. In al-Khāniyyah: The sinner if he repents, his testimony is not accepted until a time passes showing his repentance. Then some estimated it as six months, some as a year, and the correct view is that it is left to the opinion of the judge and the modifier. End. In al-Khulāṣah: If one was upright then testified falsely then repented and testified, it is accepted without a time period. End. Likewise in al-Manaḥ. [1] His saying: 'and did not persist...' etc.; apparently there is no need for his saying 'and did not persist in minor sins' because persistence in a minor sin is a major sin. After considering that, there is no need for his saying 'and his correctness prevailed' because whoever avoids major sins and does not persist in minor sins, apparently his good deeds outweigh his evil deeds. Then if the meaning of major sins includes all, even backbiting, then finding a witness like that is rare; rather they explicitly said that the testimony of a wine drinker if not addicted is accepted. If the meaning is some of them, then it is vague speech with no benefit. It is possible that the first meaning is intended, and his purpose is to state that whoever is of this description, his testimony is accepted, not that it is a condition for acceptance. Al-Birjandī mentioned this in Sharh al-Niqāyah; reflect on it and do not rush. [2] His saying: 'al-kabāʾir'; plural of kabīrah, they are the seven great ones. It is said: what the Lawgiver has specifically threatened, and it is said: what has a prescribed legal punishment, and it is said: the relativity is additional. Likewise in al-Mirqāh. If you want further detail, refer to al-Zawājir, for it contains what cures the sick. Page 557 Book of testimony and retraction, Chapter of acceptance and non-acceptance. 5. Accusing a believer of falsehood. 6. Adultery. 7. Drinking wine. Some added: consuming orphan's wealth unjustly, and consuming usury. And it has been narrated in the hadith: 'Avoid the seven destructive sins: associating partners with Allah, sorcery, killing the soul which Allah has forbidden except by right, consuming usury, consuming orphan's wealth, turning away on the day of battle, and accusing chaste believing women who are heedless.' And he said: 'The major sins are: associating partners with Allah, disobedience to parents...'
Source pages p. 556

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the conditions for accepting testimony, specifically the requirement of 'adālah (uprightness). The author first quotes the base text: 'and he did not persist in minor sins, and his correctness prevailed.' He notes that scholars differed on the definition of major sins (kabāʾir), listing seven: shirk, flight from battle, disobedience to parents, and murder. The best definition, according to Abu Yusuf, is that uprightness means avoiding all major sins, not persisting in minor sins, and having apparent murūʾah (chivalry/decorum). The passage then discusses repentance of a sinner: the Khāniyyah states that repentance must be demonstrated over time (six months or a year), but the correct view is that it is up to the judge. However, if an upright person then commits perjury and repents, his testimony is accepted immediately (al-Khulāṣah). The commentator then critically examines the base text: he argues that 'not persisting in minor sins' is redundant because persistence in a minor sin is itself a major sin, and 'prevailing correctness' is also redundant because avoiding major sins and not persisting in minor sins implies good deeds outweigh bad. He also notes that defining kabāʾir as all major sins (including backbiting) makes a qualified witness rare; yet they accept testimony of a non-addicted wine drinker. Thus the intended meaning is likely that such a description makes testimony acceptable, not that it is a strict condition. The passage then lists more major sins (5-7) and adds others (orphan's wealth, usury) based on the hadith of the seven destructive sins narrated by Bukhari and Muslim, and another hadith listing shirk, disobedience to parents, murder, and false oath (or false testimony). The commentary briefly explains terms like tawallī (retreat) and muḥṣanāt (chaste women). The overall teaching is that the standard for witness uprightness is high but practically flexible, and major sins are those with specific textual threats.

Key Terms
العدالة (al-ʿadālah): Uprightness: avoiding major sins, not persisting in minor sins, and having apparent murūʾah, with good deeds prevailing.
الكبيرة (al-kabīrah): Major sin: an act for which a specific threat or prescribed punishment is mentioned in the texts, or any of the seven (or more) enumerated sins.
الإصرار (al-iṣrār): Persistence in a minor sin, which elevates it to a major sin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is the effect of persistence (iṣrār) on a minor sin?
A) It makes the minor sin a major sin.
B) It has no effect on the sin's category.
C) It only affects the witness's murūʾah.
D) It requires the witness to repent for six months.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi condition for witness uprightness is avoiding major sins and not persisting in minor sins; persistence turns a minor sin into a major one, so effectively the witness must avoid all major sins.

Major Sins (Kabāʾir) in Hanafi Fiqh: Definitions and Proofs
Defining and enumerating major sins with hadith evidence and scholarly definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the seven destructive sins from the hadith, state the Hanafi definitions of a major sin, and evaluate the different criteria offered by scholars.

Core Idea
Major sins are those explicitly threatened with punishment or for which a fixed legal penalty is prescribed; persistence in minor sins also makes them major.

The concept of major sins (kabāʾir) is crucial for witness uprightness and for personal accountability. This lesson presents the enumeration of major sins from the hadith of the 'seven destructive sins' and other narrations, along with the scholarly definitions that determine which sins are considered major.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith proof
The text quotes the hadith: 'Avoid the seven destructive sins' from Bukhari and Muslim, and another hadith listing shirk, disobedience to parents, murder, false oath.
These hadiths are the primary textual evidence for the enumeration of major sins.
2
List the sins mentioned
From the first hadith: shirk, sorcery, murder, usury, orphan's wealth, fleeing battle, slandering chaste women. From the second: shirk, disobedience to parents, murder, false oath (or false testimony).
The lists overlap; the seven destructive sins are the most commonly cited.
3
Examine the definitions of kabīrah
Several definitions are given: (1) what has a specific threat, (2) what has a prescribed punishment (hadd), (3) relative (comparative to other sins).
The Hanafi school generally favors the first definition (textual threat) supplemented by the principle of iṣrār.
4
Note the Hanafi-specific rule
Persistence in a minor sin makes it a major sin (iṣrār). This is a distinctive Hanafi principle that expands the scope of major sins.
This is derived from the commentary's argument that persistence turns a minor sin into a major one.
5
Apply to witness ʿadālah
A witness must avoid all major sins. If he commits any major sin, he is fasiq and his testimony is rejected. Persistence in a minor sin also makes him fasiq.
Thus the list of major sins directly impacts who qualifies as a witness.

And he did not persist in minor sins, and his correctness prevailed. They differed in interpreting the major sins: It is said they are seven: 1. Associating partners with Allah, 2. Fleeing from the battle, 3. Disobedience to parents, 4. Killing a soul without right. And the best of what is accepted is what was narrated from Abu Yusuf - may Allah have mercy on him - that uprightness is to avoid major sins, not persist in minor sins, and that his chivalry (murūʾah) be apparent; lacking it forfeits it. In al-Khāniyyah: The sinner if he repents, his testimony is not accepted until a time passes showing his repentance. Then some estimated it as six months, some as a year, and the correct view is that it is left to the opinion of the judge and the modifier. End. In al-Khulāṣah: If one was upright then testified falsely then repented and testified, it is accepted without a time period. End. Likewise in al-Manaḥ. Definition of Major Sins with Hadith Evidence
Explanation of major sins definition and hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of a major sin (kabirah) and distinguish it from the non-exhaustive lists found in hadith.

Core Idea
A major sin is defined by textual punishment or social repugnance, not by a fixed list.

In this passage, the commentator explains the criteria for identifying major sins (kaba'ir), a concept crucial for witness testimony (shahada) in Hanafi law. While hadith mention specific destructive sins, the Hanafi school emphasizes a broader definition based on text and custom. Understanding this definition helps in distinguishing major from minor sins and assessing the integrity of witnesses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith evidence cited
The text quotes the hadith of seven destructive sins and another listing major sins. Note that these are examples, not exhaustive.
The hadith are from Bukhari and Muslim.
2
Extract the definitional criteria
The text states that a major sin is anything termed 'fahishah' (abomination) or for which a definitive punishment is established in this world or the Hereafter.
Examples include sodomy and marrying a father’s ex-wife.
3
Consider Imam al-Halwani’s supplementary criterion
Anything socially repugnant that violates divine sanctity is also a major sin.
This adds a customary dimension.
4
Note the principle that persistence on a minor sin transforms it into a major sin
Even a minor sin, if done persistently with intention, becomes major.
This is an important ethical principle.
5
Apply the definition to a test case
Determine whether a given sin (e.g., false testimony) qualifies as major based on the criteria.
False testimony is explicitly mentioned in a hadith narration as a major sin.
