The book of testimony and retraction, chapter of acceptance and non-acceptance. 5. Accusing a believer (of adultery). 6. Adultery. 7. Drinking wine. Some added: consuming orphan's wealth unjustly, and consuming usury. And it has been reported in the hadith: 'Avoid the seven destructive sins: associating partners with Allah, magic, killing a soul which Allah has forbidden except by right, consuming usury, consuming orphan's wealth, turning back on the day of battle, and slandering chaste, believing, heedless women.' He said: 'And it has been reported... etc.' Bukhari and Muslim narrated from Abu Hurairah that the Messenger of Allah said: 'Avoid the seven destructive sins.' They said: 'O Messenger of Allah, what are they?' He said: 'Associating partners with Allah, magic, killing a soul which Allah has forbidden except by right, consuming usury, consuming orphan's wealth, turning back on the day of battle, and slandering chaste, heedless believing women.' [2] He said: 'And turning back on the day of battle'; turning back (al-tawalli) means retreat, and al-zahf is the group: i.e., those who march towards the enemy: i.e., walk towards them. [3] He said: 'al-muhsanat' - with fath on the sad and sometimes kasr - meaning those whom Allah has protected and preserved, or those who have protected their private parts from adultery, and al-ghafilat is a metaphor for the innocent, for the innocent is heedless of what they are slandered with. [4] He said: 'And he said... etc.' Bukhari narrated from 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar and Anas that the Messenger of Allah said: 'The major sins are: associating partners with Allah, disobedience to parents, killing a soul, and the false oath (al-yamin al-ghamus).' And in a narration of Anas: 'and false testimony' instead of 'false oath'. So the correct view is that these hadith are not for enumeration, so a major sin is everything that is called an abomination (fahishah) like sodomy and marrying a father's wife, or for which a punishment is established by a definitive text in this world or the Hereafter. And Imam al-Halwani said: Whatever is considered repugnant among the Muslims and involves violation of the sanctity of Allah and the religion, then it is a major sin. Then after avoiding all major sins, it is necessary not to persist in minor sins, for persistence in a minor sin makes it a major sin.
Source pages p. 557

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on the chapter of testimony (shahada), discussing the definition of major sins (kaba'ir) as relevant to the integrity of witnesses. The author first lists some major sins from hadith: accusing a chaste believer of adultery, adultery, drinking wine, and others added by some scholars such as consuming orphan's wealth and usury. He then cites the famous hadith of the seven destructive sins (al-mubiqat) from Bukhari and Muslim, which enumerates seven specific major sins. Another hadith mentions five major sins including false oath (al-yamin al-ghamus). The author clarifies that these hadith are not meant to be exhaustive; rather, they provide examples. The correct definition is that a major sin is any sin that is termed 'fahishah' (abomination) such as sodomy or marrying one's father's wife, or any sin for which a definitive text establishes a capital punishment (hadd) in this world or a promised punishment in the Hereafter. Imam al-Halwani adds a practical criterion: anything considered repugnant among the Muslim community and involves violation of Allah's sanctity and the religion is a major sin. Finally, the author states the important principle that persistence (israr) on a minor sin elevates it to the level of a major sin, so avoiding major sins requires also avoiding persistence in minor sins.

Key Terms
الكبيرة (al-kabīrah): A major sin; defined as any sin that is called an abomination (fahishah) or for which a decisive textual punishment exists in this world or the Hereafter, or anything considered repugnant among Muslims involving violation of Allah's sanctity.
الصغيرة (al-ṣaghīrah): A minor sin; a sin that is not classified as major according to the definition. Persistence on a minor sin elevates it to a major sin.
الإصرار (al-iṣrār): Persistence or insistence on a sin; committing a minor sin repeatedly with the intention to continue, which turns it into a major sin.
الموبقات (al-mūbiqāt): Destructive sins; sins that destroy one's Hereafter if committed without repentance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi commentary in this passage, which of the following best defines a major sin (kabirah)?
A) Any sin that is among the seven listed in the hadith of the destructive sins.
B) Any sin that is termed an abomination (fahishah) or for which a definitive punishment is established in this world or the Hereafter.
C) Any sin that involves disobedience to parents only.
D) Any sin that is committed with knowledge and without repentance.

Key Takeaway
Major sins are not limited to a fixed list; they are defined by their textual severity and societal impact, with persistence on minor sins elevating them to major.

The Definition of a Just Witness in Hanafi Law
Definition of a just witness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of a just witness ('adl) including the additional condition of avoiding base acts (khisal khasisah) and explain why minor sins do not disqualify.

Core Idea
A just witness avoids major sins, has predominant good deeds, and avoids base acts that indicate lack of muru'ah.

In Islamic court testimony, the uprightness of a witness is critical. The Hanafi school defines 'adl not only by religious piety but also by social decency. This lesson unpacks that definition from the commentary, exploring what qualifies a witness and how base acts like eating in the street can bar testimony even if not sinful per se.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Locate the base definition from the matn: a just witness avoids major sins and his good deeds predominate. This is the foundation of 'adalah in testimony.
Minor sins do not automatically disqualify, as long as good deeds outweigh bad.
2
Extract the additional condition
The commentator adds a condition: the witness must avoid base acts (khisal khasisah) that indicate lack of muru'ah, such as eating in the street or urinating in public.
This condition is separate from sin; it relates to social dignity.
3
Analyze the objection and response
Consider the objection that requiring total avoidance of all sins would close the door to testimony. The response distinguishes major sins (which are disqualifying) from minor sins (which are overlooked if good deeds dominate).
The ruling upholds the principle of reviving rights by not being overly strict.
4
Distinguish base acts from non-voluntary impediments
Understand that base acts are voluntary and indicate lack of muru'ah, while non-voluntary impediments like blindness are not disqualifying by themselves but may prevent testimony due to other reasons.
The commentator clarifies that the additional condition does not contradict the definition; it is a separate impediment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : وغَلَبَ صوابُهُ : أي حسناته أغلب من سيئاته ، فإن الإلمام بالصغيرة لا يُسقط العدالة.
فقوله : ومن اجتنب الكبائر إلى قولِهِ : وغَلَبَ صوابه تفسير العدل. أقول : ولا بُدَّ فيه من قيد آخر، وهو أن يجتنب الأفعال الخسيسة الدالة على الدناءة : أي عدم المروءة : كالأكل في الطريق، والبول على الطريق.
[١] قوله : أي حسناته...الخ ؛ وإلى هذا أشار صاحب الهداية»، وقال: هذا هو الصحيح في حد العدالة المعتبرة، إذ لا بُدَّ له من توقي الكبائر كلّها، وبعد ذلك يعتبر الغالب كما ذكر، فأما الإلمام بمعصية لا تنقدح بها العدالة المشروطة فلا يرد به الشهادة المشروعة ؛ لأنَّ في اعتبار اجتنابه الكلّ سد بابه ، وهو مفتوح ؛ إحياء للحقوق. انتهى.

His statement: and his righteousness predominates: meaning his good deeds are more than his evil deeds, for committing a minor sin does not remove uprightness. So his statement: and whoever avoids major sins up to his statement: and his righteousness predominates is the interpretation of uprightness. I say: there must be an additional condition in it, which is that he avoids base acts indicating lowness, i.e., lack of chivalry: such as eating in the road and urinating on the road. [1] His statement: meaning his good deeds... etc.; the author of al-Hidayah referred to this, saying: this is the correct view in defining the uprightness that is considered, for he must avoid all major sins, and after that what is predominant is considered as mentioned. As for committing a sin that does not impair the required uprightness, his testimony is not rejected because requiring him to avoid all sins would close the door, while it is open to revive rights. End. [2] His statement: I say: there must be... etc.; and it is answered that the upright person is as mentioned in the text, and the testimony of an upright person may be rejected due to an impediment such as blindness. Then eating and urinating in the road are also impediments, so what he mentioned does not invalidate it, that such as urinating in the road is prohibited due to its baseness, and blindness is not voluntary, so it does not impair uprightness. Understand.
Source pages p. 558

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of a just witness ('adl) required for testimony. The commentator explains that 'adl means a person avoids major sins (kaba'ir) and his good deeds outweigh his bad deeds. Committing a minor sin does not automatically disqualify a witness. The commentator adds an additional condition: the witness must also avoid base acts that indicate lack of muru'ah (chivalry), such as eating in public streets or urinating in public places. The commentator then addresses potential objections: first, that requiring total avoidance of all sins would make it impossible to find witnesses; the response is that only major sins are absolutely disqualifying, and minor sins are overlooked as long as good deeds predominate. Second, the additional condition might seem to add to the definition, but the answer is that the text gives the general definition, and such acts are separate impediments (mawani') that can prevent acceptance of testimony even from an otherwise upright person, similar to blindness. The Hanafi position is that these base acts are not sins but indicate low character, and thus they affect the acceptability of testimony.

Key Terms
العدل (al-'adl): A person who avoids major sins, has predominant good deeds, and avoids base acts (khisal khasisah) that indicate lack of muru'ah.
المروءة (al-muru'ah): Chivalry or decency; avoiding acts that are considered degrading in societal custom, such as eating in the street.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) He must be wealthy.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a just witness must not only avoid major sins and have predominant good deeds, but also avoid base acts that undermine social decency (muru'ah).

Testimony of the Uncircumcised: Conditions and Contempt
Ruling on testimony of an uncircumcised adult male

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting testimony from an uncircumcised man and apply the condition of istikhfaf to determine when testimony is rejected.

Core Idea
Lack of circumcision does not disqualify a witness unless it stems from contempt for religion.

In this passage, the commentator expands on the ruling regarding the testimony of an uncircumcised adult male (al-aqlaf). The Hanafi school holds that circumcision is sunnah, so its mere absence does not impair a person's uprightness ('adalah). However, the motive behind leaving it matters. The passage details the views of Qadi Khan and al-Zayla'i, both of whom accept the testimony if there is a valid excuse, and reject it if left out of contempt. Understanding this nuance is crucial for distinguishing between a permissible omission and one that indicates moral deficiency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The text states that the testimony of an uncircumcised man is accepted because circumcision is sunnah, not fard. The base principle is that 'adalah is not impaired by omitting a sunnah.
2
Note the condition from Qadi Khan
Qadi Khan restricts acceptance to cases where the man left circumcision due to fear for himself (e.g., fear of pain or health risk). If there is no excuse, testimony is rejected.
3
Note the condition from al-Zayla'i
Al-Zayla'i expands excuses to include old age and fear of death, and explicitly states that leaving it without excuse out of contempt (istikhfaf) for religion leads to rejection.
4
Extract the key principle
The decisive factor is contempt for religion (istikhfaf). If the omission stems from disregard for Islamic practice, the witness loses 'adalah and testimony is rejected.
5
Compare with other schools
The Shafi'i school considers circumcision obligatory, so they reject testimony outright if uncircumcised. The Hanafi school uniquely distinguishes based on intent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأقلف إلا إذا ترك الاختتان استخفافاً بالدين
[٣] قوله : والأقلف ؛ وفي بعض النسخ والأغلف بالغين المعجمة، أي ولا تقبلُ شهادة كبير لا يختن ؛ لأنَّ العدالة لا تختل بتركه الختان ؛ لكونه سنة عندنا، وقيده قاضي خان بأن يتركه لخوف على نفسه، أما إذا تركه بغير عذر لم تقبل.
وقال الزيلعي (۳) : هذا إذا تركه لعذر به من كبر أو خوف هلاك، وإن تركه من غير عذر استخفافاً بالدين لا تقبل شهادته ؛ لأنه لم يبق عدلاً مع الاستخفاف بالدين
And the uncircumcised, unless he left circumcision out of contempt for religion. [3] His statement: and the uncircumcised; and in some copies it is 'al-aghla' with a dotted ghayn, meaning the testimony of an adult male who does not circumcise is not accepted because uprightness is not impaired by leaving circumcision since it is sunnah in our school. Qadi Khan restricted it to the case where he leaves it due to fear for himself. As for if he leaves it without excuse, his testimony is not accepted. And al-Zayla'i said: this is if he leaves it due to an excuse such as old age or fear of death; if he leaves it without excuse out of contempt for religion, his testimony is not accepted because he is no longer upright due to contempt for religion.
Source pages p. 559

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on accepting testimony from an uncircumcised adult male (al-aqlaf). In the Hanafi school, circumcision is considered sunnah, not obligatory (fard). Therefore, the lack of circumcision does not inherently impair a person's uprightness ('adalah) required for testimony. However, the ruling is conditioned: if a man leaves circumcision due to a valid excuse such as fear (e.g., health risk) or old age, his testimony is accepted. But if he leaves it without any excuse, particularly out of contempt (istikhfaf) for the religion, then his testimony is not accepted because such contempt demonstrates a lack of uprightness. The commentator notes a variant reading 'aghla' (with ghayn), but the meaning is similar. The passage relies on the principle that contempt for religious practices disqualifies one from being a just witness.

Key Terms
الأقلف (al-aqlaf): An adult male who has not been circumcised.
الاستخفاف (al-istikhfaf): Contempt or disdain for religious practices.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Only if he is under the age of puberty.

Key Takeaway
An uncircumcised witness's testimony is accepted unless the omission stems from contempt for religion, which destroys 'adalah.

Testimony of Eunuch, Bastard, and Laborers
Hanafi ruling with proofs and inter-madhhab dispute on shahadat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the testimony of a eunuch, child of adultery, and laborer, identify the proof for each, and explain the refutation of the Maliki objection regarding the child of adultery's testimony in adultery cases.

Core Idea
Testimony is accepted based on the witness's own uprightness, not physical condition or parentage.

This lesson examines three categories of witnesses whose testimony might be questioned due to personal circumstances: eunuchs, children of adultery, and laborers. The Hanafi school affirms their testimony provided they are upright ('adl). We will explore the proofs, including the precedent of Caliph Umar accepting a eunuch's testimony, and address the Maliki objection that a child of adultery is biased in adultery cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
List the three types of witnesses discussed: the eunuch (khasiyy), the child of adultery (walad al-zina), and laborers ('ummal). Note the general ruling that their testimony is accepted if they are upright.
The category of laborers includes those working for the sultan or hiring themselves out.
2
Locate the proof for the eunuch
Find the dalil: the hadith where Umar (ra) accepted the testimony of Alqama the eunuch. Understand the analogy (qiyas) that a eunuch is like an amputee—missing a limb unjustly does not imply sin.
This proof is a Sunnah ahad (zanni al-thubut).
3
Analyze the Maliki dispute on child of adultery
Contrast the Hanafi position (accepted if upright) with Malik's objection (rejected in adultery cases due to bias). Examine the Hanafi rebuttal: an upright person does not wish others to be like himself.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is that in Hanafi fiqh the testimony is accepted in all cases, while Maliki rejects it specifically for adultery.
4
Note the condition for laborers
Identify that laborers' testimony is accepted unless they are helpers of oppression ('awn 'ala al-zulm). Work itself is not fisq; nobility of work is supported by the hadith about earning from one's hand.
There is an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Yusuf accepted testimony of a prestigious laborer even if sinful, but the mufta bihi requires 'adala.

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi ruling on the acceptance of testimony from three categories: eunuchs, children of adultery, and laborers. The matn states that their testimony is accepted, and the commentary expands with proofs and refutes Malik's objection regarding the child of adultery testifying on adultery, arguing that uprightness of the witness is key, not his parents' sin. For laborers, work itself is not sin; if they are helpers of tyranny, their testimony is rejected, otherwise accepted. There is an interesting interjection on circumcision timing (khitan) which is unrelated to testimony but included in the text. The commentary cites the practice of Umar accepting testimony of a eunuch, and uses qiyas to compare the eunuch to an amputee. For the child of adultery, the principle is that one's parentage does not affect one's own 'adala. The laborers are defined as those who work for the sultan or hire themselves out; Abu Yusuf allowed testimony of a prestigious sinner due to lowered likelihood of lying. The ruling generally accepts testimony of these individuals if they are upright.

Key Terms
خصي (khasiyy): A eunuch; a male whose testicles have been removed, considered like an amputee for purposes of testimony acceptance.
ولد الزنا (walad al-zina): Child born out of wedlock; considered upright if he himself is righteous, despite parents' sin.
عمال ('ummal): Laborers; specifically those who work for the sultan or hire themselves out; their testimony is accepted unless they are helpers of oppression.

Testimony for Relatives and of the Blind
Hanafi ruling on testimony for relatives and blind persons with commentary expanding on conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on accepting testimony for relatives and from a blind person, and identify the condition of separate property as the basis for accepting testimony for a brother versus rejecting it for a father.

Core Idea
Testimony for a brother is accepted because his property is separate; testimony for a father is rejected due to shared interests — a blind person cannot testify because he cannot see.

The Hanafi school has detailed rules about who can testify and for whom, balancing the need for evidence against the risk of bias. This lesson examines a key passage from the commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'aya that clarifies when testimony for relatives is accepted and why the blind are generally disqualified from testifying.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is giving testimony (shahadah) in court. The base ruling states that testimony is accepted for one's brother, uncle, and relatives by rada (milk kinship) or musahara (marriage affinity), but not from a blind person.
2
Analyze the condition for accepting testimony for relatives
The commentary explains that testimony for a brother is accepted because properties and benefits are separate (tabayun al-amlak), eliminating suspicion of partiality. For a father, benefits are connected, so testimony is rejected when the father is alive.
Contrast with testimony for a deceased father, which is accepted.
3
Apply the condition of sight
The blind person's testimony is rejected because he cannot visually identify the subject of testimony. This is a general rule, though some exceptions exist for matters that can be perceived by other senses.
4
Consider the conduct of witnesses
The commentary adds that if a witness has actively argued for the plaintiff in litigation before testifying, his testimony is rejected. However, if he merely quarreled with the defendant but remains upright, the testimony may be accepted according to some authorities.
This shows that the ruling is not absolute but depends on circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲] قوله : ولأخيه وعمه ، ومَن حُرِّمَ رضاعاً أو مصاهرة. لا من أعمى. [١]قوله : تقبل شهادته ؛ لأنه لمهابته لا يتجاسر أحدٌ على استئجاره على الشهادة الكاذبة، ولوجاهته لا يقدم على الكذب حفظاً للمروءة، وفي «الكافي»: هذا كان في زمانهم ؛ لأنَّ الغالب عليهم الصلاح، وفي زماننا : لا تقبل شهادة العمال بغلبة ظلمهم. انتهى. [٢] قوله : ولأخيه...الخ ؛ أي وتقبل الشهادة لهم ؛ لانعدام التهمة، لأنَّ الأملاك ومنافعها متباينة، ولا بسوطة لبعضهم في مال بعض وفي «المحيط البرهاني»: وهذا الجواب لا يشكل فيما إذا شهد لأبيه والأب ،ميّت، إنما يشكل فيما إذا شهد لأبيه والأب حي. والجواب : إنَّ شهادة الإنسان لأبيه إنّما لا تقبل ؛ لأنَّ منافع الأملاك بين الأب والابن متصلة، وكانت الشهادة للأب شهادة لنفسه من وجه فلم تقبل، وأما شهادته لأخيه فليست لنفسه أصلا ؛ لتباين الأملاك. انتهى. وفي «القنية : امتدت الخصومة سنين، ومع المدَّعي أخ وابن عم يخاصمان له مع المدعى عليه، ثم شهدا له في هذه الخصومة بعد هذه الخصومات لا تقبل شهادتهما. انتهى. (۲) وقال في «البحر ) بعد نقل هذا القول المذكور : قال في «خزانة الفتاوى»: إذا تخاصم الشهود والمدعى عليه تقبلُ إن كانوا عدولاً. انتهى. قال : وينبغي حمله على ما إذا لم يساعد المدَّعي في الخصومة، أو لم يكثر ذلك منهم توفيقاً. انتهى. ونقل في «السراجية» ما يوافقُ «خزانة الفتاوى» حيث قال : إذا تشاجرا ثم شهد أحدهما على الآخر تقبل إن كان عدلا. انتهى.
[2] His statement: 'to his brother and his uncle, and those prohibited by rada or musahara. Not from a blind person.' [1] His statement: 'his testimony is accepted' because due to his prestige no one dares to hire him for false testimony, and due to his dignity he does not advance to lying to preserve his manliness. In al-Kafi: this was in their time because righteousness was prevalent; in our time, the testimony of laborers is not accepted due to the prevalence of their oppression. End. [2] His statement: 'to his brother...etc.' meaning testimony is accepted for them because there is no suspicion of partiality, since properties and their benefits are separate, and no one has control over the property of another. In al-Muhit al-Burhani: this answer is not problematic when he testifies for his father when the father is dead; it is only problematic when he testifies for his father while the father is alive. The answer: the testimony of a person for his father is not accepted because the benefits of properties between father and son are connected, and the testimony for the father is like testimony for himself from one aspect, so it is not accepted. As for his testimony for his brother, it is not for himself at all, because properties are separate. End. In al-Qunyah: the litigation extended for years, and with the plaintiff were a brother and a cousin who argued for him against the defendant, then they testified for him in this litigation after these arguments; their testimony is not accepted. End. (2) And he said in al-Bahr after citing this statement: in Khazanat al-Fatawa: if witnesses argue with the defendant, their testimony is accepted if they are upright. End. He said: it should be interpreted as when they do not assist the plaintiff in the argument, or they do not do so frequently, as a reconciliation. End. In al-Sirajiyyah, it agrees with Khazanat al-Fatawa, saying: if they quarrel and then one testifies against the other, it is accepted if he is upright. End.
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the acceptance of testimony for relatives (brother, uncle, those related by rada and musahara) and the rejection of testimony from a blind person. The commentary explains that testimony for a brother is accepted because properties are separate, eliminating suspicion of partiality unlike testimony for a parent where interests are connected. The blind person's testimony is rejected because he cannot see the subject, but the text only mentions 'not from a blind person' without elaboration. The commentary also discusses nuances: a prestigious laborer's testimony may be accepted in their time due to prevalent righteousness, but in later times it is rejected due to oppression. Additionally, if a witness argues with the defendant before testifying, his testimony is accepted if he remains upright; the books al-Qunyah and Khazanat al-Fatawa harmonize these rulings.

Key Terms
شهادة (shahadah): Testimony in court; accepted from upright witnesses with certain qualifications.
أعمى (a'ma): Blind person; his testimony is not accepted due to inability to identify the subject visually.
رضاع (rada'): Milk kinship; creates prohibition of marriage and here expands the category of relatives for whom testimony is accepted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is testimony for a brother accepted while testimony for a father is not, according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because a brother is never a beneficiary.
B) Because properties and benefits between brothers are separate, eliminating presumption of bias.
C) Because a father's testimony is always rejected due to potential lying.
D) Because a brother is considered more trustworthy than a father.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, testimony for a brother is accepted because of separate property, but testimony for a living father is rejected due to shared interests, and a blind person's testimony is generally rejected due to inability to see.

Testimony of a Respectable Worker Even if Fasiq
Shahada exception for a respected worker despite fisq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the exceptional ruling that a fasiq worker's testimony is accepted if he possesses respectability and mura'ah, and explain the rational proof and its temporal limitation.

Core Idea
A fasiq's testimony is rejected unless he is a respected worker with mura'ah — but only when trust prevails.

In Hanafi law, the general rule is that a fasiq (sinner) lacks ‘adalah and his testimony is rejected. However, this passage presents a specific exception for a certain class of people: workers (‘āmil) who are respected (wajīh) and possess mura'ah (integrity). The rationale, reported from Imam Abu Yusuf, is that such a person's social standing and personal honour prevent him from committing perjury. Yet, as the commentary from al-Kafi notes, this ruling was contingent on the moral conditions of early Islamic society; in later corrupt times, it was no longer applied.

Guided Path
1
State the exceptional ruling
Identify the precise statement: a worker who is respected and possesses mura'ah, even if fasiq, his testimony is accepted.
2
Identify the proof
Locate the dalil: a narration from Imam Abu Yusuf which provides the basis, supported by rational reasoning (istihsan).
3
Analyse the rationale
Understand why: due to his awe (haybah) no one dares to hire him for false testimony, and his mura'ah prevents him from lying.
4
Recognize the temporal condition
Note from al-Kafi that this ruling applied only when righteousness prevailed; in later times of widespread injustice, it is no longer accepted.
This shows how rulings can change with social conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : العامل إذا كان وجيهاً ذا مروءة لا يجازف في كلامِه تُقْبَلُ شهادته ، وإن كان فاسقاً، فقد رُوي عن أبي يوسف له : إن الفاسق إذا كان وَجيهاً لوجاهته لا يقدم على الكذب تقبل شهادته. [١]قوله : تقبل شهادته ؛ لأنه لمهابته لا يتجاسر أحدٌ على استئجاره على الشهادة الكاذبة، ولوجاهته لا يقدم على الكذب حفظاً للمروءة، وفي «الكافي»: هذا كان في زمانهم ؛ لأنَّ الغالب عليهم الصلاح، وفي زماننا : لا تقبل شهادة العمال بغلبة ظلمهم. انتهى.
And it is said: The worker, if he is respected, possessing mura'ah (integrity), not careless in his speech, his testimony is accepted, even if he is a fasiq (sinner). It has been narrated from Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him): Indeed, the fasiq, if he is respected due to his respectability, he does not proceed to lying, so his testimony is accepted. [1] He said: His testimony is accepted because due to his awe, no one dares to hire him for false testimony, and due to his respectability, he does not proceed to lying to preserve his mura'ah. And in al-Kafi: This was in their time because the majority were righteous, but in our time, the testimony of workers is not accepted due to the prevalence of their injustice. End.
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario: the testimony of a worker (عامل) who is described as وجيها (respected) and ذا مروءة (possessing integrity). Such a person, even if he is a فاسق (sinner) in other aspects, may still have his testimony accepted because his respected status and integrity prevent him from lying or being coerced into false testimony. The commentary cites a narration from Imam Abu Yusuf supporting this position, and provides the rationale: his awe inspires fear of being caught, and his mura'ah (manliness/honour) protects him from perjury. However, the commentator notes from al-Kafi that this ruling was situation-specific; in the early times when righteousness was prevalent, it was applicable, but in later times, due to widespread injustice, the testimony of such workers is no longer accepted. This shows the dynamic nature of rulings based on social conditions. The Hanafi madhhab uses here a rational consideration (istihsan) based on the preservation of integrity, overriding the general rule of rejecting the fasiq's testimony.

Key Terms
عامل (āmil): A worker or official in a position of authority, here specifically a public servant or governor.
وجيه (wajīh): Respected, of high status and dignity in society.
مروءة (murū'ah): Integrity, manliness, moral uprightness that prevents one from base actions.
فاسق (fāsiq): A person who commits major sins or persists in minor sins, lacking 'adalah (justice).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the testimony of a respected fasiq worker accepted?
A) Because he is wealthy.
B) Because his awe (haybah) and mura'ah prevent him from lying.
C) Because the law automatically accepts testimony of all workers.
D) Because he is a close relative of the judge.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows the testimony of a fasiq worker if he is respected and possesses mura'ah, but this exception is limited to contexts where integrity prevails; in corrupt times it is suspended.

Shahadat — Testimony

Testimony for Relatives: Brother, Uncle, and Prohibited Relations – The Principle of Separation of Interests
Ruling on acceptance of testimony for certain relatives and the exception for father

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on testimony for close relatives and explain why testimony for a living father is rejected while testimony for a brother is accepted.

Core Idea
Testimony is accepted for relatives with separate property interests, but rejected if interests are connected.

In Hanafi law, the admissibility of testimony turns on the absence of suspicion (tuhma) regarding bias. This lesson examines the rule for testifying on behalf of relatives: brother, uncle, milk relatives, and in-laws are acceptable because their financial interests are distinct. However, a son may not testify for his living father because their economic interests are intertwined. We also explore a case from al-Qunyah where prior litigation disqualifies a witness.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify the relatives listed in the matn: brother, uncle, anyone prohibited in marriage due to milk relationship (rada') or in-law relationship (musahara). Testimony for them is accepted.
2
Identify the rationale (ta'lil)
The reason is the absence of tuhma (suspicion of bias) because their ownership and benefits are separate, and none has control (basuta) over the other's property.
This is the key principle: separate property interests = no tuhma.
3
Examine the exception for the father
The commentary explains that testimony for a living father is not accepted because the benefits of ownership between father and son are connected; testifying for the father is like testifying for oneself. For a deceased father, it is accepted.
The connection of interests creates tuhma.
4
Apply the ruling from al-Qunyah
If a witness has been actively litigating on behalf of a party for years, then testifies in the same case, their testimony is rejected because they have become partisan.
This is an extension of the principle: prior involvement creates bias.
5
Synthesize the underlying principle
A witness must be free from any personal interest or involvement in the case. The Hanafi school thus carefully distinguishes between relatives based on the separateness or connectedness of their property interests.
This principle guides all rulings on testimony in the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأخيه وعمه ، ومَن حُرِّمَ رضاعاً أو مصاهرة. [٢] قوله : ولأخيه...الخ؛ أي وتقبل الشهادة لهم؛ لانعدام التهمة، لأنَّ الأملاك ومنافعها متباينة، ولا بسوطة لبعضهم في مال بعض وفي «المحيط البرهاني»: وهذا الجواب لا يشكل فيما إذا شهد لأبيه والأب ميّت، إنما يشكل فيما إذا شهد لأبيه والأب حي. والجواب : إنَّ شهادة الإنسان لأبيه إنّما لا تقبل؛ لأنَّ منافع الأملاك بين الأب والابن متصلة، وكانت الشهادة للأب شهادة لنفسه من وجه فلم تقبل، وأما شهادته لأخيه فليست لنفسه أصلا؛ لتباين الأملاك. انتهى. وفي «القنية: امتدت الخصومة سنين، ومع المدَّعي أخ وابن عم يخاصمان له مع المدعى عليه، ثم شهدا له في هذه الخصومة بعد هذه الخصومات لا تقبل شهادتهما. انتهى.
And (testimony is accepted) for his brother and his uncle, and whoever is prohibited (in marriage) due to rada' (milk) or musahara (in-law relationship). [2] He said: 'For his brother...etc.' i.e., testimony is accepted for them due to the absence of accusation, because ownerships and their benefits are separate, and none of them has control over the property of the other. And in al-Muhit al-Burhani: This answer is not problematic when he testifies for his deceased father, it is only problematic when he testifies for his father while the father is alive. The answer: Indeed, a person's testimony for his father is not accepted because the benefits of ownership between father and son are connected, so the testimony for the father is in a sense testimony for oneself, so it is not accepted. As for his testimony for his brother, it is not for himself at all due to the separation of properties. End. And in al-Qunyah: A lawsuit extended for years, and with the plaintiff were his brother and cousin who were litigating on his behalf against the defendant, then they testified for him in this lawsuit after these litigations, their testimony is not accepted. End.
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This lesson deals with the acceptance of testimony on behalf of certain close relatives. The matn states that testimony for one's brother, uncle, and those related through rada' (milk-kinship) or musahara (affinity through marriage) is accepted. The reasoning is that the legal connection does not create a suspicion of bias (tuhma) because their properties are separate and neither has authority over the other's wealth. The commentary then addresses a specific issue: testimony for a father. If the father is deceased, it is clearly accepted. But if the father is alive, the testimony is not accepted because the economic interests between father and son are intertwined; in testifying for the father, the son is indirectly benefiting himself. This is in contrast to testifying for a brother, where the interests are completely separate. Finally, a ruling from al-Qunyah is cited: if witnesses have actively litigated on behalf of the plaintiff for years, then later testify for him in the same dispute, their testimony is rejected because they have become partisan. This underscores the Hanafi principle that witnesses must be neutral and free from any personal involvement in the case.

Key Terms
رضاع (raḍāʻ): Milk-suckling relationship that creates prohibition for marriage.
مصاهرة (muṣāharah): In-law relationship through marriage.
تهمة (tuhmah): Suspicion of bias or self-interest that could affect a witness's credibility.
بسوطة (basūṭah): Authority or control; here, lack of control over each other's property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is testimony for a living father not accepted according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because the son is too emotionally attached.
B) Because the benefits of ownership between father and son are connected.
C) Because the father is not considered a reliable witness.
D) Because the son cannot testify against his father.

Key Takeaway
Testimony is accepted for relatives with separate property interests, but rejected if the witness's interests are connected to the party, as with a living father or after prior litigation.

Testimony of Witnesses Who Have Litigated with the Defendant
Ruling on accepting testimony if witnesses had prior litigation with defendant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which the testimony of a witness who has previously litigated with the defendant is accepted in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Prior litigation does not disqualify a witness; only lack of 'adalah and excessive bias do.

This lesson addresses a common concern: if a witness has had a legal dispute with the defendant, is their testimony still valid? The Hanafi sources clarify that mere prior litigation does not automatically render a witness unreliable. Instead, the key is the witness's integrity ('adalah) and whether they exhibited partisanship in that prior case. By understanding this nuance, you will be able to properly assess witness credibility in court.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State the main legal position: the testimony of witnesses who have litigated with the defendant is accepted.
This is from Khazanat al-Fatawa and al-Sirajiyya.
2
Note the primary condition
The witnesses must be 'adl (just). This is the overarching requirement for all witnesses.
'Adl means morally upright and credible.
3
Interpret the qualification
The commentator adds that the ruling applies 'when they did not assist the plaintiff in the litigation, or did not do so excessively.' This means the witness must not have actively sided with the plaintiff in the prior dispute, or if they did, it must not indicate bias.
Assisting the plaintiff could show partisanship.
4
Understand the rationale
The reason is to avoid bias. If the witness remains neutral and just, their testimony is reliable despite the past conflict.
The goal is to ensure impartiality.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider a case where two neighbors quarrel, then one testifies against the other. If the witness is known for honesty and did not take an active role in the quarrel, his testimony is accepted.
The judge must assess the extent of involvement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في «البحر» بعد نقل هذا القول المذكور: قال في «خزانة الفتاوى»: إذا تخاصم الشهود والمدعى عليه تقبل إن كانوا عدولاً. انتهى. قال: وينبغي حمله على ما إذا لم يساعد المدعي في الخصومة، أو لم يكثر ذلك منهم توفيقاً. انتهى. ونقل في «السراجية» ما يوافق «خزانة الفتاوى» حيث قال: إذا تشاجرا ثم شهد أحدهما على الآخر تقبل إن كان عدلاً. انتهى.
And it is said in al-Bahr after transmitting this mentioned saying: It is said in Khazanat al-Fatawa: When the witnesses and the defendant have disputed (litigated), their testimony is accepted if they are just. End. He said: And it should be interpreted as when they did not assist the plaintiff in the litigation, or they did not do so excessively, as a reconciliation. End. And it is transmitted in al-Sirajiyya that which agrees with Khazanat al-Fatawa, where he said: When two persons quarrel, then one of them testifies against the other, it is accepted if he is just. End.
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This lesson addresses a scenario where potential witnesses have themselves been involved in litigation with the defendant. According to Khazanat al-Fatawa, if the witnesses and the defendant have had a dispute or litigation, the testimony of such witnesses is still acceptable provided they are ‘adl (just). However, the commentator adds a qualification: this ruling applies only if the witnesses did not assist the plaintiff (i.e., the person in whose favour they are testifying) in that prior litigation, or that the assistance was not excessive. This is to ensure that the witnesses are not biased. The same opinion is reported in al-Sirajiyya, which gives the example of two people who quarrel, and then one testifies against the other: if he is just, his testimony is accepted. This shows that mere prior dispute does not automatically disqualify a witness; rather, the key is their ‘adalah and lack of partisanship in the specific case.

Key Terms
تخاصم (takhāṣum): Mutual litigation or dispute between two parties.
عدول (ʻudūl): Plural of 'adl, just witnesses who are upright and credible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Khazanat al-Fatawa, what condition must be met for witnesses who have litigated with the defendant to have their testimony accepted?
A) They must be wealthy.
B) They must be just and not have assisted the plaintiff excessively.
C) They must be relatives of the plaintiff.
D) They must have won the previous litigation.

Key Takeaway
Testimony of a witness who has previously litigated with the defendant is accepted if the witness is just and does not exhibit excessive bias.

Testimony of a Blind Person: The Hanafi Position and Its Proofs
Detailed dispute on acceptance of blind testimony and its exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the testimony of a blind person and explain the proofs and counter-proofs in the dispute with other schools.

Core Idea
Blind testimony is rejected due to unreliable identification and abundance of sighted witnesses.

The testimony of a blind person has been a subject of debate among the schools of thought. While the Hanafi school generally does not accept such testimony, there are nuanced exceptions. This lesson unpacks the various positions, focusing on the Hanafi reasoning and its response to opposing views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main positions
List the views of Abu Hanifa, Zufar, Abu Yusuf, al-Shafi'i, and Malik regarding blind testimony.
Note the exception for hearsay (tasammu') in one narration from Abu Hanifa.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Understand why the Hanafi school rejects blind testimony for rights other than hearsay: the blind cannot reliably distinguish litigants by tone, risk of talqin, and abundance of sighted witnesses.
The analogy with hudud and qisas (consensus rejection) strengthens the argument.
3
Examine the opposing argument
Understand the reasoning of Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i: the blind equal the sighted in hearing, performance relies on speech, and identification by name/lineage suffices.
They analogize to testimony on a deceased person, where pointing is impossible.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
See the key distinction: in live cases, sighted witnesses are abundant; in deceased cases, necessity dictates using name/lineage.
The Hanafis block the means to error (sadd al-dhara'i') by rejecting blind testimony.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice: Hanafi courts generally reject blind testimony (except for hearsay); Shafi'i accepts if sighted at tahammul; Maliki accepts absolutely.
This affects how cases involving blind witnesses are handled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي رواية عن أبي حنيفة لله تقبل فيما يجرى فيه التَّسامعُ ، وهو قولُ زُفَرَ له ، وعند أبي يوسف له والشَّافِعِي له تقبل إذا كان بصيراً عند التحمل، وإن الأداء قبل القضاء فلا يقضي القاضي عند أبي حنيفة له ومحمد الله خلافا لأبي يوسف له ، وقوله أظهر . عمي بعد [١]قوله : تقبل ...الخ ؛ لأنه يساوي البصير في السماع، إذ لا خلل في سمعه. [٢] قوله : تقبل إذا كان...الخ ؛ لحصول المقصود بالمعاينة، وهو العلم، والأداء يختص بالقول، ولسانه صحيح فصيح ، والتعريف يحصل بالنسبة كما في الشهادة على الميت، ولا خلل في حفظه، ولم يفت في حقه إلا الإشارة، وذكر الاسم يقوم مقامهما تعدرها، كما في الشهادة على الميت. وقال مالك : تقبل شهادته مطلقاً كالبصير. ولنا : إنَّ الأداء يفتقر إلى التمييز بين الخصمين، ولا يفرق بينهما إلا بالنغمة ، فيخشى عليه التلقين من الخصم، إذ النغمة تشبه النغمة، وربما يشاركه غيره في الاسم والنسب، فكان فيه شبهة، وهذه الشبهة يمكن التحرُّزُ عنها بجنس الشهود، فإنَّ بالشهودِ البصراء كثرة ، وفيه غنية عن شهادة الأعمى. بخلاف الشهادة على الميت، فإنَّ الاحتراز عنه بجنس الشهود غير ممكن ؛ لأنَّ المدعي وإن استكثر من الشهودِ يحتاجُ إلى إقامة الاسم له، والنسبة مقام الإشارة، والنسبة لا تكون إلا لتعريف الغائب لا الحاضر، فصار كالحدود والقصاص، فإنَّ شهادة الأعمى في الحدودِ والقصاص لا تقبل إجماعاً، فكذا في غيرها. [٣]قوله : فلا يقضى...الخ ؛ لأنَّ قيام الأهلية شرط وقت القضاء لتصير حجّةُ، فصار كما إذا خرس أو جنّ أو فسق أو ارتد نعوذ بالله من ذلك، بخلاف ما إذا ماتوا أو غابوا ؛ لأنّ الأهلية تنتهي بالموت، وبالغيبة باقية على حالها. كذا في «التبيين». [٤]قوله : وقوله أظهر ...الخ ؛ ردَّه يعقوب باشا : بأنَّ المفهوم من سائر الكتب عدم أظهريّته.
And in a narration from Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him): it is accepted in matters where hearsay is sufficient, and that is the statement of Zufar (may Allah have mercy on him). And according to Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) and al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him): it is accepted if he was sighted at the time of bearing testimony, and if he was blind at the time of performance, then the judge does not adjudicate based on it according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) contrary to Abu Yusuf, and his statement is more apparent. He became blind after. [1] He said: 'It is accepted...etc.' because he equals the sighted person in hearing, as there is no defect in his hearing. [2] He said: 'It is accepted if...etc.' because the objective is achieved by seeing, which is knowledge, and performance is specific to speech, and his tongue is sound and eloquent, and identification is achieved by lineage just like testimony concerning a deceased person, and there is no defect in his memory, and nothing is lost from his side except pointing, and mentioning the name takes its place when pointing is impossible, just as in testimony concerning a deceased person. And Malik said: His testimony is accepted absolutely like the sighted. We (Hanafis) say: Indeed, performance requires distinguishing between the two litigants, and he can only distinguish them by the tone of voice, so he is susceptible to prompting from the litigant, since one tone resembles another, and perhaps someone else shares the name and lineage, so there is ambiguity. And this ambiguity can be avoided by [using] sighted witnesses, for sighted witnesses are abundant, and there is self-sufficiency from the testimony of the blind. Unlike testimony concerning a deceased person, because avoiding [ambiguity] by [using] sighted witnesses is not possible, because the plaintiff, even if he brings many witnesses, needs to establish the name and lineage in place of pointing, and lineage is only for identifying the absent, not the present. So it becomes like hudud and qisas, for the testimony of a blind person in hudud and qisas is not accepted by consensus, so likewise in other [cases]. [3] He said: 'So he does not adjudicate...etc.' because the existence of legal capacity is a condition at the time of adjudication for it to become a proof, so it becomes as if he became mute, insane, sinful, or apostate (may Allah protect us from that), unlike if they died or were absent, because capacity ends with death, and with absence it remains as it was. As in al-Tabyin. [4] He said: 'And his statement is more apparent...etc.' Ya'qub Pasha refuted it: 'Indeed, what is understood from other books is that it is not more apparent.'
Source pages p. 562

Commentary
This lesson presents a comprehensive treatment of the testimony of a blind person, and the sub-case of a person who becomes blind after bearing testimony. The matn states simply 'لا من أعمى' (not from a blind person), but the commentary reveals significant disagreement. Several positions are presented: 1. A narration from Abu Hanifa: The blind person's testimony is accepted in matters where hearsay is sufficient (i.e., where the witness reports what is commonly known, not what requires direct vision). This is also the view of Zufar. 2. Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i: The blind person's testimony is accepted if he was sighted at the time of bearing testimony (tahammul), even if he is blind at the time of performance (ada'). This is based on the idea that the essential objective was achieved through sight, and his memory and speech are intact; identification can be made through name and lineage, just as in testimony concerning a deceased person where pointing is impossible. 3. Malik: The blind person's testimony is accepted absolutely, like that of a sighted person. 4. The position attributed to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (according to the commentator) is that if a person becomes blind after bearing testimony and before the judge's ruling, the judge should not rule based on that testimony. Abu Yusuf disagrees and says it is permissible, and the commentator says Abu Yusuf's view is more apparent (أظهر). However, Ya'qub Pasha refutes this, stating that other books indicate it is not more apparent. The Hanafi response to Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i's position is provided: performance requires distinguishing between litigants, and a blind person can only distinguish by tone, which is unreliable and subject to prompting. Moreover, there is no need to accept the blind person's testimony because sighted witnesses are abundant. This contrasts with testimony concerning a deceased person, where the need is greater and avoidance is not possible. This lesson is a classic example of Hanafi ihtiyat (caution) and the use of sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking means to evil) - the potential for error and manipulation leads to rejection. The dispute also illustrates the Hanafi principle of 'ibra li ghalib (consideration of the predominant) - since sighted witnesses are common, there is no need to risk the uncertainty of blind testimony.

Key Terms
أعمى (aʻmā): A blind person; in this context, one who lacks sight at the time of testimony.
تحمل (taḥammul): The act of bearing or receiving testimony, i.e., witnessing the event.
أداء (adāʼ): The act of performing or delivering testimony in court.
النغمة (al-naghmah): The tone of voice, which a blind person must rely on to distinguish litigants.
التلقين (al-talqīn): Prompting or coaching from a litigant, which a blind witness might be vulnerable to.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the testimony of a blind person generally not accepted in cases requiring direct observation?
A) Because the blind are not trustworthy.
B) Because they cannot distinguish between litigants reliably and there is risk of prompting.
C) Because they are not allowed to be witnesses in court.
D) Because they cannot speak clearly.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rejects blind testimony for live events due to unreliable identification and abundance of sighted witnesses, but accepts it for hearsay.

Shahadat — Testimony
advanced
6 min
Becoming Blind After Bearing Testimony: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ruling based on testimony of a witness who becomes blind

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a witness who becomes blind after bearing testimony and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Once blind after bearing, testimony is rejected except in Abu Yusuf's view.

A unique scenario arises when a sighted witness bears testimony (tahammul) but becomes blind before performance (ada'). This lesson explores the disagreement between Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf, and the weight of their opinions in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
A person is sighted at the time of witnessing an event (tahammul) but becomes blind before he testifies in court (ada').
The key question: does his blindness affect the legal validity of his testimony?
2
Identify the Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: The judge should not rule based on his testimony. Abu Yusuf: The judge may rule.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Trace the proof for Abu Yusuf
He argues that the legal capacity is intact; only the ability to point is lost, which is replaced by name/lineage, similar to testimony on the deceased.
This reasoning aligns with the Shafi'i position.
4
Analyze the Hanafi majority proof
They argue that legal capacity at the time of ruling is a condition for the testimony to be a proof. Blindness nullifies this capacity, analogous to going mute, insane, sinful, or apostate.
The condition is time-sensitive: it must exist at ada'.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The text states Abu Yusuf's view is أظهر (more apparent), but Ya'qub Pasha refutes this, indicating that other books do not support this preference. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is the majority position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
This shows that later tarjih can override a textual indication of preference.

Commentary
This lesson presents a comprehensive treatment of the testimony of a blind person, and the sub-case of a person who becomes blind after bearing testimony. The matn states simply 'لا من أعمى' (not from a blind person), but the commentary reveals significant disagreement. Several positions are presented: 1. تحمل (taḥammul): The act of bearing or receiving testimony, i.e., witnessing the event.
أداء (adāʼ): The act of performing or delivering testimony in court.
الأهلية (al-ahliyyah): Legal capacity, which must exist at the time of adjudication for testimony to be valid.

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), what is the ruling on a witness who becomes blind after bearing testimony?
A) His testimony is accepted if he was sighted at the time of bearing.
B) The judge should not rule based on his testimony.
C) His testimony is accepted only for hearsay matters.
D) The judge may rule, but it is not preferred.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, if a witness becomes blind after bearing testimony, the judge should not rule on his testimony, despite Abu Yusuf's contrary view and the text indicating it is أظهر, because later tarjih favours the majority.

Shahadat — Testimony

Testimony of the Blind Person (Shahadat al-A'ma)
Ruling on accepting blind testimony with ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on testimony of a blind person, identify the conditions for its acceptance, and evaluate the ikhtilaf regarding performance after loss of sight.

Core Idea
Blind testimony is generally invalid due to ambiguity in distinguishing litigants, except in matters of common transmission.

The testimony of a blind person raises questions about the conditions of legal capacity at the time of bearing and performance. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents the Hanafi position, the views of other imams, and the reasoning behind the school's reliance on sighted witnesses. We will examine the ikhtilaf and the qiyas used to draw analogies to hudud and qisas.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi default ruling and its exception.
Identify that the Hanafi school generally does not accept blind testimony, except in matters of common transmission (tasammu') like lineage and marriage.
The exception is based on a narration from Abu Hanifa.
2
Identify the opposing positions.
Note that Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i accept blind testimony if the witness was sighted at bearing, and Malik accepts it absolutely. Zufar agrees with the exception for tasammu'.
These differences hinge on the importance of sight at the time of performance.
3
Analyse the Hanafi proof.
Examine the illa: a blind person cannot distinguish litigants except by voice, which creates risk of prompting and ambiguity, and sighted witnesses are available.
The Hanafi response contrasts with testimony about a deceased person, where such ambiguity is unavoidable.
4
Examine the qiyas from hudud and qisas.
The text analogizes blind testimony in other matters to hudud and qisas, where it is rejected by consensus due to similar ambiguity.
This strengthens the Hanafi position that the ruling is general.
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf.
The practical difference: if a witness becomes blind after bearing but before performing, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad the testimony is invalid, while Abu Yusuf accepts it.
This is the key point of dispute within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية وفي رواية عن أبي حنيفة لله تقبل" فيما يجرى فيه التَّسامعُ ، وهو قولُ زُفَرَ له ، وعند أبي يوسف له والشَّافِعِي له تقبلُ إذا كان بصيراً عند التحمل، وإن الأداء قبل القضاء فلا يقضي القاضي عند أبي حنيفة له ومحمد الله خلافا لأبي يوسف له ، وقوله أظهر . عمي بعد [١]قوله : تقبل ...الخ ؛ لأنه يساوي البصير في السماع، إذ لا خلل في سمعه. [٢] قوله : تقبل إذا كان...الخ ؛ لحصول المقصود بالمعاينة، وهو العلم، والأداء يختص بالقول، ولسانه صحيح فصيح ، والتعريف يحصل بالنسبة كما في الشهادة على الميت، ولا خلل في حفظه، ولم يفت في حقه إلا الإشارة، وذكر الاسم يقوم مقامهما تعدرها، كما في الشهادة على الميت. وقال مالك : تقبل شهادته مطلقاً كالبصير. ولنا : إنَّ الأداء يفتقر إلى التمييز بين الخصمين، ولا يفرق بينهما إلا بالنغمة ، فيخشى عليه التلقين من الخصم، إذ النغمة تشبه النغمة، وربما يشاركه غيره في الاسم والنسب، فكان فيه شبهة، وهذه الشبهة يمكن التحرَّزُ عنها بجنس الشهود، فإنَّ بالشهودِ البصراء كثرة ، وفيه غنية عن شهادة الأعمى. بخلاف الشهادة على الميت، فإنَّ الاحتراز عنه بجنس الشهود غير ممكن ؛ لأنَّ المدعي وإن استكثر من الشهودِ يحتاجُ إلى إقامة الاسم له، والنسبة مقام الإشارة، والنسبة لا تكون إلا لتعريف الغائب لا الحاضر، فصار كالحدود والقصاص، فإنَّ شهادة الأعمى في الحدودِ والقصاص لا تقبل إجماعاً، فكذا في غيرها. [٣]قوله : فلا يقضى...الخ ؛ لأنَّ قيام الأهلية شرط وقت القضاء لتصير حجّةُ، فصار كما إذا خرس أو جنّ أو فسق أو ارتد نعوذ بالله من ذلك، بخلاف ما إذا ماتوا أو غابوا ؛ لأنّ الأهلية تنتهي بالموت، وبالغيبة باقية على حالها. [٤]قوله : وقوله أظهر ...الخ ؛ ردَّه يعقوب باشا : بأنَّ المفهوم من سائر الكتب عدم أظهريّته.
Cream of the Conclusion for the Support of Care. And in a narration from Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) that it is accepted in what people commonly transmit, and this is the saying of Zufar. And according to Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i that it is accepted if he was sighted at the time of bearing witness. And if the performance (of testimony) is (after he becomes blind) before the judge's judgment, the judge shall not judge according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf, and his saying is more apparent. He became blind afterwards. [1] His saying: 'it is accepted' etc.; because he equals the sighted in hearing, since there is no defect in his hearing. [2] His saying: 'it is accepted if he was' etc.; because the purpose is achieved by direct observation, which is knowledge, and performance is specific to speech, and his tongue is sound and eloquent, and identification is obtained by lineage as in testimony regarding the deceased, and there is no defect in his memory, and nothing is missing for him except pointing, and mentioning the name takes its place when it is difficult, as in testimony regarding the deceased. And Malik said: his testimony is accepted absolutely like the sighted. And we (Hanafis) say: performance requires distinction between the two litigants, and one distinguishes them only by voice, so it is feared that he may be prompted by the litigant, since voices resemble each other, and perhaps others share his name and lineage, so there is ambiguity. This ambiguity can be avoided by the abundance of sighted witnesses, and there is sufficiency in that, so there is no need for the blind man's testimony. Unlike testimony regarding the deceased, because avoidance by the abundance of witnesses is not possible; for the plaintiff, even if he brings many witnesses, needs to establish the name and lineage in place of pointing, and lineage is only for identifying the absent, not the present, so it becomes like hudud and qisas, for the blind man's testimony in hudud and qisas is not accepted by consensus, thus also in other than them. [3] His saying: 'the judge does not judge' etc.; because the possession of legal capacity is a condition at the time of judgment for the testimony to become a proof, so it becomes as if he became mute, insane, sinful, or apostate (we seek refuge with Allah from that), unlike when they die or are absent; because capacity ends with death, and in absence it remains as it was. [4] His saying: 'his saying is more apparent' etc.; Ya'qub Pasha refuted it, that what is understood from the rest of the books is that it is not more apparent.
Source pages p. 562

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary (sharh) on a matn regarding the acceptance of testimony from a blind person. It begins with the matn's ruling: in one narration from Abu Hanifa, the testimony of a blind person is accepted in matters that people commonly transmit orally (like lineage, marriage, etc.), and Zufar agrees. According to Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, it is accepted if the witness was sighted at the time of bearing the testimony (i.e., he saw the event). If he later became blind before testifying in court, then according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the judge should not accept his testimony, while Abu Yusuf accepts it, and the author states that Abu Hanifa's view is more apparent (azhar). The commentary then explains each part. The first point clarifies that a blind person is equal to a sighted person in hearing, so his testimony is acceptable in that respect. The second point explains the rationale behind Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i's position: the purpose of testimony is to convey knowledge, and blind person can convey what he saw while sighted. He still has proper speech and memory, and identification can be done by lineage (as in testimony about a deceased person). Imam Malik goes further and accepts blind testimony absolutely. The Hanafi response (wa lana) argues that testimony in court requires the witness to distinguish between the litigants, which a blind person can only do by voice, making him susceptible to prompting. Moreover, voices can be similar and names shared, creating ambiguity. Since there is no shortage of sighted witnesses, there is no need to rely on the blind. However, this reasoning does not apply to testimony about a deceased person, because there the witness must identify the deceased by name and lineage, which cannot be replaced by sighted witnesses, so blind testimony is necessary. Thus, analogy is made to hudud and qisas, where blind testimony is universally rejected. The third point explains why if the witness loses capacity (like blindness, insanity, apostasy) before the judge's ruling, the testimony is invalid, as capacity is a condition for the proof. The fourth point notes that Ya'qub Pasha refuted the claim that Abu Hanifa's view is more apparent, citing other texts.

Key Terms
التَّسامعُ (al-tasammu'): Matters that are commonly transmitted orally among people, such as lineage, marriage, and death.
التحمل (al-tahammul): The act of bearing witness, i.e., observing or hearing the event being testified to.
الأداء (al-ada'): The act of delivering testimony in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a witness was sighted when he observed the event but became blind before testifying in court, what is the ruling on his testimony?
A) It is accepted according to all Hanafi imams.
B) It is not accepted according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but accepted according to Abu Yusuf.
C) It is accepted only in hudud and qisas.
D) It is accepted absolutely as per Imam Malik.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school generally rejects blind testimony due to inability to distinguish litigants, but makes an exception for matters of common transmission where sight is not needed.

Testimony of Slaves and Those Punished for Qadhf
Compilation of rulings on rejected testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the testimony of slaves and those punished for qadhf, and explain the Hanafi rebuttal to the Shafi'i exception based on repentance.

Core Idea
A slave lacks legal authority to testify; one punished for qadhf loses testimony permanently — repentance does not restore what is part of the hadd.

In this passage, the text presents two categories of people whose testimony is permanently rejected in Hanafi law: slaves and those who have been subjected to the fixed punishment (hadd) for false accusation of adultery (qadhf). While the ruling on slaves is straightforward — their lack of legal authority (wilaya) disqualifies them — the ruling on the qadhf offender involves a significant inter-madhhab dispute. The Shafi'i school accepts testimony after repentance, relying on Quranic exception. The Hanafi school argues that the rejection is part of the hadd itself and therefore not removed by repentance. We will examine the proofs on both sides and understand the Hanafi methodological approach to textual interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories and their ruling in Hanafi law
From the text, list: (1) a slave (mamluk) of any type, and (2) a person who has been subjected to hadd al-qadhf. The ruling for both is that their testimony is not accepted (la tuqbal shahadatuhum).
The ruling for the qadhf offender applies even after repentance (wa in taba).
2
Understand the rationale for rejecting slave testimony
The text explains that a slave has no legal authority (wilaya) over himself, like a minor. Since he cannot even be a witness for himself, he cannot be a witness for others. This applies to all categories: qinn (chattel), mukatab, mudabbir, and umm walad.
Partial freedom (e.g., part-slave) is treated like a mukatab; testimony is also rejected.
3
Analyze the primary proof for rejecting testimony after qadhf
The proof is Quranic: 'And never accept their testimony' (Surah an-Nur 24:4). The text argues this command is perpetual (abada). Moreover, rejecting testimony is part of the hadd itself — a deterrent punishment that 'pains the heart' just as lashing pains the body.
Since the original hadd (lashing) is not nullified by repentance, the completion (rejection of testimony) also remains.
4
Present the opposing school's proof and the Hanafi response
Imam al-Shafi'i (and Malik, Uthman al-Batti) point to the exception in the next verse: 'except those who repent' (24:5). They interpret that the exception applies to the rejection of testimony, so after repentance testimony is accepted. The Hanafi response is twofold: (a) the exception refers only to the immediately following clause 'and those are the wicked' (i.e., they are no longer called wicked), not to the command; (b) it is a disconnected exception (munqati') meaning 'but', referring to divine forgiveness, not legal testimony.
Thus the Hanafi school maintains the perpetual rejection.
5
Derive the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In Hanafi law, a person who was punished for qadhf can never serve as a witness, even if he sincerely repents. In Shafi'i law, his testimony becomes acceptable after repentance. This difference affects court proceedings in jurisdictions that follow different schools.
The Hanafi position is relied upon (mufta bihi) based on the literal meaning and the logic of hadd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشهادة والرجوع عنها باب القبول وعدمه ومملوك، ومحدودٍ في قذف وإن تاب إلا من حد في كفره فأسلم [١] قوله : ومملوك ؛ أي ولا تقبل الشهادة من مملوك سواء كان قناً أو مكاتباً أو مديراً أو أم ولد ؛ لأنَّ المملواً لا ولاية له على نفسه كالصبي، فعلى غيره أولى، ومعتق البعض كالمكاتب، والمعتقُ في المرض كالمكاتب في زمن سعاية لا تقبل شهادته. كذا في «البزازية». [٢] قوله : محدود في قذف وإن تاب ؛ أي لا تقبل شهادته لقوله : وَلَا نَقْبَلُوا لَمْ شَهَدَةٌ أَبَدًا (٣) ؛ ولأنَّ ردَّ الشهادة من تمام الحدّ ؛ لكونه مانعاً عن القذف ؛ لأنَّ فيه معنى الزجر ؛ لأنه يؤلم قلبه ، كما أنَّ الجلد يؤلم بدنه ، وقد آذاه بلسانه، فعوقب بإهدار منفعة لسانه جزاءً وفاقاً، فيبقى بعد التوبة كأصله وهو الجلد ؛ لأن ردّ شهادته من تمام الحد، وأصلُ الحدّ لا يرتفعُ بالتوبة ، فكذا المتمم له. كذا في «الكفاية». [٣] قوله : تقبل عند الشافعي له إذا تاب ؛ لقوله لة : إِلَّا الَّذِينَ تَابُوا . وحكم المستثنى يكون بخلاف المستثنى منه، وبه قال مالك لله و عثمان البتي له. قلنا : الاستثناء ينصرف إلى ما يليه وهو قوله : وَأَوْلَبَكَ هُمُ الْفَنسِقُونَ ) لأنه للاستئناف ؛ لأنَّ ما قبله أمر ونهي، وهذه جملة اسميَّةٌ إخبار عن حال قائمةٍ فيهم، فلم يحسن العطف فكانت مستأنفة ، فانصرافُ الاستثناء إلى هذه الجملة فحسب، أو هوا استثناء منقطع بمعنى لكن ؛ لأنَّ التائبين ليسوا من جنس الفاسقين، فكان معناه : لكن الذين تابوا فإن الله يغفر ذنوبهم ويرحمهم، فكان كلاماً مبتدأ غير متعلق بما قبله.
Book of Testimony and Retraction from It, Chapter of Acceptance and Its Opposite. And a slave, and one who has been subjected to the prescribed punishment for slander, even if he repents, except one who was punished for his disbelief and then embraced Islam. [1] His saying: 'a slave'; i.e., the testimony of a slave is not accepted, whether he is a chattel slave, a mukatab, a mudabbir, or an umm walad, because a slave has no authority over himself, like a child, so over others it is more so. And a part-slave is like a mukatab, and one freed while ill is like a mukatab in the period of earning, his testimony is not accepted. This is in al-Bazzaziyyah. [2] His saying: 'one who has been subjected to the prescribed punishment for slander, even if he repents'; i.e., his testimony is not accepted because of His saying 'And never accept their testimony' (24:4), and because rejecting his testimony is part of the hadd penalty, as it is a deterrent against slander; for it contains the meaning of deterrence, because it pains his heart just as the lashing pains his body. He harmed others with his tongue, so he is punished by disabling the benefit of his tongue as a fitting recompense. Thus it remains after repentance like its origin, which is the lashing; for the rejection of his testimony is part of the hadd, and the original hadd is not removed by repentance, so neither is its completion. This is in al-Kifayah. [3] His saying: 'it is accepted according to al-Shafi'i if he repents'; because of His saying 'except those who repent' (24:5). And the ruling of the excepted is contrary to the ruling of the excepted from. Malik and Uthman al-Batti also said this. We (Hanafis) say: the exception refers to what follows it, which is His saying 'and those are the wicked' (24:4), because it is for a new beginning; for what precedes it is a command and prohibition, while this is a nominal sentence informing of a state existing in them, so conjunction is not good, so it is independent. Thus the exception applies only to this sentence. Or it is a disconnected exception meaning 'but'; because the repentant are not of the same kind as the wicked, so its meaning is 'but those who repent, then Allah forgives their sins and has mercy on them', so it is a new statement not connected to what precedes it.
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage continues the chapter on the acceptance of testimony. It covers two categories of people whose testimony is rejected: (1) slaves and (2) those who have been subjected to hadd al-qadhf (the fixed punishment for false accusation of adultery). The commentary explains the first: all types of slaves—chattel, mukatab (contractually freeing), mudabbir (free on death of master), umm walad (mother of master's child)—are not accepted as witnesses because they lack legal authority over themselves, like minors. Partial freedom (like a part-slave) is similar to mukatab, and one freed during illness (which is like mukatab during earning period) also fails to have testimony accepted. The second ruling addresses the one punished for qadhf. His testimony is rejected perpetually, even after repentance. The proof is the Quranic verse (Surah an-Nur 24:4) which commands never to accept their testimony. The commentary provides a rationale: the rejection of testimony is part of the hadd itself, intended to deter slander; it pains the heart as lashing pains the body. Since the original hadd (lashing) is not nullified by repentance (though one may be forgiven by Allah), its completion (rejection of testimony) also remains. This is the Hanafi position. The Shafi'i school holds that after repentance, such a person's testimony becomes acceptable, relying on the exception in the following verse (24:5) 'except those who repent'. The Hanafi rebuttal is twofold: either the exception refers only to the immediately following clause ('those are the wicked') and not to the command of rejecting testimony, or it is a disconnected exception meaning 'but' and is a separate statement about divine forgiveness, not about legal testimony. Thus, repentance does not reinstate the witness's credibility in Hanafi law.

Key Terms
مملوك (mamluk): A slave; a person owned by another. Includes qinn (chattel), mukatab, mudabbir, and umm walad.
محدود في قذف (mahdud fi qadhf): A person who has been subjected to the fixed punishment (hadd) for falsely accusing another of unlawful sexual intercourse (qadhf).
الحد (al-hadd): The fixed corporal punishment for certain crimes (like qadhf, theft, etc.) as prescribed in Quran and Sunnah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the testimony of a slave is categorically rejected due to lack of legal authority, and the testimony of one punished for qadhf is perpetually rejected — repentance does not restore it because the rejection is an integral part of the hadd punishment.

Rejection of Slave Testimony in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling that the testimony of a slave is not accepted

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on slave testimony, identify the categories of slaves included, and explain the rationale based on lack of wilayah.

Core Idea
A slave cannot testify because he lacks legal authority over himself, a prerequisite for testimony.

This passage from the Hanafi text 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies a key ruling: the testimony of a slave is never accepted, regardless of the type of slavery. This lesson will unpack the reasoning and scope of this ruling, grounding it in the concept of legal capacity (wilayah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Read the passage and note that the testimony of a mamluk (slave) is rejected. Understand that this includes all categories: qinn (full slave), mukatab, mudabbar, and umm walad.
2
Analyze the rationale (illah)
The text gives the reason: the slave has no wilayah (legal authority) over himself, similar to a minor. If he has no authority over himself, he has even less over others.
Wilayah is a condition for valid testimony.
3
Extend to borderline cases
Note the expansion: a partially freed slave and a slave manumitted during the master's terminal illness are also treated as slaves for the purpose of testimony.
These are extensions based on the same illah.
4
Identify the source
The ruling is cited from al-Bazzaziyyah, a later Hanafi fatwa collection, indicating its authority in the school.
The ruling is consistent with the established Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that the testimony of a slave is rejected. The commentator explains that this applies to all types of slaves: full slaves, slaves on a contract of manumission (mukatab), slaves promised freedom after the master's death (mudabbar), and umm walad. The reason given is that the slave lacks legal authority (wilayah) over himself, similar to a minor; if he has no authority over himself, he has even less over others. The commentary also extends this to a partially freed slave and a slave manumitted during the master's terminal illness, who is treated like a mukatab during the period of earning. This ruling is from al-Bazzaziyyah, a later Hanafi fatwa collection.

Key Terms
مملوك (mamlūk): A person owned by another; includes all categories of slaves
ولاية (wilāyah): Legal authority to act on behalf of oneself or others
