Exception: Testimony Accepted from One Punished for Kufr After Islam
Exception to permanent rejection of testimony after qadhf punishment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on testimony of a dhimmī who becomes Muslim after being punished for qadhf, and distinguish it from the case of an emancipated slave.

Core Idea
Islam creates a new qualification for testimony—the old rejection does not carry over.

The general rule in Hanafi fiqh is that a person punished for qadhf (false accusation of adultery) has their testimony permanently rejected. However, there is a critical exception: if the punishment occurred while the person was a non-Muslim (dhimmī) and they later embrace Islam, their testimony becomes valid. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this exception and contrasts it with the analogous case of a slave who is punished and then freed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is the testimony of a former dhimmī who was punished for qadhf and then became Muslim. The ruling is that his testimony is accepted against both dhimmīs and Muslims.
The text says: 'جازت شهادته على أهل الذمة وعلى المسلمين جميعاً'.
2
Locate the reasoning
The proof (dalīl) is a rational argument: the testimony after Islam is a new testimony acquired after the punishment. Therefore, the previous rejection (due to punishment in kufr) does not attach to it.
The text states: 'لأن هذه الشهادة استفادها بعد الحد، بالإسلام، فلم يلحقها رد'.
3
Analyze the 'illa (effective cause)
The key distinction is that the disqualification from qadhf punishment is tied to the person's status at the time of the punishment. When the person becomes Muslim, their legal personality changes, so the new testimony is not tainted by the old disability.
This is a form of istiḥsān based on change of status.
4
Contrast with the slave case
The text compares with a slave who is punished and then freed. In that case, the testimony remains rejected because at the time of punishment the slave had no valid testimony at all (due to slavery), so the rejection was completed after emancipation.
Key difference: a slave cannot testify ab initio, so no new testimony can arise after emancipation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا من حد في كفره فأسلم؛ تفصيله: إن ذمياً إذا حد في قذف لم يجز شهادته بعد ذلك على أهل الذمة، ثم إذا أسلم جازت شهادته على أهل الذمة وعلى المسلمين جميعاً؛ لأن هذه الشهادة استفادها بعد الحد، بالإسلام، فلم يلحقها رد؛ لأن التي ردت غير هذه. بخلاف العبد إذا حد ثم أعتق، حيث لم تقبل شهادته؛ لأنه لم يكن له شهادة على أحد وقت الجلد، فلم يتم الرد إلا بعد الإعتاق في حقه فلا يتصور قبولها.
Except one who was punished in his kufr then became Muslim: its detail: A dhimmī if he is punished for qadhf, his testimony is not allowed after that against the dhimmīs, then if he becomes Muslim, his testimony becomes allowed against the dhimmīs and against all Muslims; because this testimony he acquired after the punishment, by Islam, so no rejection attaches to it; because the one that was rejected is different from this. Contrary to a slave if he is punished then freed, where his testimony is not accepted; because he had no testimony against anyone at the time of lashing, so the rejection was not completed except after emancipation in his case, so acceptance cannot be conceived.
Source pages p. 564

Commentary
This passage explains an exception: if a dhimmī (non-Muslim subject) is punished for qadhf, his testimony is rejected as long as he remains dhimmī. But if he later accepts Islam, his testimony becomes accepted even against Muslims. The reason is that this is a new testimony acquired after the punishment by virtue of Islam; the previous rejection was for a different situation. The passage contrasts this with a slave who is punished then freed: in that case, his testimony is still not accepted because at the time of punishment he had no testimony at all (since his testimony was already rejected due to slavery), so the rejection was completed after emancipation in his right, and it cannot be changed.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmī): A non-Muslim subject living under Muslim rule with a covenant of protection
كفر (kufr): Disbelief; here refers to the state of being a non-Muslim

Key Takeaway

Rejection of Testimony of a Worldly Enemy
Ruling that the testimony of someone who is an enemy due to worldly matters is rejected

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between worldly enmity and religious enmity and apply the ruling that testimony of a worldly enemy is rejected.

Core Idea
Worldly enmity discredits a witness; religious enmity does not.

In Hanafi law, not all enmity disqualifies a witness. This lesson examines a key distinction: animosity rooted in worldly matters versus religious conviction. Understanding this difference is crucial for correctly adjudicating witness testimony in courts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the explicit ruling: testimony of a person who is an enemy due to worldly reasons is not accepted (ghayr maqbul).
2
Understand the Rationale (Illa)
The text gives the cause: worldly enmity is haram (forbidden). A person who commits such enmity cannot be trusted not to distort the truth. Thus, their 'adalah (justice) is defective.
The illa is the lack of trustworthiness arising from forbidden enmity.
3
Contrast with Religious Enmity
Religious enmity (e.g., against a sinner or disbeliever) is praiseworthy; it shows strong faith and justice. Therefore, testimony from such a person is accepted.
This is a unique Hanafi position.
4
Apply to Examples
Consider scenarios: if A and B are business rivals and A sues B, B's testimony against A is rejected if their enmity is worldly. If C and D differ over a religious issue, C's testimony against D is accepted.
The key is the motive behind the enmity.
5
Recognize Ikhtilaf
Other schools may not draw this distinction or may reject testimony in both cases. The Hanafi school insists on the distinction, relying on the principle that hatred for Allah is commendable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعدو بسبب الدنيا؛ أي لا تقبل الشهادة منه؛ لأن المعاداة لأجل الدنيا حرام، فمن ارتكبها لا يؤمن من النقول عليه، بخلاف المعاداة لأجل الدين، فإنها تدل على كمال دينه وعدالته، كما تقدم.
And an enemy due to worldly reasons: meaning his testimony is not accepted; because animosity for the sake of the world is forbidden, so one who commits it cannot be trusted not to distort [the truth], unlike animosity for the sake of religion, for it indicates completeness of his religion and justice, as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 564

Commentary
This passage states that the testimony of a person who is an enemy of the litigant due to worldly reasons is rejected. The rationale is that such animosity is haram (forbidden) because it is based on worldly interests, and a person who engages in such enmity cannot be trusted to be truthful in testimony. In contrast, if the enmity is for religious reasons (e.g., due to doctrinal differences), it actually indicates the person's religious commitment and justice, so the testimony is accepted. This distinction is based on the principle that hatred for the sake of Allah (al-ghadab lillah) is praiseworthy, while hatred for worldly gains is blameworthy.

Key Terms
عدو بسبب الدنيا (adū bi-sabab al-dunyā): A person who is an enemy for worldly reasons, such as financial or personal disputes
عداوة (adāwah): Enmity or hostility; here, it is the basis for disqualifying a witness

Key Takeaway

Testimony for Relatives and Spouses: Prohibition and Proof
Commentary on the rule that testimony is not accepted for one's ancestor, descendant, or spouse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that testimony for one's ancestor, descendant, or spouse is rejected, cite the hadith proof, and explain the rationale of connected benefits.

Core Idea
Testimony for a parent, child, or spouse is rejected because benefits are connected, making it like testifying for oneself.

The matn in al-Wiqayah states a clear rule: testimony is not accepted for one's ancestor, descendant, or spouse. The commentary explains the proof from a hadith and the legal reasoning behind it. This lesson unpacks that proof and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The matn says 'wa la li-aslihi wa far'ihi wa zawjihi wa 'ursihi' – testimony is not accepted for these individuals. Note that 'li' indicates 'for' not 'against'.
The ruling is about acceptance (qubul), not the act of testifying.
2
Examine the Hadith Proof
The author cites a hadith listing those for whom testimony is rejected. Identify the categories mentioned.
The hadith is narrated by al-Khassaf and also reported as a saying of Shurayh.
3
Understand the Rationale (Ta'lil)
The commentator explains that benefits (manafi') are connected between these individuals, so testifying for them is like testifying for oneself, which is not allowed.
This is why zakat cannot be given to them either.
4
Apply the Exception for Testimony Against
The rule applies only when testifying for them. Testifying against them is accepted, because the bias is reversed.
An exception: if a grandfather testifies against his son for his grandson's benefit, it is rejected because the ultimate beneficiary is a descendant.
5
Extend to the Child of Li'an
The ruling extends to a child born from li'an – his testimony for his ancestor or descendant is also rejected, even though he does not inherit or receive maintenance, because the rulings of filiation are partially established.
This is based on the principle that some filiation effects remain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشهادة والرجوع عنها باب القبول وعدمه ولا لأصله، وفرعه، وزوجه، وعرسه ولا لأصله، وفرعه، وزوجه، وعرسه) في العدو لا تقبل شهادته على من يعاديه، وتقبل له، وفي الأصل إلى آخره على العكس (1) قوله : ولا لأصله وفرعه ؛ أي ولا تقبل الشهادة لهم ؛ لقوله : «لا يقبلُ شهادة الولد لوالده، ولا الوالد لولده، ولا المرأة لزوجها ولا الزوج لامرأته، ولا العبد لسيده ، ولا السيد لعبده، ولا الأجيرُ من استأجره» (١) ، رواه الخصافُ له بإسناده إلى النبي ، وهو في «مصنف ابن أبي شَيْبَة» و «عبد الرزاق» من قول شريح ، ذكره الزيلعي له في «نصب الراية» (۳). ولأنَّ المنافع بين الأولادِ والآباء متّصلة ؛ ولهذا لا يجوز أداء الزكاة إليهم، فتكون شهادة لنفسه من وجه، أطلق الفرع فشمل الفرع من وجه، فلا تقبل شهادة أولد] الملاعن لأصوله أو هو له ، أو لفروعه ؛ لثبوته من وجـه بدلـيـل صـحـة دعـوتـه مـنـه ، وعدمها من غيره، وتحرم مناكحته، ووضع الزكاة فيه ، فأحكام البنوة ثابتةٌ له إلا الإرث والنفقة من الطرفين، صرح به في «المنح»(۳)، وغيره. وقيد بالشهادة لهم ؛ ؛ لأنَّ الشهادة على أصلِهِ وفرعه مقبولة، إلا إذا شهد الجد على ابنه لابن ابنه على أبيه ، فإنّها لا تقبل لوجودِ المانع من المشهود له. كذا في «البحر» بسبب (٤) [٢]قوله : لا تقبل ... الخ ؛ قال اليَرْجُنْدِيُّ في «شرح النُّقاية»: ما ذكر من أنَّ العدو الدُّنيا لا تقبل شهادته مذكور في «المحيط» وغيره، وهو اختيار المتأخرين، والرواية المنصوصةُ ما ذكره في «خزانة الفقه»: إنّه يقبل شهادة العدو مطلقاً ؛ إذ العداوة لو كانت مسقطة للشهادة وجب أن يكون كذلك في الكل كالفسق. وذكر في «شرح السنة» : إنَّ شهادة العدو لا تقبلُ عند الشافعي ، وتقبل عند أبي حنيفة له إذا كان عدلاً. وفي «القُنية : قال أستاذنا : الصحيحُ أنَّ العدو إذا كان عدلاً يقبل شهادته مطلقاً، وعليه الاعتماد، وهذا إذا شهد العدوّ على العدوّ ، فإن شهد له ينبغي أن تقبل اتفاقا ؛ لعدم التهمة حينئذ. انتهى. (۱) قوله : خلاف الشافعي الله ؛ فيقول : لا قرابة بينهما، والزوجية قد تكون سبباً للتنافر والعدواة، وقد تكون سبباً للميل والإيثار، فصار نظير الأخوة؛ ولهذا يجري القصاص بينهما، والحبس بالدين، ولا اعتبار بالمنفعة الثابتة ضمناً كما في الغريم إذا شهد لمديونه المفلس. ولنا : ما روينا من الحديث، وما بينا في المعنى، وهو أنَّ المنافع بينهما متّصلة ؛ ولهذا يعد أحدهما غنياً لغناء صاحبه ، وقيل : المراد بقوله علا: وَوَجَدَكَ عَابِلًا فَأَغْى ) (۳) أي بمال خديجة رضي الله تعالى عنها. كذا في «التبيين»(4)، وغيره.
The Book of Testimony and Retraction from It. Chapter of Acceptance and Non-Acceptance. (And not for his ancestor, his descendant, his wife, and his spouse) (And not for his ancestor, his descendant, his wife, and his spouse) Regarding the enemy: his testimony against the one he opposes is not accepted, and it is accepted for him. In the original text to its end, it is the opposite. (1) His statement: 'And not for his ancestor and his descendant' means: testimony is not accepted for them, because of his ﷺ saying: 'The testimony of a child for his parent is not accepted, nor the parent for his child, nor the woman for her husband nor the husband for his wife, nor the slave for his master nor the master for his slave, nor the hired man for the one who hired him.' Narrated by al-Khassaf with his chain to the Prophet, and it is in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah and Abd al-Razzaq as the saying of Shurayh, mentioned by al-Zayla'i in Nasb al-Rayah. And because the benefits between children and fathers are connected; for this reason, it is not permissible to give zakat to them. So it is testimony for oneself in a sense. The term 'descendant' is absolute, so it includes a descendant in one sense. So the testimony of the child of li'an for his ancestors or he for him, or for his descendants, is not accepted, because of its affirmation in one sense by the proof of the validity of his claim from him and the lack of it from others. Marriage with him is prohibited, and zakat is not placed on him. So the rulings of filiation are established for him except inheritance and maintenance from both sides. This is explicitly stated in al-Minh and others. And it is restricted to testimony for them, because testimony against his ancestor and descendant is accepted, except if the grandfather testifies against his son for his grandson against his father, then it is not accepted due to the existence of an impediment from the one for whom testimony is given. This is in al-Bahr. (2) His statement: 'It is not accepted...' etc.; al-Yarjandi said in Sharh al-Nuqayah: What is mentioned, that the testimony of an enemy in worldly matters is not accepted, is mentioned in al-Muhit and elsewhere, and it is the choice of the later scholars. The transmitted report is what he mentioned in Khizanat al-Fiqh: Indeed, the testimony of an enemy is accepted absolutely, because if enmity were to cause rejection of testimony, it must have been the same in all cases like fisq. And it is mentioned in Sharh al-Sunnah: Indeed, the testimony of an enemy is not accepted according to al-Shafi'i, and it is accepted according to Abu Hanifa if he is just. And in al-Qunyah: Our teacher said: The correct view is that if the enemy is just, his testimony is accepted absolutely, and upon it is reliance. This is if the enemy testifies against the enemy; if he testifies for him, it should be accepted by agreement, because there is no accusation then. End. (1) His statement: 'And there is a disagreement of al-Shafi'i regarding the spouse'; he says: There is no kinship between them, and marriage may be a cause of estrangement and enmity, and may be a cause of inclination and preference, so it becomes like brotherhood. For this reason, qisas is carried out between them, and imprisonment for debt. And consideration is not given to the benefit established indirectly, as in the case of a creditor testifying for his bankrupt debtor. Our evidence: the hadith we have narrated, and what we have explained about the meaning, which is that benefits between them are connected. For this reason, one is considered rich due to the richness of his spouse. And it is said that the meaning of His exalted saying: 'And He found you in need and made you rich' refers to the wealth of Khadijah, may Allah be pleased with her. This is in al-Tabyin and others.
Source pages p. 565

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a commentary on al-Wiqayah, addresses the conditions for accepting testimony when the witness has a relationship with the party involved. The ruling stated in the matn is that testimony is not accepted for one's ancestor (parent or grandparent), descendant (child or grandchild), or spouse. The commentator immediately clarifies that this is contextual: the testimony is rejected when it benefits the relative, but accepted when it is against them. The proof is a hadith transmitted by al-Khassaf listing those for whom testimony is rejected, including the converse parent-child, spouses, master-slave, and employer-employee. The rationale given is the connection of interests—since the witness benefits indirectly, the testimony is akin to testifying for oneself. The commentator then extends this to the child of li'an (a child born from a disowned pregnancy), who inherits certain filiation rulings but not inheritance or maintenance; his testimony for his ancestor or descendant is still rejected. A specific exception: if a grandfather testifies against his son for the benefit of his grandson, it is rejected because the ultimate beneficiary (the grandson) is a descendant. The passage then discusses enmity. The core Hanafi position, according to the later scholars, is that a just enemy's testimony is accepted absolutely, because enmity does not automatically imply falsehood. The proof is that enmity is not mentioned as a disqualifier in the primary texts; only fisq (sinfulness) is. The Shafi'i view is that an enemy's testimony is not accepted at all. Finally, the commentary addresses the spouse's testimony with a rebuttal to al-Shafi'i: the Prophet's hadith explicitly rejects it, and the connection of benefit is clear, as each spouse's wealth is considered in determining eligibility for zakat and maintenance.

Key Terms
أصل (asl): Ancestor, including parents and grandparents upward.
فرع (far'): Descendant, including children and grandchildren.
زوج (zawj): Husband.
عرس ('urs): Wife, synonym for zawjah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason the Hanafi school rejects testimony for a parent or child?
A) Because the witness might lie out of love.
B) Because the benefits are connected, making it akin to testifying for oneself.
C) Because the Quran explicitly prohibits it.
D) Because the witness is not considered just.

Key Takeaway
Testimony for one's ancestor, descendant, or spouse is rejected due to the hadith and the principle of connected benefits, and this applies only when testifying for them, not against.

Shahadat — Testimony

Testimony of an Enemy and the Spouse Dispute: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Commentary on enemy testimony and Shafi'i dispute on spouse testimony.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi mufta bihi on testimony of a just enemy, and compare it with the Shafi'i view, and analyse the Shafi'i challenge regarding spousal testimony.

Core Idea
A just enemy's testimony is accepted in Hanafi; spousal testimony is rejected despite Shafi'i analogies.

The commentary continues with two important discussions: the testimony of an enemy, where the Hanafi school takes a lenient position, and the Shafi'i objection to rejecting spousal testimony. This lesson explores the proofs and the tarjih.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Position on Enemy Testimony
The later Hanafi scholars hold that a just enemy's testimony is accepted absolutely. The earlier transmitted report also accepts it unconditionally. The key condition is 'adl (justice).
This is the mufta bihi position according to al-Yarjandi and the author of al-Qunyah.
2
Understand the Shafi'i Objection on Enemy
Al-Shafi'i rejects enemy testimony absolutely because enmity creates bias. Hanafi responds that enmity does not necessarily lead to falsehood; only fisq disqualifies.
The Hanafi position is that justice overcomes the suspicion of bias.
3
Analyse the Spouse Dispute
Al-Shafi'i argues that there is no kinship between spouses, so testimony should be accepted like for a brother. He points out that qisas and imprisonment for debt are possible between them.
Hanafi responds with the explicit hadith and the connection of benefits (one is considered rich by the other's wealth).
4
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
In Hanafi, spouse's testimony is not accepted; in Shafi'i it is accepted. For enemies, Hanafi accepts if just; Shafi'i rejects absolutely. The practical outcome differs in court.
Knowing these differences helps when practicing in different jurisdictions.

Source Text

Testimony of a Master for His Slave and Mukatab: The Self-Interest Principle
Commentary on rejection of testimony of master for slave/mukatab and partner for joint affairs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the testimony of a master for his slave or mukatab, and a partner for his partner, and explain the exceptional condition that changes the ruling.

Core Idea
A master's testimony for his slave is rejected as testifying for oneself, but if the slave has debts and is sold, the master becomes a stranger and his testimony is accepted.

In this lesson, we examine a key principle in the Hanafi law of testimony: individuals who have a direct interest in the outcome cannot testify for each other. The master-slave relationship and partnership are classic examples. However, the Hanafi school recognizes that circumstances can change the status of a person, affecting the admissibility of testimony. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains these nuances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories of prohibited testimony
List the three types of witnesses whose testimony is rejected according to the matn: master for slave, master for mukatab, and partner for partner in joint matters.
2
Understand the proof
The hadith explicitly includes 'wa-la al-sayyid li-'abdihi'. The reasoning (ta'lil) is that testifying for one's slave is like testifying for oneself because the master owns the slave.
The Quranic basis is not mentioned; only the hadith is cited.
3
Analyze the exception for a slave with debt
If the slave has debts and is sold to creditors, the master becomes a stranger (ajnabi) and his testimony is accepted. The slave's condition is seen as fluid.
This shows that the ruling is not absolute but depends on the status of the parties.
4
Apply the same reasoning to the mukatab
The mukatab's status is suspended: if he pays the kitabah, he becomes free and a stranger; if he fails, he returns to slavery. Thus, the master's testimony for him is also ambiguous.
5
Note the ruling for partners
The testimony of one partner for another in joint matters is rejected due to direct benefit in the shared property.
The passage does not provide an exception for partners, but the reasoning is similar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسيد لعبدِه ، ومكاتيه ، وشريكه فيما يشتركانه وفي الزوج والعرسِ خلاف الشَّافِعِيُّ ). وسيد لعبده"، ومكاتبه، وشريكه" فيما يشتركانه) [٢] قوله : وسيد لعبده ومكاتبه ؛ لما روينا من الحديث ؛ ولأنَّ شهادة السيد لعبده شهادة لنفسه من كل وجه ؛ لأنَّ العبد وما يملكه لمولاه ، وهذا إذا لم يكن على العبد دين، أو من وجه إذا كان عليه دين ؛ لأنَّ حالَ العبد دائر بين أن يصير العبد للغرماء بسبب بيعهم إياه في دينهم، فيصيرُ أجنبياً فتقبل شهادة السيد له، أو يبقى للمولى كما كان بسبب قضاء دينه. وشهادة السيد لمكاتبه أيضاً شهادة لنفسه ؛ لكون حال العبدِ موقوفاً مراعى ، فإنَّ المكاتب إن أدّى بدل الكتابة صار أجنبياً ، وإلا عاد رقيقاً، فتكون شهادة لنفسه. (۳) قوله : وشريكه...الخ ؛ أي ولا تقبل شهادة أحد الشريكين للآخر فيما
And a master for his slave, his mukatab, and his partner in what they jointly own. And regarding the spouse, there is a disagreement of al-Shafi'i. And a master for his slave, his mukatab, and his partner in what they jointly own. (2) His statement: 'And a master for his slave and his mukatab'; because of the hadith we have narrated; and because the testimony of the master for his slave is testimony for himself in every respect, because the slave and what he owns belong to the master. This is if the slave has no debt. Or in one respect if he has a debt, because the condition of the slave fluctuates between becoming the slave of the creditors due to their selling him for their debt, thus he becomes a stranger and the master's testimony for him is accepted, or remaining the master's as before due to the payment of his debt. And the testimony of the master for his mukatab is also testimony for himself, because the condition of the slave is suspended and considered. For if the mukatab pays the kitabah, he becomes a stranger; otherwise, he returns to slavery. So it is testimony for himself. (3) His statement: 'And his partner...' etc., meaning testimony of one partner for the other is not accepted in what they jointly own.
Source pages p. 566

Commentary
This passage continues the chapter on testimony, addressing additional categories of witnesses whose testimony for a party is rejected. The matn statement lists: a master testifying for his slave, for his mukatab (a slave under a contract of manumission), and a partner for his partner in matters concerning their joint property. The commentator first addresses the master-slave relationship: the hadith previously cited explicitly rejects a master's testimony for his slave. The reason is that the slave and his possessions belong to the master, so testifying for the slave is effectively testifying for oneself. An exception arises if the slave has debts: if the slave is sold to creditors, he becomes a stranger (ajnabi), and the master's testimony for him would then be accepted. For a mukatab, the situation is similar but with a suspended status; if the mukatab pays the full kitabah, he becomes free and a stranger, so later testimony would be accepted; otherwise, he remains a slave and the testimony is rejected. Regarding partners, the commentator states that testimony of one partner for the other in matters concerning their joint property is not accepted, due to the direct benefit to the testifying partner.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has made a contract (kitabah) with his master to buy his freedom by paying installments.
شريك (sharik): Partner in joint property or business.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition does the master's testimony for his slave become accepted according to the Hanafi commentary?
A) If the slave is a mukatab.
B) If the slave has no debts.
C) If the slave has debts and is sold to his creditors, making him a stranger.
D) If the master pays the slave's debts.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rejects a master's testimony for his slave due to conflict of interest, but allows it when the slave's debts sever that interest.

Testimony of Spouses: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Explaining Hanafi acceptance of spousal testimony against Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on spousal testimony, explain the Shafi'i objection, and evaluate the Hanafi response using the madhhab dispute map.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school accepts spousal testimony because mutual benefit creates a legal connection akin to partial self-testimony.

In this lesson, we examine a classic inter-madhhab dispute: can spouses testify for or against each other? The Hanafi school says yes, while Imam al-Shafi'i says no. The disagreement hinges on how each school interprets the nature of the spousal relationship and the strength of its analogies. We will walk through the proofs and see why the Hanafi view prevails in the school.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling that the Hanafi school holds: spousal testimony is accepted.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The opposing school is the Shafi'i madhhab; they reject spousal testimony entirely.
Imam al-Shafi'i relies on analogy to brotherhood.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i reasoning: marriage does not create legal kinship, and it can cause enmity or bias; therefore it is like brotherhood where testimony is rejected.
They also cite that qisas and debt imprisonment apply between spouses, showing lack of complete unity.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school counters with a textual evidence (hadith) and a rational argument: the benefits between spouses are directly connected, so each is enriched by the other, making testimony akin to partial self-testimony.
The Quranic verse about the Prophet's enrichment through Khadijah supports this.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: in Hanafi fiqh, a spouse can testify for or against the other; in Shafi'i fiqh, such testimony is rejected unless a specific exception applies.
This affects family legal matters where a spouse is the only witness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الزوج والعرسِ خلاف الشَّافِعِيُّ ). (۱) قوله : خلاف الشافعي الله ؛ فيقول : لا قرابة بينهما، والزوجية قد تكون سبباً للتنافر والعدواة، وقد تكون سبباً للميل والإيثار، فصار نظير الأخوة؛ ولهذا يجري القصاص بينهما، والحبس بالدين، ولا اعتبار بالمنفعة الثابتة ضمناً كما في الغريم إذا شهد لمديونه المفلس. ولنا : ما روينا من الحديث، وما بينا في المعنى، وهو أنَّ المنافع بينهما متّصلة ؛ ولهذا يعد أحدهما غنياً لغناء صاحبه ، وقيل : المراد بقوله علا: وَوَجَدَكَ عَائِلًا فَأَغْنَى ) أي بمال خديجة رضي الله تعالى عنها. كذا في «التبيين»(4)، وغيره.
And regarding the husband and wife, there is disagreement with al-Shafi'i. (1) His statement: there is no kinship between them, and marriage may be a cause for estrangement and enmity, and may be a cause for inclination and preference, so it became analogous to brotherhood; for this reason, retribution can be carried out between them, and imprisonment for debt, and no consideration is given to the implicitly established benefit as in the case of the creditor if he testifies for his insolvent debtor. And our evidence is what we narrated from the hadith, and what we explained in the meaning: that the benefits between them are connected; hence, one is considered wealthy due to the wealth of the other. And it is said that the intended meaning of His statement, Most High: 'And He found you needy and enriched you' is with the wealth of Khadijah, may Allah be pleased with her. Thus in al-Tabyin and others.
Source pages p. 566

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the acceptance of testimony between spouses. The Shafi'i school rejects it, analogizing marriage to brotherhood, where testimony is also rejected. They argue marriage can cause either enmity or undue bias. They also note that qisas and debt imprisonment valid between spouses, and indirect benefit (as in creditor-testimony for debtor) is not considered. The Hanafi school counters with a narrated hadith (indicating acceptance) and a rational argument: the benefits between spouses are directly connected (each's wealth benefits the other), as evidenced by the Quranic verse about the Prophet's enrichment through Khadijah's wealth. This interconnectedness makes spousal testimony akin to testifying for oneself, which is invalid only if completely identical, but here it is partially so, thus accepted. The relied-upon Hanafi position is that spousal testimony is accepted.

Key Terms
الزوج والعرس (al-zawj wa al-urs): Husband and wife (spouses) in the context of testimony
الغريم (al-gharim): Creditor; in the example, a creditor testifying for his debtor

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school accept testimony between spouses, according to the passage?
A) Because the Quran explicitly commands it
B) Because their benefits are mutually connected, and each is enriched by the other
C) Because there is no possibility of enmity between spouses
D) Because the hadith states that spouses are like one person

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts spousal testimony based on textual evidence and the rational argument of connected benefits, unlike the Shafi'i school.

The Testimony of an Upright Enemy
Ruling from al-Qunya on accepting enemy testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting testimony from an enemy and identify the condition that makes it valid.

Core Idea
An enemy's testimony is accepted if the witness is upright, as uprightness overrides the suspicion of bias.

The Hanafi school of law recognizes that enmity between parties can create suspicion (tuhma) that might affect a witness's credibility. However, the school balances this concern with the principle that a witness's uprightness (adala) is the primary criterion. In this lesson from al-Qunya, we examine the ruling that an upright enemy's testimony is accepted absolutely, with a special note on when the testimony is for or against the enemy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The act is shahadah (testimony) given by an 'aduw (enemy). The hukm (ruling) is that it is accepted (yuqbal) if the witness is 'adl (upright).
The ruling is not unconditional; it depends on the witness's uprightness.
2
Locate the condition
The only condition mentioned is that the witness must be 'adl. Uprightness overcomes the potential tuhma (suspicion) arising from enmity.
Other schools may have additional conditions; here it is simply 'adala.
3
Distinguish the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between testifying 'ala al-'aduw (against the enemy) and testifying lahu (for the enemy). In the first case, it is accepted according to the correct view (al-sahih). In the second case, it is accepted by agreement (ittifaqan) because there is no tuhma.
The absence of tuhma in the second case makes it even more clear.
4
Determine the authority weight
The author of al-Qunya attributes this to his teacher and states 'alayhi al-i'timad (upon it is reliance). This is from later fatawa (fatawa mutakhkhirin), indicating the mufta bihi position.
Not from Zahir al-Riwayah but relied upon.
5
Understand the cross-madhhab difference
Some schools may reject enemy testimony unconditionally. The Hanafi position is conditional on uprightness, balancing between the need for testimony and caution against bias.
This is an area of ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي «القُنية : قال أستاذنا : الصحيحُ أنَّ العدو إذا كان عدلاً يقبل شهادته مطلقاً، وعليه الاعتماد، وهذا إذا شهد العدوّ على العدوّ ، فإن شهد له ينبغي أن تقبل اتفاقا ; لعدم التهمة حينئذ. انتهى.
And in al-Qunya: Our teacher said: The correct view is that the enemy, if he is upright, his testimony is accepted absolutely, and upon it is reliance. And this is if the enemy testifies against the enemy; if he testifies for him, it should be accepted by agreement, due to the absence of accusation at that time. End.
Source pages p. 566

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the book al-Qunya, clarifying the acceptance of testimony from an enemy. The key condition is that the witness ('aduw) must be 'adl (upright). If he is upright, his testimony is accepted whether for or against the enemy, but with a nuance: when testifying against the enemy, there is potential accusation (tuhma) of hostility, yet it is still considered acceptable absolutely in the correct view. When testifying for the enemy, it is accepted by consensus because there is no tuhma. This ruling is relied upon ('alayhi al-i'timad). The Hanafi school generally treats enmity as a potential cause for rejection but allows it if the witness is upright, balancing between need for testimony and caution against bias.

Key Terms
العدو (al-aduw): Enemy; someone with whom the witness has hostility
التهمة (al-tuhma): Accusation or suspicion that the witness might lie due to bias

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When he testifies against the enemy
B) When he testifies for the enemy
C) In all cases if he is upright
D) Only if the enemy is a criminal

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an enemy's testimony is accepted if the witness is upright, with absolute acceptance when testifying for the enemy due to absence of suspicion.

Master's Testimony for His Slave
Explaining the rejection of a master's testimony for his slave with proofs and an exceptional scenario.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a master's testimony for his slave, explain the principle of self-testimony, and identify the exceptional case where it may be accepted.

Core Idea
A master's testimony for his slave is rejected as self-testimony, except when the slave's debt leads to his sale to creditors.

This lesson examines a specific rule in the chapter of testimony (shahadat): a master may not testify on behalf of his slave. The reason is rooted in the principle that one cannot testify for oneself. However, the ruling is nuanced when the slave carries debt. We will explore the proof and the logical basis for this position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its context
The passage states that the master's testimony for his slave is not accepted. Recognise that this is part of the broader principle that a witness must not have a personal interest in the case.
2
Understand the proof (dalil)
Two proofs are given: a hadith (narrated) and a rational argument. The hadith is mentioned but not quoted; it establishes the general prohibition of self-serving testimony. The rational argument is that the slave and his property belong to the master, so testifying for the slave is testifying for oneself.
The hadith is not specified, indicating its well-known nature among scholars.
3
Analyse the exception for the indebted slave
If the slave has debt, his status is uncertain: creditors may sell him to recover their dues, making him a stranger to the master. In that scenario, the master's testimony may be accepted after the sale because the personal interest is removed.
The acceptance is conditional upon the slave actually becoming a stranger through sale.
4
Determine the practical application
In a real case, a judge would first check whether the slave has debts. If no debt, the testimony is rejected outright. If debt exists, the testimony is suspended until the uncertainty resolves—either the debt is paid (slave remains owned) or the slave is sold (slave becomes stranger, testimony accepted).
The ruling is built on the concept of 'testimony for oneself' which the Hanafis treat as a preventer (mani') of acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٢) قوله : وسيد لعبده ومكاتبه ; لما روينا من الحديث ; ولأنَّ شهادة السيد لعبده شهادة لنفسه من كل وجه ; لأنَّ العبد وما يملكه لمولاه ، وهذا إذا لم يكن على العبد دين، أو من وجه إذا كان عليه دين ; لأنَّ حالَ العبد دائر بين أن يصير العبد للغرماء بسبب بيعهم إياه في دينهم، فيصيرُ أجنبياً فتقبل شهادة السيد له، أو يبقى للمولى كما كان بسبب قضاء دينه. وشهادة السيد لمكاتبه أيضاً شهادة لنفسه ; لكون حال العبدِ موقوفاً مراعى ، فإنَّ المكاتب إن أدّى بدل الكتابة صار أجنبياً ، وإلا عاد رقيقاً، فتكون شهادة لنفسه.
(2) His statement: And a master (testifying) for his slave and his mukatab; due to what we narrated from the hadith, and because the testimony of a master for his slave is testimony for himself from every aspect, because the slave and what he owns belong to his master. And this is if there is no debt on the slave; or from one aspect if there is debt on him, because the state of the slave oscillates between the slave becoming for the creditors due to their selling him for their debt, so he becomes a stranger and the master's testimony for him is accepted, or he remains for the master as he was due to paying off his debt. And the testimony of the master for his mukatab is also testimony for himself, because the state of the slave is suspended and considered, for the mukatab: if he pays the kitaba price he becomes a stranger, otherwise he returns to slavery, so it becomes testimony for himself.
Source pages p. 566

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding a master testifying for his slave or mukatab. The ruling: such testimony is not accepted. The proof is a hadith (narrated) and a rational argument: the master's testimony for his slave is essentially testimony for himself because the slave and his property belong to the master. Exception: if the slave has debt, the master may become a stranger when the creditors sell the slave, then the testimony might be accepted. For the mukatab (a slave with a contract of manumission), the master's testimony is also rejected because the mukatab's status is suspended—if he pays, he becomes free (a stranger); if not, he remains a slave. Thus the testimony is either for oneself (if slave) or potentially for oneself (if mukatab becomes slave again). The Hanafi school consistently rejects such self-interested testimony.

Key Terms
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has contracted to purchase his freedom by paying installments to his master
الغرماء (al-ghurama'): Creditors; if the slave has debts, they can sell him to recover debts, making him a stranger to the master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Always, because the master owns the slave
B) When there is no debt on the slave
C) When the slave has debt and the creditors sell him, making him a stranger
D) Only if the master is also a creditor

Key Takeaway
A master's testimony for his slave is rejected due to self-interest, but an exception exists when the slave's debt leads to his sale to creditors.

Master's Testimony for a Mukatab
Explaining why a master's testimony for a mukatab is rejected.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a master's testimony for his mukatab and explain why it is treated as testimony for oneself due to the mukatab's suspended status.

Core Idea
A master's testimony for his mukatab is rejected because the mukatab's status is suspended—if he fails to pay, he reverts to being a slave.

This lesson extends the principle of self-testimony to the mukatab, a slave under contract for manumission. The Hanafi school considers that the master's testimony for a mukatab is also testimony for himself because the mukatab's freedom is not yet absolute. The ruling is straightforward, with no exception comparable to the indebted slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for the mukatab
The passage states that the master's testimony for his mukatab is also testimony for himself. Recognise that this is an extension of the same principle applied to the full slave.
2
Understand the basis: suspended status
The mukatab's status is 'muwquf' (suspended). If he pays the kitaba price, he becomes free (a stranger); if he fails, he remains a slave. Therefore, at the time of testimony, there is a possibility that he may return to slavery, making the testimony potentially for oneself.
Unlike the slave with debt, there is no exception for the mukatab because the uncertainty is always present.
3
Contrast with the slave case
For a full slave, an exception exists when debt leads to sale. For a mukatab, no such exception is mentioned because his status is always tied to payment of the kitaba, which is not a debt to creditors but a contractual obligation to the master. Thus the master's interest persists.
This shows the Hanafi attention to the nature of the relationship.
4
Apply the ruling
In practice, a judge would always reject a master's testimony for his mukatab, regardless of any external factors, because the master stands to benefit from the mukatab's failure to pay.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(2) His statement: And a master (testifying) for his slave and his mukatab; due to what we narrated from the hadith, and because the testimony of a master for his slave is testimony for himself from every aspect, because the slave and what he owns belong to his master. And this is if there is no debt on the slave; or from one aspect if there is debt on him, because the state of the slave oscillates between the slave becoming for the creditors due to their selling him for their debt, so he becomes a stranger and the master's testimony for him is accepted, or he remains for the master as he was due to paying off his debt. And the testimony of the master for his mukatab is also testimony for himself, because the state of the slave is suspended and considered, for the mukatab: if he pays the kitaba price he becomes a stranger, otherwise he returns to slavery, so it becomes testimony for himself.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding a master testifying for his slave or mukatab. The ruling: such testimony is not accepted. Thus the testimony is either for oneself (if slave) or potentially for oneself (if mukatab becomes slave again). The Hanafi school consistently rejects such self-interested testimony.

Key Terms
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has contracted to purchase his freedom by paying installments to his master
الغرماء (al-ghurama'): Creditors; if the slave has debts, they can sell him to recover debts, making him a stranger to the master.

Testimony of Partners for Each Other
Ruling that partners cannot testify for each other in shared matters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on partners testifying for each other and apply the principle of indivisibility of testimony (ghayr mutajazzi'a) to identify when such testimony is accepted.

Core Idea
A partner's testimony for his partner in shared property is invalid; testimony is indivisible, so partial self-interest spoils the whole.

This passage from Sharh al-Wiqayah addresses a specific question in the chapter on testimony: can one partner testify for the other? The answer depends on what the testimony is about. The key principle is that testimony is a single indivisible act; if any part of it is tainted by self-interest, the entire testimony is rejected. This lesson will walk you through the ruling, its reasoning, and its scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
First, locate the core ruling: 'the testimony of one partner for the other in what they share is not accepted.' Note the phrasing 'fima yashtarikan' (in what they share) – this is the key qualifier.
2
Understand the Reasoning
The text explains: because the testimony is 'for himself from one aspect' – each partner has a share in the property, so testifying about it is testifying about your own property partially. That partial self-interest makes the testimony invalid.
3
Grasp Indivisibility (Ghayr Mutajazzi'a)
Hanafi fiqh treats testimony as one unified declaration. You cannot split it into valid and invalid parts. If part is tainted, the whole is tainted. This is a key principle in shahada.
4
Recognize the Exception
The text adds: this ruling applies only to shared property. For matters outside the partnership (ghayr mal al-sharika), the partner's testimony is accepted. So the bar is not absolute.
5
Extend to Analogous Cases
The passage ends by comparing a partner to an ajir (employee). The same reasoning applies: an employee cannot testify for the employer in matters involving his own wages or duties. This shows the principle of partial interest extends beyond partnerships.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۳) قوله : وشريكه...الخ ; أي ولا تقبل شهادة أحد الشريكين للآخر فيما يشتركانه ; لكون هذه الشهادة شهادة لنفسه من وجه; لاشتراكهما فيه; لأنه يصير شاهداً لنفسه في البعض، وذلك باطل، وبطلان البعض يستلزم بطلان الكل; لكونها غير متجزئة، فإنّها شهادة واحدة. إنما قال هذا ; لأنه تقبلُ للشَّرِيكِ في غير مال الشركة، وكذا لا تقبل شهادة الأجير.
(3) His statement: And his partner... etc.; i.e., the testimony of one partner for the other in what they share is not accepted; because this testimony is testimony for himself from one aspect, due to their sharing in it; for he becomes a witness for himself in part, and that is invalid, and the invalidity of the part necessitates the invalidity of the whole, because it is indivisible; indeed it is one testimony. He only said this because it is accepted from the partner in non-partnership property, and similarly the testimony of the ajir is not accepted.
Source pages p. 566

Commentary
This is a ruling statement concerning testimony between partners. The rule: partners cannot testify for each other in matters related to their shared property (al-mal al-mushtarak). The reasoning is that such testimony is partially for oneself (since each partner has a share in the property). Testimony is indivisible; if part is invalid, the whole is invalid. However, the testimony is accepted for matters outside the partnership. The text also mentions that the same applies to an ajir (employee) but does not expand here. The distinction is crucial: partnership does not create total bar; only when the testimony directly involves the shared interest.

Key Terms
الشريك (al-sharik): Partner in a business or property
غير متجزئة (ghayr mutajazzi'a): Indivisible; testimony cannot be split; if partially invalid, whole is invalid

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the testimony of a partner for his partner in shared property rejected?
A) Because partners are considered enemies in Hanafi law
B) Because the testimony is partially for himself, and testimony is indivisible
C) Because the partner is not a reliable witness by default
D) Because the testimony might encourage illegal partnerships

Key Takeaway
A partner's testimony for his partner is invalid only when it concerns their shared property, due to the indivisible nature of testimony making partial self-interest fatal to the whole.

The Ajir in Testimony: Definition and Rationale
Definition and rationale for rejection of ajir's testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two interpretations of 'ajir' in the context of rejected testimony and explain the rationale for rejecting the testimony of an exclusive hire.

Core Idea
An employee whose entire labor belongs to the employer cannot testify for him; it is like testifying for oneself.

The term 'ajir' appears in the rule that an ajir's testimony for his employer is rejected. But who exactly is an 'ajir'? This definitional passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies that the rule applies specifically to a 'special student' or a yearly/monthly hiree, not every worker. Understanding the rationale—complete economic dependency—helps you apply the rule correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term under definition
The passage defines 'ajir' as used in the context of rejected testimony (shahadat). Note that the rule parallels the rejection of a partner's testimony for his partnership.
The term appears in the broader principle: a person whose benefit or harm is tied to another cannot testify for that other.
2
State the two interpretations
Two interpretations are given: (1) the special student (al-talamidh al-khass) who is completely dependent on his teacher; (2) the employee hired by year or month (ajir musanahan aw mushaaharan).
Both share the feature of complete economic dependency during the period.
3
Analyze the rationale for the employee
Focus on the second interpretation. The passage explains that all the employee's labor during the hire period belongs to the employer. Therefore, testifying for the employer is like testifying for himself because his labor (including testimony if it is part of his work) is owed to the employer, and he is receiving wages for it.
The rationale is that the employee is effectively testifying for his own benefit (the wages he earns).
4
Understand the connection between the two interpretations
Both the special student and the exclusive hiree are in a position of complete economic dependency. The passage clarifies that 'ajir' means this specific kind of dependent worker, not a general independent contractor.
This refines the rule: not every hired worker is barred from testifying; only those whose entire work belongs to the employer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وقيل : يراد به التلميذ الخاص الذي يعد ضرر أستاذه ضرر نفسيه ، ونفعه نفع نفسه . وقيل : يراد به الأجير مسانهة أو مشاهرة. قوله : الأجير مسانهة أو مشاهرة ; فيستوجب الأجير الأجر على المستأجرِ بمنافع نفسه ; لأنَّ عمل الأجير كله في مدة الإجارة للمستأجر، فصار الأجير حينئذ بالشهادة للمستأجر مستوجب الأجر عليه، فصار الأجير بمنزلة المستأجر على الشهادة ; لأنَّ الشهادة عمل من أعماله، فجميع أعماله مستحقة للمستأجر في المدة. كذا في «النهاية».
His statement: And it is said: It means the special student who considers the harm to his teacher as harm to himself, and his benefit as benefit to himself. And it is said: It means the employee who is hired by year or by month. His statement: The employee hired by year or by month: then the employee deserves the wage from the employer for his own services; because all the work of the employee during the period of hire belongs to the employer, so the employee then, by testifying for the employer, becomes deserving of the wage from him, so the employee becomes like the employer regarding the testimony, because testimony is one of his works, and all his works are owed to the employer during the period. Thus in al-Nihayah.
Source pages p. 567

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'ajir' as it appears in the context of rejected testimony (like the partner's testimony). Two interpretations are given: first, the 'special student' (talamidh khass) who lives with and is dependent on his teacher, such that the teacher's harm or benefit is his own. Second, a hired employee by year or month (ajir masanahan aw mushaaharan). The commentary then explains the reasoning for the employee: during the hire period, all his labor (including testimony if it is part of his work) belongs to the employer. Testifying for the employer would be like testifying for himself because he is owed wages. Therefore, such testimony is rejected. This clarifies that the bar on testimony extends to those in a position of complete economic dependency.

Key Terms
الأجير (al-ajir): A hired employee, specifically hired by year or month, whose labor belongs to the employer during the hire period.
التلميذ الخاص (al-talamidh al-khass): A special student who lives with his teacher and is economically dependent on him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the employee's work, including testimony, belongs to the employer, so testifying becomes self-testimony for wages
C) Because the employer might force the employee to lie
D) Because the employee is considered a member of the household

Key Takeaway
An 'ajir' whose testimony is rejected is an exclusive hiree (or fully dependent student), not every worker; the rationale is complete economic dependency and self-interest.

Definition of Mukhanath: Effeminate Man in Testimony
Clarifying the term 'mukhanath' as related to witness testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct pronunciation and definition of the term 'mukhanath' and explain its relevance to witness testimony in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
The mukhanath is an effeminate man; his testimony hinges on his uprightness, not his effeminacy.

In Hanafi fiqh, the term 'mukhanath' appears in discussions of witness testimony. Understanding its precise meaning and pronunciation is essential for correctly applying the ruling. This lesson clarifies the definition and notes that the testimony of a mukhanath is accepted if he is a reliable witness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its pronunciation
Locate the term 'mukhanath' in the source and note the vowel on the nun. The passage states it is commonly pronounced with a fatha (mukhanath) as the passive participle.
Some scholars mention a variant with kasra (mukhanith) as the active participle.
2
Understand the definition
A mukhanath is an effeminate man who imitates women in speech and mannerisms. It is not a hermaphrodite (khuntha).
The term is a legal qualifier, not a medical condition.
3
Connect to the legal context
In Hanafi testimony law, the ruling depends on the witness's 'adalah (uprightness), not merely the label. If his effeminacy does not render him sinful, his testimony is accepted.
The passage sets the stage for this ruling by defining the term.

The definition is necessary to understand which individuals are being discussed in the legal discourse.

Rejection of Testimony from Specific Individuals: The Hanafi Position
Rulings on non-acceptance of testimony from specific individuals with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on the rejection of testimony from hired workers, effeminate men, wailing women, singing women, and habitual drinkers, and cite the hadith evidence for the wailing and singing women.

Core Idea
Testimony is rejected from those whose partiality, fisq, or imitative behavior undermines their integrity.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah addresses the conditions for accepting testimony in court. The Hanafi school specifies that certain individuals are disqualified due to either a conflict of interest, engaging in forbidden acts, or lacking moral integrity. The text explains the rationale behind each category, providing proofs from the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories
List all the individuals whose testimony is rejected according to this passage: partner (in partnership wealth), hired worker (ajir), effeminate man (mukhanath who does reprehensible acts), wailing woman (na'iha), singing woman (muganni), habitual drinker (mudmin al-shurb).
2
Understand the underlying cause for each
For each category, determine why their testimony is rejected: partiality (partner, hired worker, special student), fisq (wailing, singing, habitual drinking), or reprehensible imitation (effeminate man).
The cause determines whether the rejection is absolute or conditional.
3
Analyse the proof for wailing and singing women
The hadith 'The Prophet forbade the two foolish voices: the wailing woman and the singing woman' is cited. This indicates that these actions are forbidden and render the doer fasiq, thus testimony is rejected.
The hadith is from Sunan al-Tirmidhi; its transmission is ahad and its meaning is zanni.
4
Distinguish the rulings on the effeminate man
The text explains two meanings: a man who intentionally imitates women (rejected) and a man who merely has a soft nature without choice (accepted). The key is the active commission of reprehensible acts.
This nuance is often misunderstood.
5
Apply the principle of indivisibility of testimony
Testimony is one indivisible act. If part of it is tainted by self-interest (e.g., partner testifying for partnership), the whole testimony is invalid because it is partially for oneself.
This applies especially to the partner and hired worker cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشهادة والرجوع عنها باب القبول وعدمه
ومخنث
إنما قال هذا؛ لأنه تقبل للشريك في غير مال الشركة، وكذا لا تقبل شهادة الأجير، وقيل: يراد به التلميذ الخاص الذي يعد ضرر أستاذه ضرر نفسه، ونفعه نفع نفسه. وقيل: يراد به الأجير مسانهة أو مشاهرة.
(ومخنث)
يشتركانه؛ لكون هذه الشهادة شهادة لنفسه من وجه؛ لاشتراكهما فيه؛ لأنه يصير شاهداً لنفسه في البعض، وذلك باطل، وبطلان البعض يستلزم بطلان الكل؛ لكونها غير متجزئة، فإنها شهادة واحدة.
قوله: وقيل: يراد به؛ أي بالأجير هاهنا، أو في الحديث المذكور سابقاً التلميذ الخاص، وهو الذي يأكل مع الأستاذ. وفي عياله: الذي يعد ضرر أستاذه ضرر نفسه، ونفعه نفع نفسه، وهو معنى قوله لا شهادة للقانع بأهل البيت، رواه الترمذي عن عائشة رضي الله عنها. كذا في «الهداية». والقانع: التابع لأهل البيت؛ كالخادم لهم، وفي «المغرب»: المتعلم الذي يأكل في بيت أستاذه، يكون في معنى القانع المذكور في الحديث. كذا في «البناية».
قوله: الأجير مسانهة أو مشاهرة؛ فيستوجب الأجير الأجر على المستأجر بمنافع نفسه؛ لأن عمل الأجير كله في مدة الإجارة للمستأجر، فصار الأجير حينئذ بالشهادة للمستأجر مستوجب الأجر عليه، فصار الأجير بمنزلة المستأجر على الشهادة؛ لأن الشهادة عمل من أعماله، فجميع أعماله مستحقة للمستأجر في المدة. كذا في «النهاية».
قوله: ومخنث؛ بفتح النون، على صيغة اسم المفعول كما هو المشهور، قال في «البحر» نقلاً عن «فتح الباري» من «أبواب الإمامة»: المخنث بكسر النون وفتحها، فإن كان الأول فهو بمعنى: المتكسر في أعضائه، المتلين في كلامه، تشبهاً بالنساء، وإن كان الثاني فهو الذي يعمل به اللواطة. انتهى.
قوله: يفعل الرديء؛ من أفعال النساء من التزين بزينتهن، والتشبه بهن في الفعل والقول، فالفعل مثل كونه محلاً للواطة، والقول: مثل تليين كلامه باختياره تشبهاً بالنساء، هكذا قالوا، وجعل بعضهم الواو في قوله: والقول بمعنى: أو؛ فأحدهما كاف؛ لأن التشبه بقولهن حرام للرجال كما صرحوا به.
قوله: غير مانع للقبول؛ لأنه لا صنيع فيهما، فلا ينقدح العدل فتقبل الشهادة منه.
قوله: ونائحة ومغنية؛ أي ولا تقبل الشهادة من نائحة ومغنية، فإنه: «نهى عن الصوتين الأحمقين»: النائحة والمغنية، أخرجه الترمذي في (الجنائز)، فهما يرتكبان محرماً، فصارتا فاسقتين.
قال في «المغرب»: ناحت المرأة على الميت: إذا ندبته، وذلك أن تبكي عليه وتعدد محاسنه، والنياحة: اسم. انتهى.
وفي «القاموس»: ناح الرجل: بكى واستبكى غيره. انتهى.
والمراد بالنائحة: هي التي تنوح في مصيبة غيرها؛ لأنها ترتكب المحظورات؛ لأجل الطمع في المال، وتجعله حرفة، أما التي تنوح في مصيبتها فلا تسقط عدالتها، هكذا قالوا.
والتغني باللهو معصية في جميع الأديان، خصوصاً إن كان الغناء من المرأة، فإن نفس رفع الصوت منها حرام، فضلاً عن ضم الغناء إليه، ولهذا لم يقيد هاهنا بقوله: للناس، وقيد به فيما ذكر بعد هذا في غناء الرجل.
قوله: ومدمن الشرب على اللهو؛ أي ولا تقبل الشهادة من مدمن الشرب
Book of Testimony and Its Retraction, Chapter of Acceptance and Non-Acceptance. And the effeminate man. He only said this because testimony for the partner in other than the partnership wealth is accepted, and likewise the testimony of the hired worker is not accepted. And it is said: what is meant by it is the special student who considers his teacher's harm as his own harm and his benefit as his own benefit. And it is said: what is meant by it is the hired worker by year or month. (And the effeminate man) they share it because this testimony is testimony for himself in one respect due to the partnership, so he becomes a witness for himself partially, and that is invalid, and the invalidity of part necessitates the invalidity of the whole because it is indivisible, for it is one testimony. His statement: 'And it is said: what is meant by it' i.e., by 'the hired worker' here, or in the previously mentioned hadith, is the special student who eats with the teacher, and in his household it is he who considers his teacher's harm as his own harm and his benefit as his own benefit, and that is the meaning of his statement: 'No testimony for the qani' (dependent) among the household members,' reported by al-Tirmidhi from Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her. Thus in al-Hidayah. Al-Qani' is the follower of the household, like the servant for them. In al-Mughrib: the student who eats in his teacher's house is in the sense of the qani' mentioned in the hadith. Thus in al-Binayah. His statement: 'the hired worker by year or month' means the hired worker becomes entitled to wages from the employer for his own benefits, because all of the hired worker's work during the lease period belongs to the employer, so the hired worker then, by testifying for the employer, becomes entitled to wages from him, so the hired worker becomes like the employer regarding the testimony because testimony is one of his actions, so all his actions are due to the employer during the period. Thus in al-Nihayah. His statement: 'And the effeminate man' with fat-ha on the nun, in the form of the passive participle as is well-known. He said in al-Bahr quoting from Fath al-Bari, from the chapters on Imamate: al-mukhanath with kasr the nun and fat-ha: if the first, it means one whose limbs are relaxed and his speech is soft imitating women; and if the second, it means the one with whom sodomy is committed. End. His statement: 'he does what is reprehensible' from the actions of women: adorning with their adornment and imitating them in action and speech. Action such as being a receptacle for sodomy, and speech such as softening his speech by choice imitating women. Thus they said, and some of them made the waw in the phrase 'and speech' to mean 'or,' so one of them is sufficient because imitating their speech is forbidden for men as they explicitly stated. His statement: 'is not a barrier to acceptance' because there is no action on his part, so integrity is not impaired, hence testimony from him is accepted. His statement: 'And a wailing woman and a singing woman' meaning testimony is not accepted from a wailing woman and a singing woman, for indeed he forbade the two foolish sounds: the wailing woman and the singing woman, reported by al-Tirmidhi in (Funerals); so they commit a forbidden act, thus they become sinful. He said in al-Mughrib: naahat al-mar'atu 'ala al-mayyit: if she eulogized him, that is she wept over him and enumerated his virtues. Al-niyaha is a noun. End. In al-Qamus: naha al-rajul: he wept and made others weep. End. What is meant by the wailing woman is she who wails over a calamity of another, because she commits forbidden things for the sake of greed for money and makes it a profession. As for she who wails over her own calamity, her integrity is not dropped. Thus they said. Singing with amusement is a sin in all religions, especially if the singing is from a woman, for the very raising of her voice is forbidden, let alone adding singing to it. For this reason it was not restricted here by saying 'for people,' and it was restricted in what will come later regarding the singing of a man. His statement: 'And the habitual drinker on amusement' i.e., testimony is not accepted from the habitual drinker.
Source pages p. 567

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah discusses the conditions for accepting testimony. It begins by explaining that a partner's testimony for the partnership in property other than the partnership's wealth is not accepted because it is like testifying for oneself partially. Similarly, a hired worker's testimony for his employer is not accepted because his work belongs to the employer during the contract period, making him dependent. The text then clarifies the term 'ajir' may refer to a special student or a hired worker by year or month. Then it discusses the mukhanath (effeminate man): his testimony is rejected if he engages in reprehensible actions like imitating women in speech or action, but if he merely lacks ability to have intercourse or has soft speech without choice, it does not prevent acceptance. Next, the testimony of a wailing woman (na'iha) and a singing woman (muganni) is rejected because they commit sins (fisq) by engaging in forbidden acts. The hadith 'The Prophet forbade the two foolish voices: the wailing woman and the singing woman' is cited. Lastly, the testimony of a habitual drinker (mudmin al-shurb) is not accepted because addiction to drinking leads to fisq. The commentary provides detailed reasoning and quotes from various sources like Al-Hidayah, Al-Mughrib, etc.

Key Terms
مخنث (mukhanath): An effeminate man who imitates women in speech and action, or the one with whom sodomy is committed.
نائحة (na'iha): A woman who wails over a deceased person for hire, eulogizing and enumerating virtues.
مغنية (muganni): A singing woman who sings with amusement (lahw).
مدمن الشرب (mudmin al-shurb): One who habitually drinks intoxicants.
قانع (qani'): A dependent member of the household, such as a servant or a student who eats in the teacher's house.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rejects testimony from those whose partiality, sin, or imitative behavior compromises their integrity, with details varying based on the specific circumstance.

8 min
Disqualified Witnesses: Effeminate Men, Wailing Women, and Singing Women
Ruling on testimony of three types of immoral individuals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the testimony of effeminate men, wailing women, and singing women, and identify the evidence that disqualifies them.

Core Idea
Testimony is rejected from those who commit major sins (fisq) such as gender imitation, professional wailing, and singing, because the Prophet prohibited these acts.

The Hanafi school establishes that a witness must be 'adl (upright). Certain actions remove that uprightness, categorizing the person as fasiq. This lesson examines three specific categories from the matn: the effeminate man, the professional wailing woman, and the singing woman. We will see how the commentary defines each and what evidence supports disqualification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories and their common element
Recognize that all three are considered acts of fisq that undermine moral integrity and thus disqualify a witness.
The common element is committing a major sin (fisq).
2
Define each category according to the Hanafi school
Use the commentary definitions: effeminate (imitation in action/speech), wailing (professional lament for money), singing (professional amusement).
For effeminate, action includes being passive in sodomy; speech includes softening voice. Wailing is only for others' calamities; singing is unconditional for women.
3
Cite the evidence for the wailing and singing categories
The hadith from Tirmidhi prohibits the two foolish sounds: the wailing woman and the singing woman, indicating these acts are haram.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning is clear (qati al-dalala).
4
State the ruling on testimony acceptance for each
For effeminate: testimony rejected if imitation is deliberate; for wailing: rejected if professional for others; for singing: rejected unconditionally for women.
Physical conditions (like soft limbs) do not disqualify a man; they are not within his control.
