⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يفعل الرديء، ونائحة، ومغنية. ومدمن الشرب. قوله : يفعل الرديء؛ من أفعال النساء من التزين بزينتهن، والتشبه بهنَّ في الفعل والقول، فالفعل مثل كونُهُ محلاً للواطة، والقول: مثل تليين كلامه باختياره تشبها بالنساء، هكذا قالوا ، وجعل بعضُهم الواو في قوله : والقول بمعنى : أو ؛ فأحدهما كافٍ ؛ لأنَّ التشبه بقولهنّ حرام للرجال كما صرحوا به. قوله : غير مانع للقبول ؛ لأنه لا صنيع فيهما ، فلا ينقدح العدلُ فتقبل الشهادة منه.
قوله : ونائحة ومغنّية ؛ أي ولا تقبل الشهادة من نائحة ومغنية ، فإنه : «نهى عن الصوتين الأحمقين» : النائحة والمغنية ، أخرجه التِّرْمِذِيُّ في (الجنائز)، فهما يرتكبان محرماً ، فصارتا فاسقتين. قال في «المغرب» : ناحت المرأة على الميت : إذا ندبته، وذلك أن تبكي عليه وتعدد محاسنه ، والنياحة : اسم. انتهى. وفي «القاموس» : ناح الرجل : بكى واستبکی غیره. انتهى. والمراد بالنائحة: هي التي تنوحُ في مصيبة غيرها ؛ لأنها ترتكب المحظورات ؛ لأجل الطمع في المال، وتجعله حرفة، أما التي تنوح في مصيبتها فلا تسقط عدالتها ، هكذا قالوا. والتغني باللهو معصية في جميع الأديان، خصوصاً إن كان الغناء من المرأة، فإنَّ نفس رفع الصوت منها حرام، فضلا عن ضم الغناء إليه، ولهذا لم يقيد هاهنا بقوله : للناس، وقيد به فيما ذكر بعد هذا في غناء الرجل.
قوله : ومدمن الشرب على اللهو ؛ أي ولا تقبل الشهادة من مدمن الشرب على طريق اللهو، قال البرْجَنْدِيُّ في «شرح النُّقاية»: في «الصحاح : فلان يدمن كذا ؛ أي يديمه، ورجلٌ مُدمِنُ خمر: أي مداوم شربها. وإنّما شرط الإدمان ليظهر ذلك ، فإِنَّ مَن يَتَّهم بالشرب ولا يظهر ذلك لا يخرج من العدالة. منه ، وإنما تسقط العدالة إذا كان يظهر ذلك أو يخرج سكران يسخر منه الصبيان فلا مروءة لمثله، ولا يبالي من الكذب عادة، كذا قاله الإمامُ السَّرَخْسِي له في «المبسوط». والظاهر أنَّ الإدمان عليه من يفضي إلى إظهاره ، والمفهوم من سائر الفتاوى أنَّ الإدمان شرط ، والإعلان شرط آخر، وقد ذكر في بعض الفتاوى : إنه لا يقبل شهادة مدمن الشرب ومدمن السكر. فقيل : المراد مدمن الشرب في الخمر، ومدمن السكر في سائر الأشربة غير الخمر ؛ لأن المحرّم في سائر الأشربةِ السكر فقط، والمحرَّم في الخمر نفس الشرب. كذا في بعض شروح «الهداية». وفي «الفتاوي المنصورية»: ذكر الخصافُ الله شرب الخمر مطلقاً، والسكر من شرب النبيذ مطلقاً، ومحمد الله شرط في الخمر الإدمان، حتى لو شربها سراً لا يسقط عدالته ، وشرط في شرب النبيذ أن الاعتياد ويظهر للناس، ويسخر منه الصبيان ويلعبون، وهذا هو الصحيح. وفي «الخزانة»: ليس المراد الإدمان في الشرب ؛ لأنه لا يطاق، وإن المراد الإدمان في البينة : أي يشرب ومن نيته أن يشرب بعد ذلك إذا وجده ، أما إذا لم ينو ذلك فهو تائب. ثم قيل : إن اشتراط اللهو في غير الخمر، وأما في الخمر فلا احتياج إلى اللهو، واعترض عليه بأنه إذا شرب الخمر للتداوي ، بأن قال له الأطباء : لا علاج لمرضك إلا الخمر، فحرمتها مختلف فيها، فالأكثرون على أن لا يحل شربُها ، وإذا كانت مختلفاً فيها لا يسقط الشهادة ، فلا بد في الخمر من قيد اللهو أيضاً.
He who does evil actions, and the wailing woman, and the singing woman, and the habitual drinker. His statement: 'he who does evil actions' means from the actions of women: adorning with their adornment and imitating them in action and speech. Action such as being a passive partner in sodomy, and speech such as softening his speech voluntarily in imitation of women. Thus they said. And some of them considered the waw in his statement 'and speech' to mean 'or' so one of them is sufficient because imitating them in speech is haram for men as they explicitly stated. His statement: 'not a bar to acceptance' because there is no action in these two (inability to have intercourse and softness of limbs), so uprightness is not impaired, so testimony from him is accepted. His statement: 'and the wailing woman and singing woman' means testimony is not accepted from a wailing woman and a singing woman, because he prohibited the two foolish sounds: the wailing woman and the singing woman, recorded by al-Tirmidhi in (al-Jana'iz). So they commit a prohibited act and thus become immoral. He said in al-Mughrib: 'The woman wailed over the deceased: if she lamented him, i.e., she cries over him and enumerates his good qualities. And al-Niyaha is a noun.' End. And in al-Qamus: 'The man wailed: he cried and made others cry.' End. And what is meant by the wailing woman is she who wails over a calamity of others, because she commits prohibited things for the desire of wealth and makes it a profession. As for she who wails over her own calamity, her uprightness is not lost. Thus they said. And singing with amusement is a sin in all religions, especially if the singing is from a woman, for indeed raising the voice itself is haram, let alone adding singing to it. For this reason it is not qualified here by his statement 'for people', and it is qualified in what is mentioned later regarding a man's singing. His statement: 'and the habitual drinker for amusement' means testimony is not accepted from one who habitually drinks for the purpose of amusement. Al-Birjandi said in Sharh al-Nuqaya: In al-Sihah: 'So-and-so habitually does such-and-such, i.e., persists in it; a man addicted to wine, i.e., one who persists in drinking it.' And the condition of addiction is only so that it becomes apparent. For one who is accused of drinking and it does not become apparent, he does not leave uprightness. Rather, uprightness is only lost if he makes it apparent or comes out drunk such that boys mock him; there is no chivalry for such a person, and he usually does not care about lying. Thus said Imam al-Sarakhsi in al-Mabsut. And it appears that addiction leads to its appearance, and the understanding from other fatwas is that addiction is a condition and publicity is another condition. And it has been mentioned in some fatwas: 'The testimony of a habitual drinker and a habitual drunkard is not accepted.' It is said: What is meant is habitual drinker of wine, and habitual drunkard of other drinks besides wine, because in other drinks only intoxication is prohibited, while in wine the drinking itself is prohibited. Thus in some commentaries of al-Hidayah. And in al-Fatawa al-Mansuriyya: Al-Khassaf mentioned drinking wine absolutely, and intoxication from drinking nabidh absolutely. And Muhammad conditioned addiction for wine, so that if one drinks it secretly his uprightness is not lost, and conditioned for drinking nabidh that it become habitual and apparent to people, and boys mock him and play, and this is correct. And in al-Khazana: What is meant is not addiction in drinking, because that is not bearable; rather, what is meant is addiction in the (state of) evidence: i.e., he drinks with the intention that he will drink later if he finds it. But if he does not intend that, he is repentant. Then it was said: The condition of amusement is for other than wine, but for wine there is no need for amusement. And it was objected that if one drinks wine for medicinal purposes, because doctors say there is no cure for your illness except wine, its prohibition is disputed, and the majority hold that drinking it is not permissible. And if it is disputed, testimony is not invalidated, so the condition of amusement is also necessary for wine.
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah addresses four categories of people whose testimony is not accepted due to immorality (fisq). It opens with the matn listing these categories: the one who does evil actions (al-radee'), the wailing woman (al-na'iha), the singing woman (al-mughanniya), and the habitual drinker (midman al-shurb). The commentary then explains each in turn.

For the first category, 'he who does evil actions' refers to a man who imitates women in dress, speech, and actions. Two examples are given: in action, allowing himself to be the passive partner in sodomy; in speech, deliberately softening his voice like a woman. Scholars differ on whether both action and speech must be present or if either is sufficient, but the stronger view is that imitating women in speech alone is also haram. The commentary then clarifies that certain physical conditions—like inability to have intercourse or softness of limbs—do not disqualify a witness because these are not actions within his control and do not reflect on his moral uprightness.

The second category is the wailing woman. A hadith from Tirmidhi is cited: 'The Prophet prohibited the two foolish sounds: the wailing woman and the singing woman.' The commentary defines wailing (niyaha) as lamenting the dead, enumerating their good qualities. Importantly, the disqualification applies only to a woman who wails professionally for others' calamities for money, as she commits prohibited acts out of greed. A woman who wails for her own deceased does not lose her uprightness.

The third category is the singing woman. Singing for amusement is a sin universally, and for women it is even more severe because merely raising the voice is haram. Thus, the ruling applies unconditionally for women, while men's singing is addressed separately later.

The fourth category is the habitual drinker. Here the commentary explores various intra-Hanafi positions. The basic condition is addiction (idman) to drinking for amusement. However, scholars disagree on whether addiction alone suffices or whether publicity (i'lan)—drinking openly such that the community knows—is also required. Al-Sarakhsi holds that uprightness is lost only when the drinking becomes apparent or the person is seen drunk and mocked. Later fatwas differentiate between wine and other intoxicants: for wine, the drinking itself is haram, but some condition addiction; for other drinks, only intoxication is prohibited, so conditioning addiction and publicity is stricter. The correct view according to al-Fatawa al-Mansuriyya is that for wine, addiction is sufficient (secret drinking does not disqualify), while for nabidh, habitual open drinking is required. Another opinion from al-Khazana defines addiction as persistent intention to drink whenever available, not necessarily continuous consumption. Finally, the passage discusses whether the condition of amusement applies to wine as well. Some say it does not, but an objection is raised that if wine is drunk for medicinal purposes, its prohibition is disputed, and in disputed matters testimony is not invalidated. Therefore, the condition of amusement is also necessary for wine to ensure the drinker is acting out of mere pleasure.

Key Terms
الرديء (al-radee'): A man who imitates women in dress, speech, or action, especially in soft speech or being a passive partner in sodomy.
نائحة (na'iha): A woman who wails for the deceased professionally for money, enumerating their good qualities.
مغنية (mughenniyah): A woman who sings for amusement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on the testimony of a woman who wails for her own dead relative?
A) Her testimony is rejected.
B) Her testimony is accepted because she is not doing it professionally.
C) It depends on whether she wails loudly.
D) There is a difference of opinion among Hanafi scholars.

Key Takeaway
Deliberate imitation of women, professional wailing, and women's singing are major sins that disqualify a witness's testimony, but wailing for one's own deceased does not.

Shahadat — Testimony
advanced
10 min
Conditions for Disqualification of a Habitual Drinker's Testimony
Intra-Hanafi analysis of conditions for disqualification of a habitual drinker

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a habitual drinker is disqualified from testifying, distinguish between wine and other intoxicants, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A habitual drinker is disqualified only when addiction to drinking for amusement is combined with publicity, but the rules differ for wine and other intoxicants.

The previous lesson covered three clear categories of disqualified witnesses. The fourth category—the habitual drinker (midman al-shurb)—is more nuanced due to intra-Hanafi disagreements. This lesson examines the conditions of addiction (idman) and publicity (i'lan), how they apply differently to wine versus nabidh, and which opinion is relied upon for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the basic ruling: habitual drinker for amusement is disqualified
The matn states: 'and the habitual drinker for amusement' i.e., testimony from such a person is not accepted. The commentary elaborates on what constitutes 'habitual' and 'for amusement'.
The basic ruling is agreed upon within the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the condition of addiction (idman) and what it entails
Addiction means persistence in drinking. Al-Birjandi defines it as continuous consumption. Some scholars (e.g., al-Sarakhsi) require that the addiction becomes apparent (i.e., public knowledge or visible intoxication) for disqualification to take effect.
There is a debate whether hidden addiction suffices.
3
Analyze the additional condition of publicity (i'lan)
Many fatwas require that the drinking be done openly or that the person is seen drunk and mocked by children. This is linked to loss of muru'a (chivalry) and disregard for truthfulness.
Al-Sarakhsi emphasizes that uprightness is lost only when drinking becomes apparent.
4
Distinguish between wine and other intoxicants
For wine, drinking itself is haram; so addiction alone may suffice (Muhammad allows secret drinking to not disqualify). For other intoxicants (e.g., nabidh), only intoxication is haram, so both addiction and publicity are required for disqualification.
Al-Khassaf held that drinking wine absolutely disqualifies, even secretly.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position according to al-Fatawa al-Mansuriyya
The correct view: for wine, addiction is sufficient (secret drinking does not disqualify); for other drinks, habitual open drinking causing mockery is required.
This is the position preferred in later fatwa works.

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah addresses four categories of people whose testimony is not accepted due to immorality (fisq). It opens with the matn listing these categories: the one who does evil actions (al-radee'), the wailing woman (al-na'iha), the singing woman (al-mughanniya), and the habitual drinker (midman al-shurb). The commentary then explains each in turn.

For the first category, 'he who does evil actions' refers to a man who imitates women in dress, speech, and actions. Two examples are given: in action, allowing himself to be the passive partner in sodomy; in speech, deliberately softening his voice like a woman. Scholars differ on whether both action and speech must be present or if either is sufficient, but the stronger view is that imitating women in speech alone is also haram. The commentary then clarifies that certain physical conditions—like inability to have intercourse or softness of limbs—do not disqualify a witness because these are not actions within his control and do not reflect on his moral uprightness.

The second category is the wailing woman. A hadith from Tirmidhi is cited: 'The Prophet prohibited the two foolish sounds: the wailing woman and the singing woman.' The commentary defines wailing (niyaha) as lamenting the dead, enumerating their good qualities. Importantly, the disqualification applies only to a woman who wails professionally for others' calamities for money, as she commits prohibited acts out of greed. A woman who wails for her own deceased does not lose her uprightness.

The third category is the singing woman. Singing for amusement is a sin universally, and for women it is even more severe because merely raising the voice is haram. Thus, the ruling applies unconditionally for women, while men's singing is addressed separately later.

The fourth category is the habitual drinker. Here the commentary explores various intra-Hanafi positions. The basic condition is addiction (idman) to drinking for amusement. However, scholars disagree on whether addiction alone suffices or whether publicity (i'lan)—drinking openly such that the community knows—is also required. Al-Sarakhsi holds that uprightness is lost only when the drinking becomes apparent or the person is seen drunk and mocked. Later fatwas differentiate between wine and other intoxicants: for wine, the drinking itself is haram, but some condition addiction; for other drinks, only intoxication is prohibited, so conditioning addiction and publicity is stricter. The correct view according to al-Fatawa al-Mansuriyya is that for wine, addiction is sufficient (secret drinking does not disqualify), while for nabidh, habitual open drinking is required. Another opinion from al-Khazana defines addiction as persistent intention to drink whenever available, not necessarily continuous consumption. Finally, the passage discusses whether the condition of amusement applies to wine as well. Some say it does not, but an objection is raised that if wine is drunk for medicinal purposes, its prohibition is disputed, and in disputed matters testimony is not invalidated. Therefore, the condition of amusement is also necessary for wine to ensure the drinker is acting out of mere pleasure.

Key Terms
مدمن الشرب (midman al-shurb): A person who habitually drinks intoxicants for amusement.
إدمان (idman): Addiction or persistence in an action, here drinking.
إعلان (i'lan): Publicity or open performance of an act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in this text, what is the ruling on the testimony of a person who drinks wine secretly but habitually?
A) His testimony is rejected because wine drinking is haram.
B) His testimony is accepted because he drinks secretly.
C) It depends on whether he becomes drunk.
D) His testimony is rejected only if he also drinks nabidh.

Key Takeaway
The disqualification of a habitual drinker's testimony depends on whether the drinking is public and habitual, and differs between wine (drinking itself haram) and other intoxicants (only intoxication haram).

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate
8 min
Conditions for Rejecting Testimony Due to Drinking: Idman, Izhar, and Lahw
Determining when habitual drinking disqualifies a witness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for rejecting a witness's testimony due to drinking and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on the necessity of lahw for wine.

Core Idea
Testimony is rejected only when drinking is habitual, public, and for pleasure — not for mere consumption or medical necessity.

This lesson explores the nuanced Hanafi position on when a witness loses moral probity ('adalah) due to drinking intoxicants. The key issue is not the act of drinking itself (which is haram), but whether the drinking demonstrates a lack of dignity and reliability. Hanafi jurists carefully distinguish between private consumption and public, habitual intoxication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central question
What is the effect of drinking on a witness's moral probity? Does any drinking disqualify, or only specific patterns?
The passage answers: only habitual, public, pleasure-driven drinking removes 'adalah.
2
Understand the three key concepts
Idman (habitual drinking with intent to continue), izhar (public display of drinking or drunkenness), and lahw (drinking for pleasure, not medical necessity). These are the conditions for rejection.
The author emphasizes that idman leads to izhar, but some treat them as separate.
3
Differentiate between wine and other intoxicants
Wine (khamr) is prohibited in any amount; other drinks (nabidh) are prohibited only when intoxicating. For wine, the very act of drinking is haram; for others, only drunkenness. This affects the conditions: some scholars require idman for wine, others say any drinking suffices.
The later view requires lahw even for wine, as argued by the author.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on lahw for wine
Some Hanafis (like Khassaf) hold that drinking wine always disqualifies without need for idman or lahw. Others (Muhammad al-Shaybani, the author) require idman and public display, and the author adds lahw even for wine.
The author's argument: if wine is drunk for medical treatment, its prohibition is disputed, so testimony should not be rejected; hence lahw is needed.
5
Synthesize the relied-upon position
The correct view (al-asahh) according to the author: for all intoxicants, testimony is rejected only when idman (habit with intent), izhar (public display), and lahw (pleasure) are present. This is the mufta bihi position.
The author cites al-Bahr for further verification.

(1)
على طريق اللهو، قال البرْجَنْدِيُّ في «شرح النُّقاية»: في «الصحاح : فلان يدمن كذا ؛ أي يديمه، ورجلٌ مُدمِنُ خمر: أي مداوم شربها. وإنّما شرط الإدمان ليظهر ذلك ، فإِنَّ مَن يَتَّهم بالشرب ولا يظهر ذلك لا يخرج من العدالة.
منه ،
وإنما تسقط العدالة إذا كان يظهر ذلك أو يخرج سكران يسخر منه الصبيان فلا مروءة لمثله، ولا يبالي من الكذب عادة، كذا قاله الإمامُ السَّرَخْسِي له في
«المبسوط»(۲)
والظاهر أنَّ الإدمان عليه من يفضي إلى إظهاره ، والمفهوم من سائر الفتاوى أنَّ الإدمان شرط ، والإعلان شرط آخر، وقد ذكر في بعض الفتاوى : إنه لا يقبل شهادة مدمن الشرب ومدمن السكر. فقيل : المراد مدمن الشرب في الخمر، ومدمن السكر في سائر الأشربة غير الخمر ؛ لأن المحرّم في سائر الأشربةِ السكر فقط، والمحرَّم في الخمر نفس الشرب. كذا في بعض
شروح «الهداية». وفي «الفتاوي المنصورية»: ذكر الخصافُ الله شرب الخمر مطلقاً، والسكر من شرب النبيذ مطلقاً، ومحمد الله شرط في الخمر الإدمان، حتى لو شربها سراً لا يسقط عدالته ، وشرط في شرب النبيذ أن الاعتياد ويظهر للناس، ويسخر منه الصبيان ويلعبون، وهذا هو الصحيح. وفي «الخزانة»: ليس المراد الإدمان في الشرب ؛ لأنه لا يطاق، وإن المراد الإدمان في البينة : أي يشرب ومن نيته أن يشرب بعد ذلك إذا وجده ، أما إذا لم ينو ذلك فهو تائب.
ثم قيل : إن اشتراط اللهو في غير الخمر، وأما في الخمر فلا احتياج إلى اللهو، واعترض عليه بأنه إذا شرب الخمر للتداوي ، بأن قال له الأطباء : لا علاج لمرضك إلا الخمر، فحرمتها مختلف فيها، فالأكثرون على أن لا يحل شربُها ، وإذا كانت مختلفاً فيها لا يسقط الشهادة ، فلا بد في الخمر من قيد اللهو أيضاً.

على اللهو : أي شُرْبُ الأشربة المحرمة، فإنَّ الأشربة التي لا تحرم إدمانها لا يسقط الشهادة ما لم تسكر ، بل إدمان السكر يسقط الشَّهادة، وقد ذكر أنَّ المراد من الإدمان الإدمانُ في النِيَّةِ : وهو أن يَشْرَبَ، ويكونُ في عزمه أن يشرب كلما وَجَدَ، قال الإمامُ السَّرَخْسِي : شُرِط مع ذلك أن يُظْهِرَ ذلك للنَّاس، أو يخرجَ سكران فيسخر منه الصبيان (۱) ، حتَّى إن شرب الخمرَ في السِّرِّ لا يُسْقِط عدالته ، وقد ذكر في «الحواشي»: إنَّ هذا في غيرِ الخمر ، أما في الخمرِ فلا يحتاجُ إلى قيدِ اللَّهُو. أقولُ : لا بُدَّ في الخمر من قيدِ الشُّرْب بطريق اللهو أيضاً ، فإن شربها للتداوي بأن قال له الأطباء : لا علاج لمرضك إلا الخمر، فحرمتها مختلف فيها، فلا تسقط الشهادة. وفيه أنه إذا كان للتداوي لم يكن فيه إدمان عادة، فلا حاجة إلى قيد اللهو، فإنَّ الإدمان شرط لأسقاط العدالة على الأصح ، وإن شئت زيادة التحقيق فارجع إلى «البحر»(۲)، فإنك ستجد فيه ما لم تجده في غيره.
[١] قوله : على اللهو ؛ أي لأجل اللهو هو معروف، وأصله ترويح النفس بما لا تقتضيه، والمراد به أن لا يكون للتداوي ، فيدخل في اللهو الشرب للاعتياد، صرّح به العلامة الطحطاوي في حاشيته على الدر المختار»(۳).
[٢]قوله : أي شرب الأشربة...الخ ؛ قال في «الدر المختار: ولا من الشرب لغير الخمر؛ لأنَّ بقطرة منها يرتكب الكبيرة، فترد شهادته. انتهى.
لكنَّ هذا مخالف لما في «الكافي» حيث قال : وإنما شرط الإدمان ليكون ذلك ظاهراً منه، فإنَّ من يشرب الخمر سراً ولا يظهر منه ذلك لا يخرج من أن يكون عدلاً وإن شربها كثيراً، وإنما تسقط عدالته إذا كان ذلك يظهرُ منه، أو يخرج سكران فتلعب به الصبيان، فإنّه لا مروءة لمثله، ولا يحترز عن الكذب عادة.
Book of Testimony and Revocation, Chapter of Acceptance and Non-acceptance. (1) On the way of play/lahw. Al-Burjandi said in Sharh al-Nuqayah: In al-Sihah: 'So-and-so is addicted to such-and-such' i.e., he persists in it. And 'a man addicted to wine' i.e., one who continuously drinks it. And indeed, idman (addiction) is stipulated so that it becomes apparent; for one who is accused of drinking but it does not become apparent does not exit from 'adalah. And 'adalah only falls if that becomes apparent or he comes out drunk, with children mocking him, for there is no muru'ah for someone like him, and he does not care about lying habitually. Thus said Imam al-Sarakhsi in al-Mabsut (2). The apparent is that idman leads to its disclosure, and what is understood from other fatawa is that idman is a condition and public disclosure is another condition. And it has been mentioned in some fatawa: 'The testimony of a habitual drinker and a habitual drunkard is not accepted.' It was said: The meaning is habitual drinker of wine and habitual drunkard of other drinks besides wine, because what is prohibited in other drinks is only intoxication, while what is prohibited in wine is the drinking itself. Thus in some commentaries of al-Hidayah. And in al-Fatawa al-Mansuriyyah: Al-Khassaf said: Drinking wine absolutely (disqualifies), and intoxication from drinking nabidh absolutely; and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) stipulated idman for wine, such that if one drinks it secretly his 'adalah is not lost, and for drinking nabidh he stipulated habit and that it becomes apparent to people, that children mock him and play, and this is the correct view. And in al-Khazanah: The meaning is not idman in drinking because it is unbearable, but rather idman in intention: i.e., he drinks with the intention to drink again if he finds it, but if he does not intend that, he is repentant. Then it was said: The stipulation of lahw is for other than wine; but for wine there is no need for lahw. And it was objected: If he drinks wine for medical treatment, as doctors told him there is no cure for his illness except wine, then its prohibition is disputed; most are of the opinion that its drinking is not permissible, and if it is disputed, testimony is not forfeited; therefore the restriction of lahw is also necessary for wine. On lahw: i.e., drinking prohibited beverages, because beverages whose addiction is not prohibited do not forfeit testimony unless they intoxicate; rather, addiction to intoxication forfeits testimony. And it has been mentioned that the meaning of idman is idman in intention: that he drinks and is determined to drink whenever he finds it. Imam al-Sarakhsi said: Along with that it is stipulated that he makes it apparent to people, or comes out drunk so that children mock him (1), to the extent that if he drinks wine in secret, it does not remove his 'adalah. And it has been mentioned in al-Hawashi: This is for other than wine; as for wine, there is no need for the restriction of lahw. I say: For wine, the restriction of drinking for lahw is also necessary; for if he drinks it for medical treatment as doctors told him there is no cure for his illness except wine, then its prohibition is disputed, so testimony does not fall. And in it (the argument) is that if it is for treatment, there is generally no idman in it, so there is no need for the restriction of lahw; indeed, idman is the condition for removing 'adalah according to the most correct view. And if you want further verification, refer to al-Bahr (2), for you will find in it what you have not found elsewhere. [1] His statement: 'On lahw': i.e., for the sake of lahw, which is known; its origin is relaxation of the soul with what it inclines to; and the intended meaning is that it is not for medical treatment; thus drinking out of habit enters into lahw. This was explicitly stated by al-Allamah al-Tahtawi in his Hashiyah on al-Durr al-Mukhtar (3). [2] His statement: 'i.e., drinking beverages... etc.'; He said in al-Durr al-Mukhtar: 'And (the testimony is not rejected) from drinking other than wine, because with a single drop one commits a major sin, so his testimony is rejected.' End. But this contradicts what is in al-Kafi where he said: 'Idman is only stipulated so that it becomes apparent from him; for one who drinks wine secretly and it does not become apparent from him does not exit from being just even if he drinks it many times. His 'adalah only falls if that becomes apparent from him, or he comes out drunk and children play with him; for there is no muru'ah for someone like him, and he does not guard against lying habitually.'
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hasiyah) discussing the conditions under which a witness's testimony is rejected due to drinking intoxicants. The central issue is what constitutes loss of moral probity ('adalah). The author begins by explaining the phrase 'on the way of lahw' (play/pastime), which is a key qualifier. The early Hanafi authorities, such as Al-Burjandi and Al-Sarakhsi, stipulate that mere drinking is not enough; the drinking must be habitual (idman) and publicly manifest such that the person loses dignity (muru'ah) and becomes someone who habitually lies. Al-Sarakhsi specifically requires that the person either openly drinks or appears drunk in public where children mock him. The passage then presents a distinction between wine (khamr) and other intoxicating drinks (such as nabidh). For wine, the act of drinking itself is prohibited, whereas for other drinks only intoxication is prohibited. Therefore, some scholars argue that habitual intoxication from nabidh is needed, not just drinking. The author quotes various sources: Al-Fatawa al-Mansuriyyah records that Al-Khassaf held that drinking wine absolutely disqualifies, while Muhammad al-Shaybani required idman for wine and both habit and public display for nabidh. The correct view according to that source is Muhammad's. Another view from al-Khazanah reinterprets 'idman' as intention to continue drinking rather than continuous physical drinking. There is a dispute about whether the lahw condition applies to wine. Some say no, because wine's prohibition is absolute. But the author argues that even for wine, lahw is needed because if one drinks for medical treatment (when doctors prescribe it), the ruling is disputed and thus testimony should not be rejected. The author then clarifies that lahw means drinking not for medical treatment; drinking out of habit is included. The footnote from al-Durr al-Mukhtar says that a single drop of wine (or other completely prohibited drinks) is a major sin, so testimony is rejected without need for idman. But the author notes this contradicts al-Kafi, which insists on public manifestation. The relied-upon position is that idman (defined as habitual drinking with intention) and public display are conditions for rejecting testimony, except for wine where some say public display is not needed but the author insists it is. The summary is that testimony is rejected only when the drinking is habitual, public, and for pleasure, not for medical necessity.

Key Terms
الإدمان (al-idman): The habit of drinking intoxicants continuously, or the intention to drink again whenever possible.
اللهو (al-lahw): Drinking for play, pastime, or pleasure, as opposed to medical treatment.
العدالة (al-'adalah): Moral probity required in a witness; loss of 'adalah makes testimony unacceptable.
المروءة (al-muru'ah): Personal dignity and self-respect; its loss indicates lack of care about lying.
الخمر (al-khamr): Wine or any intoxicating beverage made from grapes; its prohibition is absolute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in this passage, what is required for a witness's testimony to be rejected due to drinking wine?
A) Drinking wine once, even secretly.
B) Habitual drinking of wine with public display.
C) Drinking wine for medical purposes.
D) Only intoxication from any drink.

Key Takeaway
Testimony is rejected only when drinking is habitual, public, and for pleasure — not for private, occasional, or medical consumption.

Conditions for Rejecting Testimony Due to Drinking Intoxicants
Explaining the condition 'amusement' for drinking to reject testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which drinking intoxicants leads to rejection of testimony according to the Hanafi school and distinguish between different scholarly positions within the school.

Core Idea
Addiction and public intoxication, not mere drinking, remove moral uprightness ('adalah) for testimony.

This passage delves into a nuanced Hanafi ruling: drinking intoxicants does not automatically disqualify a witness. Instead, the text clarifies that specific conditions—namely addiction (idman) and public display of intoxication—must be met. We will unpack the commentary on the condition 'amusement' (lahw) and explore an intra-madhhab dispute about wine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Determine what the passage is ruling on: when does drinking intoxicants invalidate testimony?
The ruling hinges on conditions, not the act alone.
2
Understand the condition of 'amusement' (lahw)
Analyze what 'lahw' means in this context—drinking for pleasure, not for medical treatment.
The passage states that lahw is required for the ruling.
3
Distinguish types of intoxicants
Note the difference between wine (khamr) and other intoxicants in the text. Some scholars treat wine differently.
The author argues even wine requires lahw.
4
Recognize the role of addiction and public display
Addiction (idman) in intention and public display (or visible intoxication) are key conditions mentioned by Imam al-Sarakhsi.
Secret drinking does not remove 'adalah.
5
Note the intra-madhhab dispute
Contrast the view in al-Hawashi (wine alone suffices) with the author's preferred view (conditions required).
The relied-upon view (al-ashahh) is that addiction is required.

على اللهو : أي شُرْبُ الأشربة المحرمة، فإنَّ الأشربة التي لا تحرم إدمانها لا يسقط الشهادة ما لم تسكر، بل إدمان السكر يسقط الشهادة، وقد ذكر أنَّ المراد من الإدمان الإدمانُ في النِيَّةِ : وهو أن يَشْرَبَ، ويكونُ في عزمه أن يشرب كلما وَجَدَ، قال الإمامُ السَّرَخْسِي : شُرِط مع ذلك أن يُظْهِرَ ذلك للنَّاس، أو يخرجَ سكران فيسخر منه الصبيان (١)، حتَّى إن شرب الخمرَ في السِّرِّ لا يُسْقِط عدالته، وقد ذكر في «الحواشي»: إنَّ هذا في غيرِ الخمر، أما في الخمرِ فلا يحتاجُ إلى قيدِ اللَّهُو. أقولُ : لا بُدَّ في الخمر من قيدِ الشُّرْب بطريق اللهو أيضاً، فإن شربها للتداوي بأن قال له الأطباء : لا علاج لمرضك إلا الخمر، فحرمتها مختلف فيها، فلا تسقط الشهادة. وفيه أنه إذا كان للتداوي لم يكن فيه إدمان عادة، فلا حاجة إلى قيد اللهو، فإنَّ الإدمان شرط لأسقاط العدالة على الأصح، وإن شئت زيادة التحقيق فارجع إلى «البحر» (٢)، فإنك ستجد فيه ما لم تجده في غيره.

[١] قوله : على اللهو ؛ أي لأجل اللهو هو معروف، وأصله ترويح النفس بما لا تقتضيه، والمراد به أن لا يكون للتداوي، فيدخل في اللهو الشرب للاعتياد، صرّح به العلامة الطحطاوي في حاشيته على الدر المختار (٣).

[٢] قوله : أي شرب الأشربة...الخ ؛ قال في «الدر المختار: ولا من الشرب لغير الخمر؛ لأنَّ بقطرة منها يرتكب الكبيرة، فترد شهادته. انتهى.
لكنَّ هذا مخالف لما في «الكافي» حيث قال : وإنما شرط الإدمان ليكون ذلك ظاهراً منه، فإنَّ من يشرب الخمر سراً ولا يظهر منه ذلك لا يخرج من أن يكون عدلاً وإن شربها كثيراً، وإنما تسقط عدالته إذا كان ذلك يظهرُ منه، أو يخرج سكران فتلعب به الصبيان، فإنّه لا مروءة لمثله، ولا يحترز عن الكذب عادة.
Regarding 'amusement': meaning drinking prohibited beverages. Those beverages that do not prohibit addiction (idman) do not cause rejection of testimony unless one becomes intoxicated. Rather, addiction to intoxication causes rejection of testimony. It has been mentioned that the meaning of addiction is addiction in intention: that he drinks and is determined to drink whenever he finds it. Imam al-Sarakhsi said: Along with that, he must display it to people, or come out intoxicated so that children mock him. Thus, if he drinks wine in secret, it does not remove his uprightness. It is mentioned in al-Hawashi: This applies to other than wine. As for wine, there is no need for the condition of amusement. I say: Wine also requires the condition of drinking in the way of amusement. If he drinks for treatment (tadawi) such that doctors say there is no cure for your illness except wine, then its prohibition is disputed, and it does not remove testimony. And it is said that if it is for treatment, there is no addiction typically, so there is no need for the condition of amusement, for addiction is a condition for removal of uprightness according to the correct view. If you wish further verification, refer to al-Bahr (2), for you will find what you did not find elsewhere.

[1] Statement: 'regarding amusement' i.e., for the sake of amusement is known, its origin is refreshing the soul with what it does not require, and the meaning is that it is not for treatment. Thus, drinking out of habit enters into amusement. This is stated by the scholar al-Tahawi in his gloss on al-Durr al-Mukhtar (3).

[2] Statement: 'i.e., drinking beverages...' etc. He said in al-Durr al-Mukhtar: 'And not from drinking other than wine, because from a drop of it one commits a major sin, so his testimony is rejected.' End.
But this contradicts what is in al-Kafi where he said: 'Addiction is conditioned so that it becomes apparent from him, for one who drinks wine in secret and does not show it is not removed from being upright even if he drinks it many times. His uprightness is only removed when it becomes apparent from him, or he comes out intoxicated and children play with him, for there is no chivalry for such a person, and he is not accustomed to guarding against lying.'
Source pages p. 570

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the condition 'amusement' (على اللهو) in the context of testimony. The author explains that drinking intoxicants does not automatically invalidate testimony; rather, it requires addiction (idman) and public display of intoxication. Imam al-Sarakhsi adds that the person must drink openly or become visibly intoxicated. Private drinking, even of wine, does not remove 'adalah. There is a disagreement: some say wine requires no condition of amusement because it is inherently a major sin, but the author argues that even for wine, the condition applies, especially if consumed for medicinal purposes, where the ruling is disputed. The footnotes provide additional sources: al-Durr al-Mukhtar states that even a drop of wine is a major sin, contradicting al-Kafi which upholds the requirement of addiction and public display. The relied-upon view (al-ashahh) is that addiction is a condition for removal of 'adalah.

Key Terms
اللهو (al-lahw): Amusement; here specifically drinking for pleasure rather than for medicinal purposes.
الإدمان (al-idman): Addiction; habitual drinking with intention to drink whenever possible.
العدالة (al-adalah): Moral uprightness required for testimony.
الشهادة (al-shahada): Testimony in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Drinking to intoxication
C) Addiction (idman) and public display
D) Drinking alone

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, drinking intoxicants does not automatically reject testimony; it requires addiction (idman) and public display of intoxication.

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate
8 min
Rejection of Testimony for Frivolous Pastimes: Birds, Tanbur, and Singing
Ruling on rejection of testimony for frivolous pastimes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on testimony acceptance for those who play with birds, play the tanbur, or sing for people, and identify the conditions that preserve or remove uprightness.

Core Idea
Public engagement in vile amusement or excessive singing removes uprightness; private amusement does not.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya examines three specific activities that may lead to rejection of testimony in Hanafi law. The key distinction is between private, non-distracting amusement and public, excessive engagement that indicates heedlessness and sin. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the moral character ('adala) of witnesses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
For each of the three activities (playing with birds, playing tanbur, singing for people), note whether the passage states that testimony is rejected or preserved.
2
Distinguish public vs private
Determine for each activity the condition that separates permissible from impermissible. For singing, the key is 'for the people' vs. private singing. For birds, it is whether he flies them (public) or keeps them for companionship (private).
3
Apply the principle of 'vile' (shani')
Understand that the tanbur is an example; the criterion is any customarily vile amusement. Look for the text's explanation that if the instrument is considered vile among people, testimony is rejected, even if it does not distract from prayers.
Vileness is determined by custom ('urf).
4
Note the exception for singing
The passage explicitly states that singing to dispel loneliness does not remove uprightness. Only singing for people (public performance) is problematic.
5
Recall the addiction condition
The opening part mentions that addiction (idman) to intoxicants removes uprightness. The passage emphasizes that this applies to all intoxicants, not just wine.
This is a separate ruling but reinforces the seriousness of habitual sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشهادة والرجوع عنها باب القبول وعدمه
ومَن يَلْعَبُ بالطيور، أو الطنبور، أو يُغني للنَّاس) : إِنما قال للنَّاسِ : لأنَّ مَن يُغَنِي لدفع الوحشة عن نفسه لا يُسقط العدالة وفي فتاوى قاضي خان (١) : لا تقبل شهادة مدمن الخمر ولا مدمن السكر ؛ لأنه كبيرة، وفي «الذخيرة»: لا يجوز شهادة مدمن الخمر. وفي «النهاية»: الإدمان شرط في الخمر أيضاً في حق سقوط العدالة. انتهى. فهذه نقول صريحة في عدم الفرق في اشتراط الإدمان بين الخمر وغيره، وعليه مشى الشارحُ البارع له ، وفسر الشرب بقوله : أي شرب الاشربة المحرمة.
وإصراره
[١] قوله : ومن يلعب...الخ ؛ أي ولا تقبل الشهادة منه لشدة غفلته، على نوع لهو ؛ ولأنَّ الغالب أن ينظر إلى العورات في السطوح وغيرها، وهو فسق ، أما إذا أمسك الحمام للاستيناس، ولا يطيرُها فلا تزول عدالته ؛ لأن إمساكها في البيوت مباح.
[٢] قوله : والطنبور ؛ أي ولا تقبلُ الشهادة من يلعب بالطنبور؛ لأنه من اللهو، والمراد بالطنبور كل لهو يكون شنيعاً بين الناس ؛ احترازاً عما لم يكن شنيعاً كضرب القصب، فإنه لا يمنع قبولها إلا أن يتفاحش ؛ بأن يرقصون به، فيدخل في حد الكبائر. كذا في «المنح» نقلاً عن «البحر»(۳).
وقال في «المحيط»: الرجلُ يلعب بشيء من الملاهي، وذلك لم يشغله عن الصلاة، ولا عمَّا يلزمُهُ من الفرائض ، ينظر إن كانت مستشنعة بين الناس كالمزامير والطنابير لم تجز شهادته ، وإن لم يكن شنيعاً لا يمنع قبولها، إلا أن يتفاحش بأن ترقص به ، فيدخل في حد المعاصي والكبائر، فتسقط به العدالة. انتهى.
[٣] قوله : أو يغني للناس ؛ أي ولا تقبل الشهادة ممن يغني للناس، اعلم : أولاً : إنَّ التغنية لغة سرور وتفنن ، والتغني كذلك. كذا في «الصراح»، ومجرده : الغنا، بمعنى سرور، وهو عرفاً : ترديد الصوت بالألحان في الشعر مع انضمام التصفيق
The book of testimony and retraction from it, chapter on acceptance and rejection.
And whoever plays with birds, or the tanbur, or sings for the people. He only said 'for the people' because one who sings to dispel loneliness from himself does not remove uprightness. And in Fatawa Qadi Khan (1): The testimony of an addict of wine nor an addict of intoxication is not accepted, because it is a major sin. And in al-Dhakhira: The testimony of an addict of wine is not permissible. And in al-Nihaya: Addiction is a condition in wine as well regarding the removal of uprightness. End. So these are explicit statements that there is no difference in conditioning addiction between wine and other drinks, and upon this the learned commentator proceeded, and he interpreted 'drinking' as meaning drinking prohibited beverages. And his insistence...

[1] Statement: 'And whoever plays...' etc. i.e., his testimony is not accepted due to his extreme heedlessness, in a type of amusement. And because he often looks at private parts on rooftops and other places, which is sin. But if he keeps pigeons for companionship and does not fly them, his uprightness does not leave because keeping them in houses is permissible.

[2] Statement: 'And the tanbur' i.e., testimony is not accepted from one who plays the tanbur because it is amusement. The meaning of tanbur is every amusement that is considered vile among people. To avoid what is not vile, like playing with reeds (qasab), for it does not prevent acceptance unless it becomes excessive, such that they dance to it, then it enters the category of major sins. So in al-Munha, quoting from al-Bahr (3). And in al-Muhit: A man plays something of musical instruments, and that does not distract him from prayer, nor from obligatory duties. It is considered: if they are considered vile among people like pipes (mazamir) and tanabir, his testimony is not accepted, and if it is not vile, it does not prevent acceptance, unless it becomes excessive such that they dance to it, then it enters the category of sins and major sins, and uprightness is removed. End.

[3] Statement: 'Or sings for the people' i.e., testimony is not accepted from one who sings for the people. Know first that 'taghniya' linguistically means joy and diversification, and 'taghanni' is similar. So in al-Sirah, and its simple form is 'ghina' meaning joy, and customarily it is the repetition of sound with melodies in poetry accompanied by clapping.
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This passage covers three activities that can lead to rejection of testimony: playing with birds, playing the tanbur (and similar vile instruments), and singing for people. For playing with birds, the key is that it causes heedlessness and often leads to looking at private parts; however, keeping birds for companionship without flying them is permissible and does not affect testimony. For the tanbur, it is considered vile amusement; even if it does not distract from prayers, if it is considered vile, testimony is rejected. The same applies to any instrument that is customarily frowned upon. Singing for people is also a cause for rejection, while singing privately to dispel loneliness is fine. The beginning of the passage also reaffirms the ruling on addiction (idman) for drinking, emphasizing that it applies to all intoxicants.

Key Terms
الطنبور (al-tanbur): A musical instrument considered vile; metaphor for any vile amusement.
الغناء (al-ghina'): Singing with melodies and clapping, for people.
الفسق (al-fisq): Sinful behavior; here referring to looking at private parts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following does NOT automatically reject testimony?
A) Playing the tanbur publicly
B) Singing for people
C) Keeping pigeons for companionship without flying them
D) Being an addict of wine

Key Takeaway
Testimony is rejected for public, vile, excessive amusement; private, non-excessive amusement does not affect uprightness.

How Playing with Birds Affects Testimony Acceptance
Explains when playing with birds renders testimony inadmissible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between permissible bird-keeping and sinful amusement, and state the ruling on testimony based on 'adalah.

Core Idea
Testimony is rejected for habitual bird-players due to fisq, but accepted if birds are kept for companionship.

The Hanafi school holds that a witness must possess 'adalah (moral integrity). This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains that playing with birds for amusement leads to heedlessness and often to looking at private parts, which is fisq and destroys 'adalah. However, keeping birds for companionship (isti'nas) without flying them is permissible and does not affect integrity. The key is the intention and consequence of the activity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage addresses two acts: playing with birds for amusement, and keeping birds for companionship. Determine the ruling for each.
Playing for amusement leads to testimony rejection; companionship does not.
2
Understand the basis for the ruling
Why does playing with birds lead to rejection? Two reasons: extreme heedlessness (shiddat al-ghaflah) and the likelihood of looking at private parts (nazar ila al-'awrat), which is fisq.
The proof is rational (dalil 'aqli): the activity typically leads to sin.
3
Define the exception
The exception is when one keeps birds for companionship (isti'nas) and does not fly them. This is permissible (mubah) and does not remove 'adalah.
The distinction is based on intention and actual behavior.
4
Apply to testimony
Since 'adalah is a condition for testimony, losing it through fisq makes testimony inadmissible. Therefore, a habitual bird-player's testimony is rejected, while a companion-keeper's testimony is accepted.
Mufta bihi: testimony rejected for those who play habitually.
5
Note the cross-madhhab dimension
Some schools may not consider such amusement as fisq, so the ruling may differ. This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.
The Hanafi position is based on the typical consequence of the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ومن يلعب...الخ؛ أي ولا تقبل الشهادة منه لشدة غفلته، على نوع لهو؛ ولأن الغالب أن ينظر إلى العورات في السطوح وغيرها، وهو فسق، أما إذا أمسك الحمام للاستيناس، ولا يطيرها فلا تزول عدالته؛ لأن إمساكها في البيوت مباح.
His statement: 'and whoever plays...' etc., meaning: testimony is not accepted from him due to his extreme heedlessness upon a type of amusement; and because the norm is that he looks at private parts on rooftops and elsewhere, and that is fisq. But if he keeps pigeons for companionship and does not fly them, then his integrity does not cease, because keeping them in houses is permissible.
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, explaining the commentary on the matn 'and whoever plays with birds'. The sharh states that such a person's testimony is rejected for two reasons: firstly, due to intense heedlessness from amusement; secondly, because it often leads to looking at people's private parts (e.g., on rooftops), which is a sinful act (fisq). However, an exception is made when one keeps pigeons for companionship (isti'nas) without flying them. This is permissible and does not affect the witness's integrity. The proof is rational: the purpose of keeping birds determines the ruling. The Hanafi position is that amusement leading to neglect of obligations or to sinful acts removes 'adalah.

Key Terms
العدالة (al-'adalah): The moral integrity required in a witness for testimony to be accepted.
الفسق (al-fisq): Sinfulness or transgression that removes 'adalah.
الاستيناس (al-isti'nas): Keeping birds for companionship, as opposed to amusement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on testimony of a person who keeps pigeons in his house without flying them for amusement?
A) His testimony is rejected because keeping birds is always prohibited.
B) His testimony is accepted because keeping them for companionship is permissible.
C) His testimony is rejected because it leads to heedlessness.
D) His testimony is accepted only if he is not a habitual player.

Key Takeaway
Testimony in Hanafi law requires 'adalah; any activity that leads to or constitutes fisq, such as playing with birds for amusement, removes that qualification, but permissible activities like keeping birds for companionship do not.

Testimony of the Tanbur Player: Social Shame as the Criterion
Clarifies when playing the tanbur invalidates testimony based on social perception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi condition of social shamefulness (shan'i) to determine the admissibility of a witness who plays a musical instrument.

Core Idea
Testimony is rejected only when the instrument is considered shameful in society, not for all amusement.

You might assume that playing any musical instrument automatically disqualifies a witness in Islamic law. The Hanafi school, however, makes a careful distinction based on social perception. This lesson unpacks a key passage from later Hanafi works that explains when playing the tanbur—or any similar instrument—affects the admissibility of testimony. You will learn to identify the decisive criterion of shamefulness (shan'i) and how it interacts with the concept of 'adalah (uprightness).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text rules on a person who plays the tanbur. The initial ruling is that his testimony is not accepted. But this is not absolute; it is qualified by whether the amusement is shameful.
Tanbur here represents any stringed instrument; the ruling extends to all musical instruments by analogy.
2
Locate the dalil (proof) and its basis
The proof is not a direct Quranic or hadith text but a rational argument based on 'adalah. The activity, when shameful, indicates a deficiency in religious commitment, thus removing the witness's uprightness.
The text relies on the principle that a witness must be 'adil (just). Shameful amusement contradicts 'adalah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal reasoning)
The key term is 'shan'i' (shameful). The text contrasts the tanbur with harmless activities like beating a reed (qasab). The social judgment of the upright community determines whether the activity is shameful.
If the community considers it shameful, testimony is rejected; otherwise, only if it becomes excessive (e.g., dancing) does it become a major sin.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (difference) and Hanafi distinctiveness
Some scholars may reject testimony for any musical instrument categorically. The Hanafi position distinguishes based on social norms (urf) and the degree of excess.
This reflects the Hanafi methodology of incorporating urf as a subsidiary source of law.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Applying the Hanafi criterion, a flute player whose community does not view the flute as shameful can have his testimony accepted, whereas a player of the same flute in a context where it is considered disgraceful would be rejected.
The ruling depends on time, place, and social standards.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والطنبور؛ أي ولا تقبل الشهادة من يلعب بالطنبور؛ لأنه من اللهو، والمراد بالطنبور كل لهو يكون شنيعاً بين الناس؛ احترازاً عما لم يكن شنيعاً كضرب القصب، فإنه لا يمنع قبولها إلا أن يتفاحش؛ بأن يرقصون به، فيدخل في حد الكبائر. كذا في «المنح» نقلاً عن «البحر». وقال في «المحيط»: الرجل يلعب بشيء من الملاهي، وذلك لم يشغله عن الصلاة، ولا عما يلزمه من الفرائض، ينظر إن كانت مستشنعة بين الناس كالمزامير والطنابير لم تجز شهادته، وإن لم يكن شنيعاً لا يمنع قبولها، إلا أن يتفاحش بأن ترقص به، فيدخل في حد المعاصي والكبائر، فتسقط به العدالة. انتهى.
And the tanbur: that is, testimony is not accepted from one who plays the tanbur, because it is amusement (lahw). By tanbur is meant every amusement that is shameful (shan'i) among people, as a precaution against what is not shameful, like beating reed (qasab), for it does not prevent acceptance unless it becomes excessive, such that they dance with it, then it enters the category of major sins. Thus in al-Minh quoting al-Bahr. And in al-Muhit: A man plays something of musical instruments, and that does not distract him from prayer nor from obligatory duties. Consider: if it is shameful among people like flutes and tanburs, his testimony is not allowed; if it is not shameful, it does not prevent acceptance unless it becomes excessive, such that they dance with it, then it enters the category of sins and major sins, and 'adalah falls thereby. End.
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the tanbur. The ruling: testimony of one who plays the tanbur is not accepted because it is lahw (amusement). The key distinction is whether the activity is considered shameful (shan'i) in the public eye. If the instrument or activity is not shameful (like beating a reed stick without dancing), testimony is accepted unless it becomes excessive (dancing). The proof is rational: such activities, when shameful, indicate a lack of religious commitment and thus remove 'adalah. The text cites two sources: al-Manh and al-Muhit, which provide conditions: even if the activity does not distract from obligations, if it is socially shameful, testimony is rejected; otherwise, only if it escalates to major sin.

Key Terms
الطنبور (al-tanbur): A stringed musical instrument; by extension, any instrument considered shameful in society.
شنيع (shan'i): Shameful, disgraceful in the eyes of upright people.
اللهو (al-lahw): Amusement that distracts from obedience.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text from al-Muhit, when does playing a flute (mizmar) invalidate testimony?
A) Only if it distracts from prayer.
B) Always, because all instruments are haram.
C) If it is considered shameful among people, even if it does not distract from obligations.
D) Only if the person dances with it.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the acceptance of testimony from a player of musical instruments hinges on whether the activity is socially shameful or excessive, not on the mere fact of playing.

Ruling on Testimony of a Singer: The Prohibition of Singing for People
Establishes singing as a major sin and sets strict conditions for leniency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on singing for people, explain its technical definition, and identify the strict conditions for a rare concession.

Core Idea
Singing for people is a major sin; testimony is rejected. Only a rare concession for spiritual masters exists under six strict conditions.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya clarifies the legal status of singing (al-ghina') and its impact on the admissibility of testimony. The Hanafi school holds singing to be a major sin (kabirah) that destroys the moral integrity ('adalah) required for a witness, except under extremely limited circumstances. Understanding this ruling requires grasping both the general prohibition and the exceptional concession granted to advanced spiritual masters (mashayikh) who meet stringent conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Subject
Determine what act is being ruled upon and the immediate legal consequence. The passage begins by stating that testimony is not accepted from one who sings for people.
The ruling directly links the act of singing to the rejection of testimony.
2
Analyze the Definition of Singing
Examine the technical definition given: repetition of voice with melodies in poetry combined with appropriate clapping. Note the three essential elements.
This definition narrows the scope of what counts as prohibited singing.
3
Locate the Proof and General Ruling
Identify the proof used: consensus (ijma) of jurists that singing is haram. Note the emphasis that even idolaters forbid it, reinforcing its universal disapproval.
The passage cites multiple sources (Nisab al-Ihtisab, al-Dhakhirah, etc.) to support the consensus.
4
Examine the Rare Concession
Understand that a limited permission (rukhsa) is granted only for spiritual masters under six specific conditions. List those conditions and note their strictness.
The concession does not apply to ordinary people; it is only for those free from lust, absorbed in remembrance, etc.
5
Determine the Operative Ruling (Mufta Bihi)
Conclude that the general ruling (haram, rejection of testimony) stands for the public. The rare concession does not affect the default prohibition for common Muslims.
The text states that whoever permits singing is a sinner, indicating the rarity of the exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو يغني للناس؛ أي ولا تقبل الشهادة ممن يغني للناس، اعلم: أولاً: إن التغنية لغة سرور وتفنن، والتغني كذلك. كذا في «الصراح»، ومجرده: الغنا، بمعنى سرور، وهو عرفاً: ترديد الصوت بالألحان في الشعر مع انضمام التصفيق المناسب، فلم يتحقق الغناء بفقدان فيه من الثلاثة: كون الألحان في الشعر، وانضمام التصفيق بالألحان، ومناسبة التصفيق. ذكره القهستاني. وثانياً: إنه حرام كما صرح به الفقهاء العظام، قال في «نصاب الاحتساب»: إن التغني والسماع [و] الغناء حرام، اجتمع عليه العلماء وبالغوا فيه، ومن أباحه من المشايخ ممن تخلى عن الهوى وتحلى بالتقوى واحتاج إلى ذلك احتياج المريض إلى الدواء، وعلامته أنه منسل عن الشهوات، مستهو بذكر الله له في الخلوات، مفرغ يديه عن الأخذ والإعطاء مجرد عن الذم والثناء مختلف بالواردات، يريد أن يتنفس بتنفس الصعداء، ويعالج ما غلب عليه تشوقه إلى مولاه من الداء، ثم إنه رخصه، وله شرائط أخذها: الأولى: أنه لا يكون فيهم أمرد. والثانية: أن لا يكون جمعهم إلا من جنسهم ليس فيهم فاسق، ولا أهل الدنيا ولا امرأة. والثالثة: أن تكون نية القوال: الإخلاص لا أخذ الأجرة والإنعام. والرابعة: أن لا يجتمعوا لأجل الطعام، أو نظر إلى فتوح. والخامسة: لا يقومون إلا مغلوبين. والسادسة: لا يظهرون وجداً إلا صادقين. انتهى. وذكر في «الذخيرة»: إنه كبيرة، ومن أباحه من المشايخ، فذلك الذي حركاته حركات المرتعش. انتهى. وذكر في «العوارف»: إنه لا يليق بمنصب المشايخ والذين يقتدى بهم؛ لأنه شأنه اللهو، وأنه يباين حال التمكن. انتهى. وقال القهستاني في «شرح النقاية»: فهو من أنواع اللعب، وكبيرة في جميع الأديان، حتى يمنع المشركون من ذلك. كما في «الاختيار»، وغيره، وفي «المضمرات»: من أباح الغناء يكون فاسقاً.
Or he sings for people: i.e., testimony is not accepted from one who sings for people. Know: first, al-taghniya linguistically means joy and diversification, and al-taghanni likewise. Thus in al-Sirah; its abstract: al-ghina means joy, and customarily: repetition of voice with melodies in poetry combined with appropriate clapping. So singing is not realized with the loss of three things: the melodies being in poetry, the joining of clapping to the melodies, and the appropriateness of the clapping. Mentioned by al-Quhistani. Second, it is haram as the great jurists have stated. He said in Nisab al-Ihtisab: al-taghanni and al-sama' [and] al-ghina are haram, the scholars have agreed upon it and emphasized it, and whoever among the mashayikh permitted it [must be] one who has abandoned desire and adorned himself with piety and needs it like a patient needs medicine, and his sign is that he is free from lusts, absorbed in remembrance of Allah in solitude, his hands empty from taking and giving, free from blame and praise, different in states, wanting to breathe a sigh, and treat the disease of longing for his Master. Then he permitted it, and it has conditions: first, that there be no beardless youth among them. Second, that their gathering be only of their kind, with no sinner, worldly person, or woman. Third, that the singer's intention be sincerity, not taking wages or favor. Fourth, that they do not gather for food or to look at openings. Fifth, that they do not stand up unless overwhelmed. Sixth, that they do not show ecstasy unless truthful. End. And in al-Dhakhirah: it is a major sin, and whoever permits it from the mashayikh, his movements are like those of a trembling person. End. And in al-Awarif: it is not befitting the rank of the mashayikh and those who are followed, because it is amusement and contrary to the state of mastery. End. And al-Quhistani said in Sharh al-Niqayah: it is a type of play, and a major sin in all religions, even idolaters forbid it. As in al-Ikhtiyar and others, and in al-Mudmarat: whoever permits singing is a sinner.
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on singing. The ruling: testimony of one who sings for people is rejected because singing is generally haram and a major sin (kabirah). The passage first defines singing linguistically and technically. Technically, singing involves three elements: melodic recitation of poetry, clapping, and appropriateness of clapping. The proof: consensus of jurists that it is haram. However, a rare concession is granted for advanced spiritual masters under strict conditions: no beardless youths, no sinners or women, sincere intention, not for food or worldly gain, only if overcome by states, and truthful ecstasy. Despite this, the dominant Hanafi position is that singing is unlawful and those who permit it are considered sinners. The passage stresses that even idolaters forbid it, indicating its universally reprehensible nature.

Key Terms
الغناء (al-ghina'): Repetition of voice with melodies in poetry combined with clapping, considered a major sin.
كبيرة (kabirah): Major sin that removes 'adalah and requires repentance.
المشايخ (al-mashayikh): Spiritual masters; the concession for singing is only for them under strict conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the conditions from Nisab al-Ihtisab, which of the following is NOT a condition for the permissibility of singing?
A) The singer must have sincere intention.
B) The gathering must include at least one woman for balance.
C) No beardless youth (amrad) should be present.
D) The listeners should not stand up unless overwhelmed.

Key Takeaway
Singing for people is a major sin that voids 'adalah and testimony; only an extremely rare concession for spiritually perfected masters under six conditions exists.

Actions That Disqualify a Witness in Hanafi Law
Commentary on actions that invalidate witness testimony.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the specific actions that disqualify a witness according to the Hanafi madhhab and explain the legal reasoning (talil) for each, including the distinction between chess and backgammon.

Core Idea
Major sins and violations of muru'a disqualify witnesses; mere chess playing without gambling does not.

In this passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya, we learn which actions render a person unacceptable as a witness. The commentary expands on each prohibition, providing the underlying rationale. You will see how the Hanafi school balances textual proofs with considerations of social dignity (muru'a) and practical necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the listed actions
The passage lists: committing acts punishable by hadd, entering the public bath without a loincloth, consuming riba, gambling with backgammon or chess, missing prayer due to these games, and frequent false oaths. List them in your notes.
These are causes of jarh (disqualification).
2
Examine the proof for each action
For each action, the author provides a reason: hadd sins are major (kabira), bath without izar is haram because it reveals the private parts, riba is a major sin, gambling leads to invalid sales which are akin to riba. Identify the common thread: all indicate fisq.
The proof is based on the general principle that fisq nullifies 'adala.
3
Analyze the distinction between chess and backgammon
Al-Hidaya states that mere chess playing is not fisq because there is room for ijtihad. Backgammon always disqualifies. Understand that chess involves skill and does not automatically lead to the same evils as backgammon.
This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
4
Evaluate the disagreement on riba vs. orphan's property
The text discusses whether eating riba requires notoriety to disqualify, while consuming orphan's property disqualifies outright. Al-Zayla'i's distinction (ownership) is rejected; the correct view is that both require evidence for the judge.
This shows the importance of judicial knowledge of fisq.
5
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Given a hypothetical witness, determine their admissibility based on known actions. For example, a known chess player who occasionally gambles: his testimony is rejected if gambling is confirmed.
Practice applying the principles to concrete cases.

As for consuming orphan's property, no one restricted it. And you know that it is necessary for it to become apparent to the judge, so there is no difference between usury and orphan's property. The conclusion is that fisq is a legal impediment to its acceptance, but the judge does not establish it except after it becomes apparent to him, so all are equal. But the implication of the aforementioned reasoning is that playing it absolutely rejects, because a person rarely escapes from invalid sales, so all of that is usury.
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya is a commentary on a ruling about the conditions for accepting testimony. It specifies actions that disqualify a witness: committing acts that incur a prescribed punishment (hadd), entering the public bath without a loincloth (izar), consuming usury (riba), gambling with backgammon or chess, and missing prayer due to these games. The commentary expands on each point. For riba, it clarifies that the prohibition is on taking the extra amount, and the term 'eating' is used metaphorically for taking. There is a discussion about singing (ghina) — though not the main focus, it notes that some scholars consider it a major sin even if done alone. For the bath without izar, the commentary cites Abu Hanifa's poetic advice and al-Karkhi's ruling on wearing only trousers in public as a lack of muru'a (dignity). For gambling, the text distinguishes between chess and backgammon: mere chess playing without gambling is not fisq because ijtihad allows it, but backgammon is always disqualifying because it is associated with invalid sales and riba. There is also a discussion comparing riba and consuming orphan's property — while both are major sins, the latter disqualifies testimony outright without requiring notoriety. The commentary by al-Zayla'i is mentioned but dismissed. The overall reasoning is that these actions indicate fisq (immorality), which prevents acceptance of testimony, but the judge must have knowledge of the sin.

Key Terms
الفسق (al-fisq): Immorality, sinfulness; committing major sins that disqualify one from being a just witness.
الكبيرة (al-kabirah): A major sin, such as those punishable by hadd, consuming riba, or gambling.
المروءة (al-muru'a): Dignity, honorable conduct; abandoning it by walking in only trousers in public is grounds for rejecting testimony.
الربا (al-riba): Usury; taking an extra amount in a loan or sale of certain items. Here it is a major sin disqualifying witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Testimony is rejected because chess is a game of chance.
B) Testimony is accepted because mere chess is not fisq.
C) Testimony is rejected if the witness plays frequently.
D) Testimony is accepted only if the witness is known to be righteous otherwise.

Key Takeaway
A witness's testimony is rejected due to fisq from major sins or gross violations of muru'a; chess is allowed unless it leads to gambling or prayer neglect.

Playing Backgammon and Chess: Effect on Witness Testimony
Analysis of when playing backgammon/chess affects witness testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on witness testimony regarding players of backgammon and chess, and explain the condition that changes the ruling.

Core Idea
Playing backgammon or chess does not disqualify a witness unless it involves gambling or causes missing prayer.

Many assume that playing games like backgammon or chess automatically makes a person unreliable as a witness. But Hanafi fiqh does not take that absolute view. Instead, the school examines whether the player engages in gambling or neglects prayers. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this conditional ruling and the evidence from other schools that support it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the legal issue
The act is playing backgammon (nard) or chess (shatranj). The legal issue is whether this act affects the player's integrity (adala) and thus the acceptance of his testimony.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The Hanafi ruling is that merely playing these games does not disqualify a witness. Disqualification occurs only if the player gambles (qimar) or habitually misses prayers due to the game.
3
Explore the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some Hanafi sources, like al-Dhakhira, hold that playing backgammon absolutely disqualifies a witness. This view is mentioned but rejected by the author.
The author calls the absolute view 'not correct' (ليس كذلك).
4
Understand the Hanafi response
The Hanafi position is supported by the permissibility of these games according to Malik, al-Shafi'i, and a narration from Abu Yusuf. Since there is room for ijtihad, the player is not automatically a fasiq.
The text also mentions that Ibn al-Shihna chose this view for focusing the mind.
5
Apply the rule
To determine if a witness's testimony is accepted, check: does he gamble at these games? Does he miss prayers due to them? If no to both, his testimony is accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سبيل القمار ولا تفوت الصلاة به لا تردُّ شهادته، وليس كذلك ، كما صرح به كثير من المتفقهين. وذكر الشارح الله أيضاً عبارة «الذخيرة» التي دلّت أنَّ اللعب بالنرد مطلقاً موجب لرد الشهادة، فالصواب أن يقول : أو يلعب من النردِ من غير شرط المقامرة، أو تفويت الصلاة. أو يقامر بالشطرنج أو تفوته الصلاة بسببه لظهور الفسق بالمقامرة، وبترك الصلاة إنما بدونهما لا يمنع العدالة ؛ لأنَّ للاجتهاد فيه مساغاً ؛ لقول مالك لله والشافعي بإباحته، وهو مروي عن أبي يوسف . كما في «المجتبى». واختارها شيخ الإسلام عبد البر ابن الشحنة الله إذا كان لإحضار الذهن ، أبو زيد الحكيم له حله. ذكره شمس الأئمة السَّرَخْسِيُّ. كذا في «البحر)، وفي «النوازل» : سئل أبو القاسم [؟] مع من ينظر إلى لاعبه من غير لعب، أيجوز قال: أخافُ أن يصير فاسقاً. كذا في «المنح" والنرد : لعبة معروفة. كما في «المصباح»، وفي «القاموس») : : إنه وضعه أَرْدَ شير بن بابك ؛ ولهذا يقال : النرد شير. انتهى. وفي «منتهى الأرب» ؛ : نرد بالفتح : بازي است معروف ، معرب ست ز شیرین بابك انرا وضع کرده لا جرم نرد شیرنیز نامندش. انتهى. وفي «البرهان القاطع»: يزوبروزن فردبازي ست معروف، از مخترعات بوزرجميهر که در برابر شطرنج ساخته و بعضي كوبند نرد قدیم ست آماد و کعبتين واشتد ودی ديكر را بوزر جهير اضافه کرده است .انتهى و شطرنج : بالكسر كجر وحل بازي ست معروف وبفارني آوند خوانند وسين لغتي ست دران دهن ماخوذ ست از شطارة یا از تسطير. كذا في «منتهى الأرب».
The path of gambling and not causing the loss of prayer does not reject his testimony, and it is not like that, as many jurists have stated. The commentator also mentioned the phrase from al-Dhakhira that indicated that playing backgammon absolutely necessitates rejection of testimony, so the correct thing is to say: or he plays backgammon without condition of gambling or missing prayer. Or he gambles at chess or misses prayer due to it, because transgression becomes apparent through gambling and through abandoning prayer. But without these, it does not prevent integrity because there is room for ijtihad in it, due to the statement of Malik [may Allah be pleased with him] and al-Shafi'i permitting it, and it is narrated from Abu Yusuf. As in al-Mujtaba. And Shaykh al-Islam Abd al-Barr ibn al-Shihna chose it for focusing the mind, Abu Zayd al-Hakim deemed it permissible. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi mentioned it. So in al-Bahr. And in al-Nawazil: Abu al-Qasim was asked about one who looks at a player without playing, is it allowed? He said: I fear he may become a transgressor. So in al-Manh. And backgammon is a well-known game. As in al-Misbah. In al-Qamus: it was invented by Ardashir ibn Babak, hence it is called nardshir. End. In al-Burhan al-Qati: it is a known game, invented by Buzurjmihr in response to chess, and some say nard is ancient and Buzurjmihr added dice. End. And chess: with kasra like kujar and hulal, a known game, in Persian called avand, and it is derived from shatara or from tastir. So in Muntaha al-Arab.

Commentary
This passage addresses the question of when playing backgammon (nard) or chess (shatranj) disqualifies a witness. The author clarifies that the mere act of playing these games does not automatically lead to rejection of testimony. Instead, the disqualifying factor is either gambling (qimar) or habitually missing prayers due to the game. If neither is present, the player retains integrity (adala) because there is legitimate scope for ijtihad on the permissibility of these games. The text mentions that Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i considered chess permissible, and Abu Yusuf also reportedly permitted it. Therefore, the Hanafi position is that playing without gambling or missing prayer does not make one a fasiq. However, the author notes that some sources like al-Dhakhira suggest absolute disqualification for backgammon, but the correct view is the conditional one. The passage also includes definitions and historical origins of the games, indicating their widespread recognition.

Key Terms
النرد (al-nard): A game known as backgammon, played with dice and pieces.
الشطرنج (al-shatranj): Chess, a two-player strategy board game.
المقامرة (al-muqamarah): Gambling, playing for stakes, which is expressly prohibited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) If he plays without gambling but misses prayer
C) If he gambles or misses prayer due to it
D) If he plays with dice

Key Takeaway
Playing backgammon or chess does not automatically disqualify a witness; disqualification depends on the presence of gambling or habitual neglect of prayer.

Actions Disqualifying a Witness: Muru'a and Sabb al-Salaf
Statement of disqualifying actions and their rationale in Hanafi law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the grounds for rejecting a witness's testimony due to lack of muru'a or manifest slander of the Salaf, and identify the condition of manifestation.

Core Idea
Testimony rejected for open shameless acts or manifest slander of the Salaf; concealment preserves admissibility.

This lesson examines two categories of behavior that disqualify a witness in Hanafi law: public shameless acts that demonstrate lack of dignity (muru'a), and open slander of the Salaf (Companions and early scholars). The text from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the reasoning and highlights an important condition: the act must be manifest. We will unpack the rulings, exceptions, and a subtle intra-Hanafi difference from Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two disqualifying categories
The passage first mentions urinating/eating on the road in a shameless manner, then manifest slander of the Salaf. Note that both are public acts indicating moral deficiency.
The text groups them under a single rule: testimony rejected.
2
Understand the rationale for public shameless acts
Such acts show lack of muru'a (dignity), leading to suspicion of dishonesty. However, minor acts (drinking water, eating beans) are excused.
Necessity (no cover) also excuses exposure for istinja.
3
Grasp the condition of manifestation for slander
Only open manifestation of sabb al-Salaf disqualifies; if the person hides such beliefs, testimony is accepted. The text emphasizes 'yuzhiru' (manifests).
This is a key nuance: private wrongdoing does not automatically disqualify.
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf accepted testimony of one who dissociates (tabarru) from the Companions but not one who slanders. The majority view treats both as disqualifying if manifested.
The tarjih (preferred view) is that manifest slander disqualifies, dissociation is less severe.
5
Apply the rules to new scenarios
Practice determining admissibility based on these criteria, including exceptions for trivial acts and necessity.
